Rulings on the Muqallid: Four Opinions
The author enumerates four opinions on the status of a muqallid's iman and sinfulness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four scholarly opinions regarding the faith and sinfulness of a person who acquires beliefs through blind imitation (taqleed), and the most sound position.

Core Idea
A muqallid capable of reflection is a believer but sinful; the incapable are excused.

Scholars debated whether blind acceptance of creedal beliefs is sufficient. This passage from Tuhfat al-Mureed presents four positions, ranging from excusing the incapable to declaring the capable sinful — and even disbelief. The author notes the celebrated scholar al-Sanusi's initial rejection of taqleed and later retraction.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read carefully the original Arabic and its literal translation to grasp the flow of opinions.
2
Identify the four opinions
List each of the four opinions mentioned, noting key words like 'asahh' (most sound) and 'qila' (it is said).
The opinions differ on two axes: belief vs disbelief, and sin vs no sin.
3
Understand the reasoning behind the most sound opinion
Reflect on why the most sound opinion distinguishes between capability and incapability of reflection (nazar).
4
Note al-Sanusi's change of stance
Observe that al-Sanusi initially rejected sufficiency of taqleed but later retracted. Consider what this teaches about scholarly humility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما من حفظ العقائد بالتقليد فقد اختلف فيه، والأصح: أنه مؤمن عاص إن قدر على النظر، وغير عاص إن لم يقدر، وقيل: مؤمن غير عاص مطلقاً، وقيل: إنه عاص مطلقاً، وقيل: إنه كافر، وجرى على القول الأخير: السنوسي في شرح الكبرى، وشنَّعَ على القول بكفاية التقليد، لكن حُكي عنه أنه رجع عنه إلى القول بكفاية التقليد.
As for the one who preserves the beliefs by blind imitation (taqleed), there is disagreement about him. The most sound (al-asahh) is that he is a believer who is disobedient if he is capable of reflection (nazar), and not disobedient if he is not capable. And it is said: a believer not disobedient absolutely, and it is said: he is disobedient absolutely, and it is said: he is a disbeliever. And the last opinion is held by al-Sanusi in his Sharh al-Kubra, and he vehemently rejected the opinion of sufficiency of taqleed, but it is narrated from him that he retracted from that to the opinion of sufficiency of taqleed.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage discusses the status of a person who acquires beliefs by taqleed (blind acceptance) without rational proof. The author presents four opinions: the most sound is that such a person is a believer but sinful if able to reason, and not sinful if unable; other opinions range from being a believer without sin to being a disbeliever. The author notes that the last opinion was held by al-Sanusi, who initially strongly refuted the sufficiency of taqleed, but it is reported that he later recanted and accepted it.

Key Terms
تقليد
نظر
إيمان
عصيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All scholars agree that taqleed is sufficient for faith.
Correction: The passage shows significant disagreement, with the most sound opinion distinguishing based on capability for reflection.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a person who learns beliefs by taqleed but is capable of intellectual reflection?
A) He is a believer and not sinful.
B) He is a believer but sinful.
C) He is a disbeliever.
D) He is excused.

Key Takeaway
The most sound position is that a muqallid capable of reflection is a believer but sinful, while one incapable is excused; this highlights the importance of rational reflection (nazar) in Islamic theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a belief about Allah simply because you were told it, without thinking it through yourself? What does this passage say about the spiritual state of such a person if they are capable of reflection?
?
If you are capable of deep thought (nazar) but have not reflected on your core beliefs, does the most sound opinion call you sinful? How does that make you feel about your current level of religious commitment?
?
The passage shows that even a scholar like al-Sanusi changed his mind. Is there a religious opinion you hold tightly that you would be unwilling to reconsider? What would it take for you to admit you might be wrong on an issue of faith?

Ruling on Taqlid in Beliefs
The author lists four opinions on the status of one who believes by taqlid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the spectrum of scholarly opinions regarding taqlid in beliefs and the conditions that affect its validity.

Core Idea
Faith through blind imitation: valid but possibly sinful, depending on ability.

The passage addresses a crucial question: can a person be considered a true believer if they have merely imitated their faith without personal reflection? Scholars disagreed sharply. This lesson presents the range of opinions and al-Sanusi's own journey on the issue.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Note the key terms: taqlid, kashf, nazar.
Pay attention to the phrase 'الأصح' (the most correct).
2
Identify the four opinions
The passage lists four views on the imitator who is capable of reflection: believer but disobedient (most correct), believer not disobedient, disobedient absolutely, or disbeliever. Locate each.
The first opinion has two conditions: ability or inability.
3
Understand the significance of ability
Notice how the most correct view distinguishes between those capable and incapable of reflection. This shows that responsibility depends on capacity.
4
Examine al-Sanusi's position
Al-Sanusi initially held the strictest view (disbeliever) but later recanted. Consider why scholars might change their opinions based on evidence.
The footnotes provide further support for the sufficiency of taqlid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقوم مقام ذلك ما لو عرف العقائد بالكشف (1). وأما من حفظ العقائد بالتقليد فقد اختلف فيه، والأصح: أنه مؤمن عاص إن قدر على النظر، وغير عاص إن لم يقدر، وقيل: مؤمن غير عاص مطلقاً، وقيل: إنه عاص مطلقاً، وقيل: إنه كافر، وجرى على القول الأخير: السنوسي في شرح الكبرى، وشنع على القول بكفاية التقليد، لكن حُكي عنه أنه رجع عنه إلى القول بكفاية التقليد (2).

(1) لأن الكشف أرقى العلوم الضرورية التي يخلقها الله تعالى في النفس، فتطمئن وتركن إليها، فضلاً عن أن تعجز عن معاندتها، قال إمامنا الغزالي في إحيائه (123): التقوى باب الذكر، والذكرُ باب الكشف، والكشف باب الفوز الأكبر؛ وهو الفوز بلقاء الله تعالى).

(2) وعبارته في شرح الكبرى (ص) (39) فالذي عليه الجمهور والمحققون من أهل السنة؛ كالشيخ الأشعري والأستاذ والقاضي وإمام الحرمين وغيرهم من الأئمة أنه لا يصح الاكتفاء به - يعني: محض التقليد - في العقائد الدينية، وهو الحق الذي لا شك فيه، قال العلامة الحامدي في تقييداته على الشرح المذكور: (الحقُّ أن التقليد يكفي في عقائد الإيمان، وأن إيمان المقلد صحيح، وقد نقل عن الأشعري أنه رجع لهذا القول، وكذلك المصنف - السنوسي - في شرح الصغرى» و«المقدمات)، وانظر المنهج السديد شرح كفاية المريد وهو «شرح الجزائرية للسنوسي (ص)، (48)، ففيه تفصيل للمسألة فقد كتبه السنوسي قبل وفاته بسنة.
And it takes the place of that if one knows the creeds by unveiling (kashf). As for one who memorizes the creeds by imitation (taqlid), there is disagreement about him. The most correct is: he is a believer, disobedient if he is able to engage in reflection, and not disobedient if he is unable. And it was said: a believer, not disobedient absolutely. And it was said: he is disobedient absolutely. And it was said: he is a disbeliever. Al-Sanusi followed the last opinion in his commentary al-Kubra, and he denounced the opinion of the sufficiency of imitation, but it is narrated that he recanted from it to the opinion of the sufficiency of imitation.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage presents the different scholarly opinions on the legal and theological status of a person who acquires beliefs by blind imitation (taqlid) rather than through rational reflection (nazar). The author begins by contrasting taqlid with kashf (unveiling), noting that knowing by kashf is superior. He then lists the views: the most correct view is that the imitator is a believer but sinful if he is capable of reflection; if incapable, he is not sinful. Other views hold him absolutely not sinful, absolutely sinful, or even a disbeliever. Al-Sanusi initially held the last view and denounced taqlid, but later recanted to the view that taqlid suffices. The footnotes elaborate: the first defines kashf as a higher knowledge granted by God, and the second provides a detailed history of the dispute, citing authorities like al-Ash'ari and al-Sanusi's own retraction.

Key Terms
تقليد
كشف
إيمان
عاص
نظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That taqlid is always invalid or always valid.
Correction: The passage shows nuanced opinions depending on the individual's ability to engage in reflection; the most correct view distinguishes between the capable (sinful) and incapable (not sinful).

Checkpoint
According to the most correct opinion mentioned, what is the ruling on a person who believes by taqlid and is capable of reflection?
A) He is a disbeliever.
B) He is a believer and not sinful.
C) He is a believer but sinful.
D) He is a believer absolutely.

Key Takeaway
The ruling on taqlid in beliefs depends on one's ability to reflect; the most correct view is that it is valid but sinful for those capable, and not sinful for those incapable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever examined the basis of your own beliefs? Are they rooted in personal reflection or in blind imitation? If you are capable of reflection, are you fulfilling your obligation to seek evidence, or are you content with taqlid?
?
If a respected scholar taught you something, but later recanted and changed his view—would you have the humility to change your own stance accordingly, as al-Sanusi did? Or would you cling to the earlier position out of pride?
?
When you teach or discuss matters of faith with others, do you encourage them to think and seek understanding, or do you expect them to simply accept what you say? How does this lesson challenge your approach?

The Meaning of Hope (Rajā') and Its Distinction from Wishful Thinking (Ṭamaʿ)
Commentary on 'Allah arjū' defines hope linguistically and technically, and includes a hadith qudsi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic concept of hope (rajā') as an active attachment of the heart accompanied by taking means, and how it differs from passive wishful thinking (ṭamaʿ).

Core Idea
True hope requires action; without it, it is blameworthy wishful thinking.

This lesson unpacks a key line from the poem where the poet says, 'I hope in Allah.' The commentator explains that this hope is exclusive to Allah and involves both heartfelt attachment and effort. He distinguishes it from mere wishful thinking by emphasizing the necessity of action. A hadith qudsi reinforces this teaching.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation.
Read the source text carefully, noting the linguistic point about the word rajā' and its technical definition.
Notice the emphasis on taking means (al-akhdh fī asbābihi).
2
Identify the key distinction.
Identify the difference between rajā' and ṭamaʿ as given in the commentary. Understand the condition for hope to be valid.
3
Reflect on the hadith qudsi.
Read the hadith qudsi and consider how it reinforces the lesson. Ask yourself: Why is hope without action blameworthy?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَاللهَ أَرْجُو فِي القَبُولِ نَافِعًا بِهَا مُرِيداً فِي النَّوابِ طَامِعَا قوله : (وَاللهَ أَرْجُو) أي: لا أرجو إلا الله ؛ لأن تقديم المعمول يفيد الحصر، ولفظ الجلالة منصوب على التعظيم والرجاء بالمد لغةً: الأمل؛ وأما بالقصر فهو الناحية، ومنه قوله تعالى: ﴿وَالْمَلَكُ عَلَى أَرْجَابِهَا ﴾ [الحَاقَّة: ١٧] جمع (رجا) بالقصر، وعرفاً : تعلق القلب بمرغوب فيه مع الأخذ في أسبابه؛ وإلا فهو طمع، وهو مذموم، فالأول كرجاء الجنة مع ترك المعاصي وفعل الطاعات ، وقد ذكر الشيخ الخطيب في التفسير حديثاً قدسيا ؛ وهو أن الله تعالى قال : (ما أقل حياءً مَنْ يطمع في جنتي بغير عمل، كيف أجودُ برحمتي على من بخل بطاعتي؟!).
And I hope in Allah for acceptance, making it beneficial, seeking, hopeful for rewards. His statement: 'And I hope in Allah' means: I hope only in Allah, because advancing the object indicates restriction. The word of majesty (Allāh) is in the accusative for honor. 'Ar-rajā' with length (al-madd) linguistically means 'hope'; with shortening (al-qaṣr) it means 'side' (the corner). From that is the verse: 'And the angels at its edges' [al-Ḥāqqah 17] – the plural of 'rajā' with qaṣr. Technically: attachment of the heart to a desired thing along with taking its means; otherwise it is wishful thinking (ṭamaʿ), which is blameworthy. The first is like hoping for paradise along with abandoning sins and performing acts of obedience. And the Shaykh al-Khaṭīb mentioned in the tafsīr a ḥadīth qudsī: that Allah said: 'How little shame is from one who hopes for My paradise without action; how can I bestow My mercy on one who is stingy with his obedience?'
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the poet's line 'wa-llāha arjū' (I hope in Allah). The commentator explains that by advancing the object, the poet restricts his hope to Allah alone. The word 'Allah' is in the accusative as a sign of reverence. Then he gives a linguistic analysis of the word 'rajā'' (hope): with a long vowel (al-madd) it means hope (amal); with a short vowel (al-qaṣr) it means a side (such as in the Quranic verse about angels on the sides of heaven). Technically, in Islamic usage, 'rajā'' is the attachment of the heart to something desirable while also taking the necessary means to achieve it. Without action, it is merely wishful thinking (ṭamaʿ), which is blameworthy. The commentator gives as an example hoping for paradise while abandoning sins and performing acts of worship. He then quotes a ḥadīth qudsī transmitted by al-Khaṭīb in his tafsīr, in which Allah criticizes those who hope for paradise without work, emphasizing that His mercy is not for those who are stingy with obedience. Thus, the passage teaches that true hope is accompanied by effort, distinguishing it from empty optimism.

Key Terms
رجاء
طمع
تعلق القلب
الأسباب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Hope is merely a passive feeling of optimism about Allah's mercy, requiring no effort.
Correction: True hope (rajā') in Islam requires taking the means (e.g., obeying commands, avoiding sins). Without action, it is blameworthy wishful thinking (ṭamaʿ).

Checkpoint
What is the difference between rajā' and ṭamaʿ according to the commentator?
A) Rajā' is desire without action, ṭamaʿ is with action
B) Rajā' is hope with action, ṭamaʿ is wishful thinking without action
C) Rajā' is for paradise, ṭamaʿ is for worldly things
D) They are synonyms

Key Takeaway
True hope in Allah is not passive; it is an active attachment of the heart accompanied by striving in obedience, without which it becomes blameworthy wishful thinking.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you hope for Paradise while your actions are filled with negligence? Read the hadith qudsi: 'How little shame is from one who hopes for My paradise without action.' How does this make you feel about your daily habits? What is one action you will commit to starting this week?
?
The commentator says true hope requires taking means. Think of a specific area in your life where you claim to hope for Allah's help but you have not taken any steps. What is holding you back from acting? Write down one concrete step you will take today.
?
The passage distinguishes between hope and wishful thinking. Which one describes your current state regarding the afterlife? Be honest. What needs to change so that your hope becomes genuine?

The True Meaning of Hope (Rajā')
Linguistic and technical definition of raja' with condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the true meaning of raja' (hope) in Islam, its linguistic and technical definitions, and the essential condition that distinguishes it from blameworthy greed (tama').

Core Idea
True hope in Allah requires action; hope without effort is mere greed.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies the term 'rajā'' as used by the author. He begins by noting that the phrase 'وَاللهَ أَرْجُو' restricts hope to Allah alone. Then he distinguishes between linguistic and technical meanings, emphasising that genuine hope must be accompanied by taking the necessary means. Without that, it becomes blameworthy 'tama'. A sacred hadith reinforces that hoping for Paradise without obedience is audacious.

Guided Path
1
Understand the linguistic nuance
The passage explains that 'raja'' with a long vowel means hope, while with a short vowel it means side. Note the Quranic example.
This is a common linguistic point in Arabic.
2
Grasp the technical definition
The technical definition adds a crucial condition: 'attachment of the heart to something desired while taking its means.' This is the core of true hope.
3
Distinguish hope from greed
Without taking means, the same attachment is called 'tama'' (greed), which is blameworthy. The example of hoping for paradise without action illustrates this.
See the hadith for reinforcement.
4
Reflect on the sacred hadith
The hadith shows how Allah criticizes those who hope for His mercy while neglecting obedience. This drives home the lesson that hope and action are inseparable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَاللهَ أَرْجُو) أي: لا أرجو إلا الله ؛ لأن تقديم المعمول يفيد الحصر، ولفظ الجلالة منصوب على التعظيم والرجاء بالمد لغةً: الأمل؛ وأما بالقصر فهو الناحية، ومنه قوله تعالى: ﴿وَالْمَلَكُ عَلَى أَرْجَابِهَا ﴾ [الحَاقَّة: ١٧] جمع (رجا) بالقصر، وعرفاً : تعلق القلب بمرغوب فيه مع الأخذ في أسبابه؛ وإلا فهو طمع، وهو مذموم، فالأول كرجاء الجنة مع ترك المعاصي وفعل الطاعات، وقد ذكر الشيخ الخطيب في التفسير حديثاً قدسيا ؛ وهو أن الله تعالى قال : (ما أقل حياءً مَنْ يطمع في جنتي بغير عمل، كيف أجودُ برحمتي على من بخل بطاعتي؟!).
His saying: 'And Allah I hope' i.e., I hope only from Allah, because advancing the object of the verb indicates restriction. The word of majesty is accusative for veneration. Linguistically, 'raja'' with elongation means hope; with shortening it means side, as in His statement: 'And the angels upon its sides' [al-Haqqa 17] – the plural of 'raja' with shortening. Technically: the heart's attachment to something desired while taking its means; otherwise it is tama' (greed), which is blameworthy. The first is like hoping for Paradise while abandoning sins and performing acts of obedience. The khatib mentioned in the tafsir a sacred hadith: Allah said: 'How little is the modesty of one who hopes for My Paradise without action? How can I grant My mercy to one who is stingy with My obedience?'
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the term 'rajā'' (hope). The author first gives the linguistic meaning: with elongation (alif) it means hope; with shortening it means side. Then the technical meaning: attachment of the heart to a desired outcome while taking the necessary means. Without taking means, it is blameworthy 'tama' (greed). The author illustrates with hoping for Paradise while abandoning sins and doing good deeds. A sacred hadith is quoted to emphasize that hope without action is foolish. This guards against the misunderstanding that hope in Allah's mercy dispenses with effort.

Key Terms
الرجاء
الطمع
تعلق القلب
حديث قدسي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that hoping for Allah's mercy or Paradise does not require any action; that mere hope is sufficient.
Correction: The passage explicitly defines true raja' as hope accompanied by taking the means (e.g., avoiding sins and doing good deeds). Hope without action is called tama' (greed) and is blameworthy. The sacred hadith condemns hoping for Paradise without obedience.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes true 'rajā'' from blameworthy 'tama''?
A) True rajā' is confident while tama' is doubtful
B) True rajā' includes taking the means, while tama' is hope without action
C) True rajā' is only for this world, tama' for the afterlife
D) True rajā' is always accompanied by fear

Key Takeaway
True hope (rajā') necessarily involves both the heart's attachment and taking the necessary means; without action it becomes blameworthy greed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What area of your life are you hoping for Allah's mercy while knowingly neglecting the means He has prescribed? Is your hope genuine, or is it greed?
?
The sacred hadith asks: 'How little is the modesty of one who hopes for My Paradise without action?' Consider one act of obedience you have been avoiding. What does this hadith say about your hope?
?
You say you hope in Allah alone—but does your daily schedule reflect that hope in your efforts, or is your hope just a feeling? Name one means you will start taking this week as part of your hope for Paradise.

The Three Degrees of Sincere Intention and the Inference to Higher Rank
Enumeration of three ranks of ikhlas and an inference from reward to higher intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three ranks of sincere intention (ikhlas) and the a fortiori inference that hoping for God Himself is more beneficial than hoping for reward.

Core Idea
Sincere intention has three ranks; the highest serves God alone.

The commentator explains that while acting for God with hope of reward is permissible, there are higher and lower degrees of sincerity. He enumerates three ranks and then draws a powerful inference: if God benefits one who hopes for reward, He will surely benefit one who hopes for God Himself. This lesson unpacks these ranks and the logic behind the inference.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from enumeration to inference.
The passage contains both classification and a logical argument.
2
Identify the three ranks
Find the descriptions of the highest, middle, and lowest degrees of sincerity. Understand what distinguishes each.
3
Understand the inference
Trace the commentator's reasoning: if God benefits the one who hopes for reward, then all the more He benefits the one who hopes for God Himself. Note how this uses the a fortiori (first principle) argument.
This shows the superiority of pure intention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي كلامه إشارة إلى أن العمل الله مع إرادة الثواب جائز وإن كان غيره أكمل فإن درجاتِ الإخلاص ثلاث عليا، ووسطى، ودنيا :
فالعليا : أن يعمل العبد الله وحده امتثالاً لأمره وقياماً بحق عبوديته. والوسطى : أن يعمل طلباً للثواب وهرباً من العقاب.
والدنيا : أن يعمل لإكرام الله له في الدنيا والسلامة من آفاتها وما عدا هذه الثلاثة فهي رياءً وإن تفاوتت أفراده، ذكره شيخ الإسلام في «شرح الرسالة القشيرية)، وقاله غيره من العلماء أيضاً .
ويُفهم من قوله: نافعاً بها مريداً في الثواب طامعاً أنه تعالى يكون نافعاً بها مريداً طامعاً في ذات الله (۲) ; لأنَّه إذا نفع بها المريد الطامع في الثواب، فبالأولى أن ينفع بها المريد الطامع في ذاتِ الله .
(۲) أي في سبيله وطاعته ومرضاته، كما هي أحد معاني الذات إذا أُضيفت، قال في «تاج العروس» (ذو) وقد تطلق الذات على الطاعة والسبيل كما قاله السبكي والكرماني، وبهما فسرا قول حبيب الذي أنشده البخاري في صحيحه :
وذلك في ذات الإله وإن يشأ يُبارك على أوصالِ شِلْوِ مُمَزَّعِ
And in his statement there is an indication that acting for God along with desiring reward is permissible, although otherwise is more perfect. For the degrees of sincerity (ikhlaṣ) are three: the highest, the middle, and the lowest. The highest: that the servant acts for God alone, in obedience to His command and fulfilling the right of His servitude. The middle: that he acts seeking reward and fleeing punishment. The lowest: that he acts for God's honoring him in this world and safety from its afflictions. And whatever is besides these three is hypocrisy, even if its instances vary. This was mentioned by Shaykh al-Islam in his commentary on al-Risāla al-Qushayriyya, and other scholars also said it. And it is understood from his statement: 'beneficial through it, intending, hoping for reward' that God, the Exalted, is beneficial through it to one who intends and hopes for God Himself (dhāt Allāh) (2); because if He benefits through it the intender hoping for reward, then all the more so He benefits through it the intender hoping for God Himself. (2) i.e., in His way, obedience, and pleasure, as is one of the meanings of 'dhāt' when used in idafa. It is stated in Tāj al-‘Arūs that 'dhāt' may be applied to obedience and way, as al-Subkī and al-Kirmānī said, and both of them interpreted the verse of Ḥabīb which al-Bukhārī narrated in his Ṣaḥīḥ: 'And that is in the essence of God (dhāt al-ilāh), and if He wills, He blesses the limbs of a torn body.'
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage explains the three levels of intention (ikhlas) in relation to the poet's words. The commentator states that acting for God while also desiring reward is permissible, but a higher level exists. He then enumerates the three ranks: highest is pure obedience without thought of reward; middle is desire for reward and fear of punishment; lowest is desire for worldly honor and safety. Anything else is hypocrisy. He then draws an inference (fahm) from the poet's words: if God benefits the one who hopes for reward, then certainly God benefits the one who hopes for God Himself (lit. 'in the essence of God'). The footnote explains that 'dhāt Allāh' here means in His way and obedience, citing lexical authorities and a poetic quotation from Bukhari.

Key Terms
الإخلاص
الرياء
ذات الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Desiring reward for good deeds is always discouraged or invalidates sincerity.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that desiring reward is permissible and still counts as sincerity, though it is not the highest rank. The highest is acting solely for God's sake.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the highest degree of sincerity (ikhlas)?
A) Acting for God while seeking reward
B) Acting solely for God in obedience without thought of reward or punishment
C) Acting for worldly honor
D) Avoiding hypocrisy

Key Takeaway
The highest sincerity is acting for God alone without ulterior motive, but hoping for reward is permissible; an even higher goal is to intend God Himself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an act of worship, which of the three degrees most often drives you? Would you be honest enough to admit if it is the lowest?
?
If tomorrow God promised you that every act of worship you do will be rewarded immediately in this world, would you continue praying and fasting? What does that reveal about your true intention?
?
The passage says 'the highest is to act for God alone.' Are you willing to examine one deed today and ask yourself: would I still do this if no one ever knew and I received no benefit in this life or the next?

Definition and Ruling of Ahl al-Fatrah
Defines ahl al-fatrah and states their salvation despite idol worship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand who ahl al-fatrah are and why they are considered saved despite never receiving a prophet's call.

Core Idea
Ahl al-fatrah are saved because the prophetic call never reached them.

In this lesson, we examine the category of people known as ahl al-fatrah—those who lived in a period without any prophet. The author explains the conditions for moral responsibility and concludes that such people are not held accountable for rejecting a religion they never knew.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic carefully and note the definition of ahl al-fatrah. Then read the translation to grasp the core ruling.
Pay attention to the key terms: ahl al-fatrah, da'wa, mukallaf.
2
Understand the condition for taklif
The passage argues that accountability depends on the call reaching. Consider the example of a person raised in isolation on a mountain, who is not obligated according to the correct opinion.
Note the difference between those who say intellect suffices and the correct opinion that the call must reach.
3
Grasp the final ruling
The passage concludes that ahl al-fatrah are saved (najoon) even if they changed laws and worshipped idols, because the specific messenger's call did not reach them.
This is the correct doctrine according to verified transmission from al-Abi.

حكم أهل الفترة ونجاة آباء النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وأمهاته. وخرج بالذي بلغته الدعوة من لم تبلغه بأن نشأ في شاهق جبل، فليس بمكلف على الأصح، خلافاً لمن قال بأنه مكلَّف لوجود العقل الكافي في وجوب المعرفة عندهم، وإن لم تبلغه الدعوة. وعلى اشتراط بلوغ الدعوة فهل يكفي بلوغ دعوة أي نبي ولو سيدنا آدم؛ لأن التوحيد ليس أمراً خاصا بهذه الأمة، أو لا بدَّ من بلوغ دعوة الرسول الذي أرسل إليه؟ والتحقيق كما نقله العلامة المَلَّوِيُّ عن الأبي في شرح مسلم، خلافاً للنووي): أنه لا بد من بلوغ دعوة الرسول الذي أرسل إليهم، فالمذهب الحقّ: أن أهل الفترة - بفتح الفاء - وهم من كانوا بين أزمنة الرسل أو في زمن الرسول الذي لم يُرسل إليهم، ناجون وإن بدلوا وغيَّروا وعبدوا الأصنام.
The ruling of ahl al-fatrah and the salvation of the Prophet's paternal and maternal ancestors, may God bless him and grant him peace. And excluded by 'the one whom the call reached' is the one whom it did not reach, such that he grew up on a remote mountain peak, for he is not obligated according to the most correct opinion, contrary to those who say he is obligated because of the existence of sufficient intellect for the necessity of knowledge in their view, even if the call did not reach him. And upon the condition of the call reaching, does the reaching of the call of any prophet, even our master Adam, suffice because tawhid is not specific to this nation? Or must the call of the messenger sent to them have reached? The verified opinion, as al-Mallawi transmitted from al-Abi in his commentary on Muslim, contrary to al-Nawawi, is that the call of the messenger sent to them must have reached. Thus, the correct doctrine is that ahl al-fatrah – with fath on the fa – who are those between the times of the messengers or in the time of a messenger not sent to them, are saved, even if they changed, altered, and worshipped idols.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage defines ahl al-fatrah (people of the interval between prophets) and gives their ruling. The author begins by explaining that the condition for moral responsibility (taklif) is that the prophetic call (da‘wa) must have reached the person. One who never received the call—e.g., raised on a remote mountain—is not obligated (mukallaf), contra some who say intellect alone suffices. Next, the author raises a further question: if the call must reach, does any prophet’s message suffice (since tawhid is universal), or must it be the specific prophet sent to that people? He states the verified view, transmitted from al-Abi, that the call of the sent messenger must reach. He then concludes that ahl al-fatrah are those between the times of prophets or in the time of a prophet not sent to them; they are saved (najoon) even if they changed the previous laws and worshipped idols. This is the correct doctrine.

الدعوة (al-da‘wa)
مكلف (mukallaf)
التوحيد (tawhid)
ناجون (najoon)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ahl al-fatrah are automatically punished for idol worship.
Correction: They are saved because the prophetic call never reached them.

Checkpoint

A) Having intellect alone
B) The prophetic call must have reached them
C) Being an adult male
D) Living in a city with scholars

Key Takeaway
People who never received any prophetic message are not accountable and are saved even if they committed shirk.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The message has reached you in abundance. Are you living as if you truly understand this privilege? What would be your state if the call had never reached you? Don't you owe a greater level of gratitude and obedience? Commit today to thank Allah for the blessing of Islam by studying a new teaching from the Qur'an or Sunnah and implementing it.
?
Ahl al-fatrah are saved even though they worshipped idols, but you have no such excuse. Where in your life are you still 'worshipping idols'—whether it be the pursuit of wealth, status, or your own desires? Repent sincerely now and resolve to purify your intention for Allah alone.
?
The passage mentions those who argue that intellect alone is sufficient for moral responsibility. Do you sometimes rely on your own reasoning over the explicit guidance of the Prophet? In what specific area of your life are you prioritizing your intellect over revelation? Are you willing to submit to the teachings of the Prophet even when they contradict your own logic?

Definition of Taqlid and the Muqallid's Faith
The author defines taqlid and explains that the muqallid's faith is accompanied by potential hesitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of taqlid and why the muqallid's faith is considered not free from hesitation (tardid).

Core Idea
Taqlid is accepting belief without evidence; such faith is inherently open to doubt.

The author begins by stating that the muqallid's faith is not free from tardid (hesitation). This might seem counterintuitive, as we often think faith is certain. However, we first need to understand what taqlid is and what is meant by 'faith' in this context. In this lesson, we explore the precise definitions at play.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the definitions and the claim.
Pay attention to the crescent moon example.
2
Identify the definition of taqlid
Note the author's specific language: 'taking the statement of another without knowing its evidence.'
Also note what 'taking' and 'statement' include.
3
Understand the example of the crescent moon
This clarifies the difference between a muqallid and a guided student.
4
Grasp why muqallid's faith is not free from tardid
The author explains that the muqallid lacks evidence, so his certainty is not fully grounded.
5
Consider the discussion of faith as certainty vs. assent
The author notes that faith is preponderantly tasdiq (assent), not mere jasm (certainty).
This distinction is important for later objection.

حكمُ إيمانِ المُقلِّدِ والخلاف فيه ]
11 - إِذْ كُلُّ مَنْ قَلَّدَ فِي التَّوْحِيدِ إِيمَانُهُ لَمْ يَخْلُ مِنْ تَرْدِيدِ
قوله : (إِذْ كُلُّ مَنْ قَلَّدَ ... إلخ)، هذا تعليل (۱) لوجوب المعرفة السابقة، فكأنه قال : وإنما وجب على المكلَّف معرفةُ ما ذُكر لأن كلَّ من قلد ... إلى آخره، فـ (إذ) للتعليل.
(۱) قوله : (هذا التعليل ... إلى آخره) أي باعتبار ما تضمنه من جواب الدليل؛ لأن وجوب المعرفة يتضمن وجوب أمور ثلاثة : الجزم، وكونه مطابقاً للواقع، وكونه ناشئاً عن الدليل، وهذه علة للثالث، وهو كونه ناشئاً عن الدليل. انتهى أجهوري.
والتقليد : هو الأخذُ بقول الغير من غير أن يُعرف دليله.
والمراد بالأخذ الاعتقاد؛ أي: اعتقاد مضمون قول الغير.
والمراد بالقول : ما يشمل الفعل والتقرير أيضاً .
وخرج بقولنا (من غير أن يعرف دليله : التلامذة بعد أن يرشدَهُمُ الأشياخُ للأدلَّة، فهم عارفون لا مقلّدون، وضربَ لهم الشيخ السنوسي مثلاً للفرق بينهم وبين المقلدين بجماعة نظروا للهلال، فسبقَ بعضُهم لرؤيته فأخبرهم به، فإن صدقوه من غير معاينة، كانوا مقلدين وإن أرشدهم بالعلامة حتى عاينوه لم يكونوا مقلدين(۳) وقوله : (فِي التَّوْحِيدِ) أي : في علم العقائد ولو تعلَّقَتْ بالرسل، فليس المراد بالتوحيد إثبات الوحدة بخصوصه .
(۳) قاله في شرح الجزائرية» (ص ٥٢ ) .
وقوله : (إِيمَانُهُ لَمْ يَخْلُ مِنْ تَرْدِيدِ) هذه الجملةُ خبرٌ عن المبتدأ الذي هو كل من قلد ... ) إلى آخره والمراد بإيمانه: جزمُهُ بأحكام التوحيد من غير دليل، وليس المراد به المعرفة؛ إذ لا معرفةً عند المقلّد كذا يفيده كلام الشارح، ولعله مبني على أن الإيمان هو المعرفة، وهو ضعيف، والراجح : أنه التصديق، وهو غير الجزم؛ لأنَّ مرجعَه الكلام النفساني، وهو قول النفس : آمنتُ وصدَّقتُ، فالأولى أن المراد بإيمان المقلد : تصديقه التابع للجزم، لا نفس الجزم.
والمراد من الترديد التردُّدُ والتحير ؛ من قولك : تردَّدَ زيد ؛ أي: تحير . واستُشكل بأن العبارة تقتضي أن الجزم يجامعُ التردُّد ! مع أنه متى كان جازماً، لا يكون متردداً أصلا (۱) ، فكيف يقولُ : إيمانه لم يخل من ترديد؟!
(۱) في (ب): (لم يكن معه تردد أصلاً).
وأجيب عن ذلك بأن كلامه على حذف مضاف والتقدير : لم يخل عن قبول ترديد، أو المعنى : أنه مصحوب بالترديد بالقوَّةِ، لا بالفعل (۳) ، ولا يرد أن العارف لا يخلو أيضاً عن قبول الترديد أو لم يخلُ عن الترديد بالقوَّة؛ لجواز أن تطمس عين معرفته والعياذ بالله تعالى؛ لأن المراد بالقبول والقوَّة القريبان من الفعل عادةً، ولا يضرُّ غيرهما . ويمكن أن يحمل الترديد على اختلافِ العلماء فيه فما يأتي كالتفسير لهذا المجمل، فهو من ذكر المفصَّل بعد المجمل .
(۳) وهذا التفسير يدفع الإشكال السابق، ولكنَّ ابنَ الناظم وكثيراً من الشراح والمحسين ساروا على القول الأول ، وانظر حاشية الأميره ( ص ٤٣ ) .
كذا قاله العلامة الملوي كما في حاشية الأمير على عبد السلام (ص) (٤٣)، وقد صوّر سيدنا علي رضي الله عنه - كما في الإحياء» (۲/۱) - هذا المتردد بالقوة بقوله : (أو منقاداً لأهل الحق لكن ينزرع الشكُ في قلبه بأول عارض من شبهة لا بصيرة له، وقوله : (بأول عارض من شبهة) يخرج العارف وإن كان يتصور منه التشكيك بالقوة، لكنه بعيد .
The ruling on the faith of the muqallid and the disagreement concerning it.
11 - Since everyone who practices taqlid in tawhid, his faith is not free from tardid (hesitation).
His statement: (Since everyone who practices taqlid ... etc.), this is a reason (1) for the obligation of the prior knowledge, as if he said: And it is obligatory for the responsible person to know what was mentioned because everyone who practices taqlid ... etc., so (إذ) is for reasoning.
(1) His statement: (This reasoning ... etc.) means by way of what it contains of the answer to the proof; because the obligation of knowledge entails the obligation of three matters: certainty, its conformity to reality, and its arising from the proof, and this is a reason for the third, which is its arising from the proof. End of al-Ujhuri.
And taqlid is: taking the statement of another without knowing its evidence.
And what is meant by taking is belief; i.e., belief in the content of the statement of another.
And what is meant by statement includes action and tacit approval as well.
And through our statement (without knowing its evidence) are excluded the students after their teachers guide them to the evidences, for they are knowers, not muqallids, and Shaykh al-Sanusi gave them an example for the difference between them and the muqallids: a group looked at the crescent moon, and one of them preceded them in seeing it and informed them of it, and if they believed him without seeing it themselves, they would be muqallids, but if he guided them by a sign until they saw it themselves, they would not be muqallids (3). And His statement: (in tawhid) means: in the science of beliefs, even if they are related to the messengers, so the intended meaning of tawhid is not specifically affirming unity.
(3) He said it in Sharh al-Jaza'iriyya (p. 52).
And His statement: (his faith is not free from tardid) this sentence is the predicate of the subject which is 'everyone who practices taqlid ...' etc., and what is meant by his faith is his certainty (jasm) of the rulings of tawhid without evidence, and it is not intended by it knowledge, for there is no knowledge with the muqallid, as the commentator's speech indicates. And perhaps it is based on the view that faith is knowledge, which is weak. And the preponderant view is that it is assent (tasdiq), which is not certainty, because its referent is inner speech (kalam nafsi), which is the utterance of the soul: 'I believe and I affirm.' So it is more appropriate that what is meant by the faith of the muqallid is his assent that follows certainty, not certainty itself.
And what is meant by tardid is hesitation and confusion; from your saying: 'Zayd hesitated,' i.e., he was confused. And it is objected that the expression implies that certainty coexists with hesitation! Whereas when one is certain, he is not hesitant at all (1), so how can he say: his faith is not free from tardid?!
(1) In (b): (there was no hesitation with him at all).
And it is answered that his speech involves an omitted genitive, and the estimation is: it is not free from the potential for tardid, or the meaning is: it is accompanied by tardid in potency (bil-quwwa), not in act (bil-fi'l) (3). And it is not objected that the knower (al-'arif) is also not free from the potential for tardid or from tardid in potency, because it is possible that the eye of his knowledge be blinded — God forbid — because what is meant by potential and potency that are close to act habitually, and other than that does not harm. And one can interpret tardid as the disagreement of the scholars concerning it, and what follows is an explanation of this general statement, so it is a mention of the detailed after the general.
(3) And this interpretation repels the previous objection, but Ibn al-Nazim and many of the commentators and instructors proceeded on the first view; see Hashiyat al-Amir (p. 43).
Thus said the scholar al-Milawi, as in Hashiyat al-Amir 'ala Abd al-Salam (p. 43), and our master 'Ali (may God be pleased with him) — as in al-Ihya' (2/1) — depicted this hesitant in potency with his saying: 'Or one who is submissive to the people of truth but doubt is planted in his heart at the first occurrence of a specious argument that he has no insight for.' And his saying: 'at the first occurrence of a specious argument' excludes the knower, even if it is conceivable that he could be subject to doubt in potency, but it is remote.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a definition and argument. It begins by citing line 11 of the poem, which states that anyone who practices taqlid in tawhid has a faith not free from tardid (hesitation). The author then provides a causal explanation linking this to the obligation of knowledge. He defines taqlid as accepting another's statement without evidence, clarifying that 'taking' means belief, and 'statement' includes actions and approvals. He distinguishes true students guided to evidences from muqallids using the crescent moon example. Next, he explains 'faith' here means certainty (jasm) without evidence, but preponderantly faith is assent (tasdiq) in inner speech. He defines tardid as hesitation, then raises an objection: how can certainty coexist with hesitation? He answers with two interpretations: either the phrase implies potential for hesitation, or it is a dispositional possibility rather than actual hesitation. He also notes that the knower (arif) is not immune to potential doubt but it is remote. Finally, he suggests tardid may refer to the scholarly disagreement that follows.

Key Terms
taqlid
jasm
tardid
tasdiq
kalam nafsi

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the muqallid's faith is fully valid and free from doubt.
Correction: The author shows that because the muqallid lacks evidence, his faith is not free from potential hesitation (tardid).

Checkpoint
What is the definition of taqlid according to the author?
A) Accepting another's statement without knowing its evidence
B) Following a scholar in all matters
C) Belief based on evidence
D) Assent to the truth without question

Key Takeaway
Taqlid is belief without evidence, and such faith is inherently open to potential hesitation (tardid).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you accepted the fundamental beliefs of your faith, did you do so because you were convinced by evidence or because you trusted someone? Is there a hidden hesitation lurking beneath that acceptance?
?
Have you ever experienced tardid – a moment when a core belief felt doubtful? Did you run from that feeling or seek evidence to resolve it? What does your response say about your relationship with certainty?
?
If your faith relies on taqlid, what would happen if you were challenged by a sincere skeptic? Would your uncertainty surface, or do you have reasons ready? Are you willing to examine those reasons now?

Objection and Answer: How Can Certainty Coexist with Hesitation?
The author presents the objection and two answers regarding the muqallid's hesitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection raised against the claim that the muqallid's faith is not free from hesitation, and the two main answers given.

Core Idea
Certainty and hesitation cannot coexist; the muqallid's hesitation is potential, not actual.

In the previous lesson, we learned that the muqallid's faith is not free from tardid. But this raises a logical problem: if one has certainty (jasm), how can there be any hesitation? In this lesson, we examine the objection and the scholarly answers that resolve this tension.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The passage states that certainty and hesitation cannot coexist.
The objection is that the expression seems contradictory.
2
First answer: potential for tardid
The muqallid's faith is not free from the possibility of hesitation, even if not actual.
The term 'bil-quwwa' (in potency) is used.
3
Second answer: tardid as scholarly disagreement
The word 'tardid' may refer to the different opinions among scholars about the muqallid's faith.
What follows is a detailed explanation.
4
Note about the knower (al-'arif)
Even a knower can have potential doubt, but it is remote.
The example of Imam Ali illustrates this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حكمُ إيمانِ المُقلِّدِ والخلاف فيه ]
11 - إِذْ كُلُّ مَنْ قَلَّدَ فِي التَّوْحِيدِ إِيمَانُهُ لَمْ يَخْلُ مِنْ تَرْدِيدِ
قوله : (إِذْ كُلُّ مَنْ قَلَّدَ ... إلخ)، هذا تعليل (۱) لوجوب المعرفة السابقة، فكأنه قال : وإنما وجب على المكلَّف معرفةُ ما ذُكر لأن كلَّ من قلد ... إلى آخره، فـ (إذ) للتعليل.
(۱) قوله : (هذا التعليل ... إلى آخره) أي باعتبار ما تضمنه من جواب الدليل؛ لأن وجوب المعرفة يتضمن وجوب أمور ثلاثة : الجزم، وكونه مطابقاً للواقع، وكونه ناشئاً عن الدليل، وهذه علة للثالث، وهو كونه ناشئاً عن الدليل. انتهى أجهوري.
والتقليد : هو الأخذُ بقول الغير من غير أن يُعرف دليله.
والمراد بالأخذ الاعتقاد؛ أي: اعتقاد مضمون قول الغير.
والمراد بالقول : ما يشمل الفعل والتقرير أيضاً .
وخرج بقولنا (من غير أن يعرف دليله : التلامذة بعد أن يرشدَهُمُ الأشياخُ للأدلَّة، فهم عارفون لا مقلّدون، وضربَ لهم الشيخ السنوسي مثلاً للفرق بينهم وبين المقلدين بجماعة نظروا للهلال، فسبقَ بعضُهم لرؤيته فأخبرهم به، فإن صدقوه من غير معاينة، كانوا مقلدين وإن أرشدهم بالعلامة حتى عاينوه لم يكونوا مقلدين(۳) وقوله : (فِي التَّوْحِيدِ) أي : في علم العقائد ولو تعلَّقَتْ بالرسل، فليس المراد بالتوحيد إثبات الوحدة بخصوصه .
(۳) قاله في شرح الجزائرية» (ص ٥٢ ) .
وقوله : (إِيمَانُهُ لَمْ يَخْلُ مِنْ تَرْدِيدِ) هذه الجملةُ خبرٌ عن المبتدأ الذي هو كل من قلد ... ) إلى آخره والمراد بإيمانه: جزمُهُ بأحكام التوحيد من غير دليل، وليس المراد به المعرفة؛ إذ لا معرفةً عند المقلّد كذا يفيده كلام الشارح، ولعله مبني على أن الإيمان هو المعرفة، وهو ضعيف، والراجح : أنه التصديق، وهو غير الجزم؛ لأنَّ مرجعَه الكلام النفساني، وهو قول النفس : آمنتُ وصدَّقتُ، فالأولى أن المراد بإيمان المقلد : تصديقه التابع للجزم، لا نفس الجزم.
والمراد من الترديد التردُّدُ والتحير ؛ من قولك : تردَّدَ زيد ؛ أي: تحير . واستُشكل بأن العبارة تقتضي أن الجزم يجامعُ التردُّد ! مع أنه متى كان جازماً، لا يكون متردداً أصلا (۱) ، فكيف يقولُ : إيمانه لم يخل من ترديد؟!
(۱) في (ب): (لم يكن معه تردد أصلاً).
وأجيب عن ذلك بأن كلامه على حذف مضاف والتقدير : لم يخل عن قبول ترديد، أو المعنى : أنه مصحوب بالترديد بالقوَّةِ، لا بالفعل (۳) ، ولا يرد أن العارف لا يخلو أيضاً عن قبول الترديد أو لم يخلُ عن الترديد بالقوَّة؛ لجواز أن تطمس عين معرفته والعياذ بالله تعالى؛ لأن المراد بالقبول والقوَّة القريبان من الفعل عادةً، ولا يضرُّ غيرهما . ويمكن أن يحمل الترديد على اختلافِ العلماء فيه فما يأتي كالتفسير لهذا المجمل، فهو من ذكر المفصَّل بعد المجمل .
(۳) وهذا التفسير يدفع الإشكال السابق، ولكنَّ ابنَ الناظم وكثيراً من الشراح والمحسين ساروا على القول الأول ، وانظر حاشية الأميره ( ص ٤٣ ) .
كذا قاله العلامة الملوي كما في حاشية الأمير على عبد السلام (ص) (٤٣)، وقد صوّر سيدنا علي رضي الله عنه - كما في الإحياء» (۲/۱) - هذا المتردد بالقوة بقوله : (أو منقاداً لأهل الحق لكن ينزرع الشكُ في قلبه بأول عارض من شبهة لا بصيرة له، وقوله : (بأول عارض من شبهة) يخرج العارف وإن كان يتصور منه التشكيك بالقوة، لكنه بعيد .
The ruling on the faith of the muqallid and the disagreement concerning it.
11 - Since everyone who practices taqlid in tawhid, his faith is not free from tardid (hesitation).
His statement: (Since everyone who practices taqlid ... etc.), this is a reason (1) for the obligation of the prior knowledge, as if he said: And it is obligatory for the responsible person to know what was mentioned because everyone who practices taqlid ... etc., so (إذ) is for reasoning.
(1) His statement: (This reasoning ... etc.) means by way of what it contains of the answer to the proof; because the obligation of knowledge entails the obligation of three matters: certainty, its conformity to reality, and its arising from the proof, and this is a reason for the third, which is its arising from the proof. End of al-Ujhuri.
And taqlid is: taking the statement of another without knowing its evidence.
And what is meant by taking is belief; i.e., belief in the content of the statement of another.
And what is meant by statement includes action and tacit approval as well.
And through our statement (without knowing its evidence) are excluded the students after their teachers guide them to the evidences, for they are knowers, not muqallids, and Shaykh al-Sanusi gave them an example for the difference between them and the muqallids: a group looked at the crescent moon, and one of them preceded them in seeing it and informed them of it, and if they believed him without seeing it themselves, they would be muqallids, but if he guided them by a sign until they saw it themselves, they would not be muqallids (3). And His statement: (in tawhid) means: in the science of beliefs, even if they are related to the messengers, so the intended meaning of tawhid is not specifically affirming unity.
(3) He said it in Sharh al-Jaza'iriyya (p. 52).
And His statement: (his faith is not free from tardid) this sentence is the predicate of the subject which is 'everyone who practices taqlid ...' etc., and what is meant by his faith is his certainty (jasm) of the rulings of tawhid without evidence, and it is not intended by it knowledge, for there is no knowledge with the muqallid, as the commentator's speech indicates. And perhaps it is based on the view that faith is knowledge, which is weak. And the preponderant view is that it is assent (tasdiq), which is not certainty, because its referent is inner speech (kalam nafsi), which is the utterance of the soul: 'I believe and I affirm.' So it is more appropriate that what is meant by the faith of the muqallid is his assent that follows certainty, not certainty itself.
And what is meant by tardid is hesitation and confusion; from your saying: 'Zayd hesitated,' i.e., he was confused. And it is objected that the expression implies that certainty coexists with hesitation! Whereas when one is certain, he is not hesitant at all (1), so how can he say: his faith is not free from tardid?!
(1) In (b): (there was no hesitation with him at all).
And it is answered that his speech involves an omitted genitive, and the estimation is: it is not free from the potential for tardid, or the meaning is: it is accompanied by tardid in potency (bil-quwwa), not in act (bil-fi'l) (3). And it is not objected that the knower (al-'arif) is also not free from the potential for tardid or from tardid in potency, because it is possible that the eye of his knowledge be blinded — God forbid — because what is meant by potential and potency that are close to act habitually, and other than that does not harm. And one can interpret tardid as the disagreement of the scholars concerning it, and what follows is an explanation of this general statement, so it is a mention of the detailed after the general.
(3) And this interpretation repels the previous objection, but Ibn al-Nazim and many of the commentators and instructors proceeded on the first view; see Hashiyat al-Amir (p. 43).
Thus said the scholar al-Milawi, as in Hashiyat al-Amir 'ala Abd al-Salam (p. 43), and our master 'Ali (may God be pleased with him) — as in al-Ihya' (2/1) — depicted this hesitant in potency with his saying: 'Or one who is submissive to the people of truth but doubt is planted in his heart at the first occurrence of a specious argument that he has no insight for.' And his saying: 'at the first occurrence of a specious argument' excludes the knower, even if it is conceivable that he could be subject to doubt in potency, but it is remote.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a definition and argument. It begins by citing line 11 of the poem, which states that anyone who practices taqlid in tawhid has a faith not free from tardid (hesitation). The author then provides a causal explanation linking this to the obligation of knowledge. He defines taqlid as accepting another's statement without evidence, clarifying that 'taking' means belief, and 'statement' includes actions and approvals. He distinguishes true students guided to evidences from muqallids using the crescent moon example. Next, he explains 'faith' here means certainty (jasm) without evidence, but preponderantly faith is assent (tasdiq) in inner speech. He defines tardid as hesitation, then raises an objection: how can certainty coexist with hesitation? He answers with two interpretations: either the phrase implies potential for hesitation, or it is a dispositional possibility rather than actual hesitation. He also notes that the knower (arif) is not immune to potential doubt but it is remote. Finally, he suggests tardid may refer to the scholarly disagreement that follows.

Key Terms
tardid
jasm
quwwa
fi'l

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the muqallid has no doubt at all because he is certain.
Correction: The author explains that the hesitation is potential, not actual, so certainty and potential doubt can coexist.

Checkpoint
What does 'bil-quwwa' mean in the context of the first answer?
A) In actuality
B) In potency
C) In knowledge
D) In practice

Key Takeaway
The muqallid's faith is not without potential doubt, even if not actual.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt a flicker of doubt about your faith, even while being certain? How do you reconcile that with the idea that your faith is supposed to be absolute?
?
If your certainty is based on the authority of scholars (taqlid), do you ever worry that your conviction might be fragile? What would it take for that potential doubt to become actual?
?
Imagine standing before Allah and being asked about the basis of your faith. Could you say it was built on certainty without any shadow of hesitation? If not, what steps will you take to strengthen that certainty today?

Disagreement on the Faith of the Muqallid
The author explains that scholars reported a disagreement on the muqallid's faith due to hesitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that scholars disagreed on the validity of the faith of a muqallid due to his hesitation, and the meaning of the term 'khulf' in this context.

Core Idea
The muqallid's hesitation causes a scholarly dispute about his faith.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies a line from the poem that mentions a disagreement (khulfa) among scholars. This disagreement arises from the uncertain nature of the muqallid's belief. Let's explore what the commentator says about the cause and meaning of this disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic line and the commentary. Notice the key terms.
2
Understand the cause of disagreement
The commentator says the disagreement is because of the muqallid's confusion and hesitation.
The letter 'fa' is causative.
3
Clarify the meaning of 'al-khulfa'
The term means disagreement (khilaf), not breaking a promise.
The commentary explicitly distinguishes it from the common usage.
4
Reflect on the significance
This dispute shows that even scholars differed on a fundamental issue of faith.

Source Text

١٢ - فَفِيهِ بَعْضُ القَوْمِ يَحْكِي الخُلْفَا وَبَعْضُهُمْ حَقَّقَ فِيهِ الكَشْفَا
وقوله : (فَفِيهِ بَعْضُ القَوْمِ يَحْكِي (الخُلْفَا) أي : فبسبب تحيره وتردده اختلف العلماء في إيمانه صحة وعدماً، فالفاء سببية (4) ، والضمير لإيمان المقلد من حيث الصحة وعدمُها، والخلف - بضم الخاء وسكون اللام - بمعنى الخلاف، لا بمعنى خلف الوعد وإن تعورف فيه.
(4) أو تفسيرية على القول الثاني .
12 - So concerning it, some of the people relate the disagreement (al-khulfa), and some of them have verified the disclosure (al-kashfa) concerning it.
And His statement: (So concerning it, some of the people relate (al-khulfa)) means: because of his confusion and hesitation, the scholars disagreed on his faith as to its validity or invalidity, so the fa' is causative (4), and the pronoun refers to the faith of the muqallid in terms of its validity or lack thereof, and al-khulf — with damm on the kha' and sukun on the lam — means disagreement, not breaking a promise, even if it is commonly used for that.
(4) Or it is explanatory (tafsiriyya) according to the second view.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage explains line 12 of the poem. The author clarifies that because of the hesitation (tardid) inherent in the muqallid's faith, scholars disagreed on whether such faith is valid or invalid. The word 'al-khulfa' here means disagreement (khilaf), not breaking a promise. The letter 'fa' is interpreted as causative, indicating the disagreement results from hesitation. A footnote offers an alternative: the 'fa' could be explanatory. The pronoun in 'fihi' refers to the muqallid's faith. Thus, the passage introduces the scholarly dispute that arises from the uncertain nature of the muqallid's belief.

khilaf

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The disagreement is only about a minor point.
Correction: It actually concerns the fundamental validity of the muqallid's faith.

Key Takeaway

Definition of the Muqallid's Faith and the Nature of Imān
The author clarifies the definition of iman and the nature of the muqallid's faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct definition of faith (iman) as assent (tasdiq) and how the faith of a blind follower (muqallid) is properly understood as assent dependent on certainty, not certainty itself.

Core Idea
Faith is not knowledge but inner assent; the muqallid's faith is that assent anchored in certainty.

In this passage, the author addresses a critical question: What is the nature of faith, and does the person who follows authority without independent reasoning (muqallid) truly have faith? He engages with a weak view that faith is mere knowledge, then establishes the stronger position that faith is assent, specifically inner speech of the soul. He then clarifies the muqallid's faith: it is the assent that arises from certainty, not certainty itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the flow: a rejected view, then the preferred view, then application to the muqallid.
Pay attention to the terms 'ma'rifa', 'tasdiq', and 'jazm'.
2
Identify the two definitions of iman
Find where the author mentions 'ma'rifa' (knowledge) and 'tasdiq' (assent). Understand why ma'rifa is considered weak.
The author calls the view that iman is knowledge 'weak' (da'if).
3
Understand 'kalam nafsani'
The passage says tasdiq is inner speech (kalam nafsani) – the soul's saying 'I believe and I affirm'. This is a key Ash'ari concept distinguishing inner conviction from outward utterance.
Kalam nafsani is the speech of the soul, not articulated words.
4
Apply to the muqallid
Distinguish between 'jazm' (certainty) and the 'tasdiq dependent on jazm'. The muqallid's faith is the latter: he assents because he has certainty, but his faith is the assent itself.
The muqallid's faith is valid because he assents, even if his certainty is derived from authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المراد به المعرفة؛ إذ لا معرفةً عند المقلّد كذا يفيده كلام الشارح، ولعله مبني على أن الإيمان هو المعرفة، وهو ضعيف، والراجح : أنه التصديق، وهو غير الجزم؛ لأنَّ مرجعَه الكلام النفساني، وهو قول النفس : آمنتُ وصدَّقتُ، فالأولى أن المراد بإيمان المقلد : تصديقه التابع للجزم، لا نفس الجزم.
What is meant by it is knowledge; for there is no knowledge with the blind follower (muqallid). Thus the commentator's speech indicates, and perhaps it is based on the view that faith (iman) is knowledge, which is weak. The preponderant view is that it is assent (tasdiq), and it is not certainty (jazm) because its reference is inner speech (kalam nafsani), which is the soul's saying: 'I believe and I affirm.' So the better view is that what is meant by the faith of the muqallid is his assent dependent on certainty, not certainty itself.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the nature of faith (iman) for the blind follower (muqallid). The author first references a view that faith is knowledge (ma'rifa), but he rejects this as weak. He then affirms the preponderant view that faith is assent (tasdiq), which is distinct from certainty (jazm). Assent is inner speech (kalam nafsani)—the soul's utterance of 'I believe and I affirm.' From this, the author concludes that the muqallid's faith is his assent that is dependent on certainty (i.e., he assents because he has certainty, but the assent itself is not the same as the certainty). This distinction preserves the proper definition of faith while acknowledging the muqallid's cognitive state.

Key Terms
إيمان (iman)
تصديق (tasdiq)
جزم (jazm)
معرفة (ma'rifa)
مقلد (muqallid)
كلام نفساني (kalam nafsani)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The muqallid's faith is certainty (jazm) itself.
Correction: The author explains that the muqallid's faith is his assent (tasdiq) which follows from certainty, not the certainty itself.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the correct understanding of the muqallid's faith?
A) It is knowledge (ma'rifa).
B) It is unwavering certainty (jazm).
C) It is assent (tasdiq) dependent on certainty.
D) It is blind acceptance without any certainty.

Key Takeaway
The muqallid's faith is valid because it consists of assent (inner speech) that depends on certainty, not the certainty itself.

?
The author distinguishes between the soul's inner affirmation and mere certainty. Have you ever mistaken intellectual agreement for true iman? In what area of your life might your certainty be genuine but your assent lacking? What will you do about it?

Resolving the Tension Between Certainty and Potential Doubt in the Muqallid's Faith
The author resolves an objection about certainty and hesitation in the muqallid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author reconciles the muqallid's certainty with the possibility of doubt by distinguishing between actual and potential hesitation.

Core Idea
Certain faith can coexist with potential doubt.

This passage addresses a critical objection: if faith requires certainty, how can the muqallid's faith be considered certain if he is susceptible to doubt? The author provides nuanced answers, showing that potential hesitation does not undermine actual certainty. We'll explore the logical moves that preserve the integrity of the muqallid's faith.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read how the objection states that certainty and hesitation are mutually exclusive. Why would this be a problem for the muqallid's faith?
The objection is based on the apparent contradiction in the earlier statement.
2
First answer: elliptical expression
The author explains that the phrase 'not free from hesitation' actually means 'not free from acceptation of hesitation' – a susceptibility, not actual doubt.
This is a common interpretive move in kalam.
3
Second answer: potential vs. actual
Another interpretation is that the hesitation is potential (bi al-quwwa), not actual (bi al-fi'l). The muqallid has no actual doubt but is capable of being presented with doubts.
This preserves the certainty of faith.
4
Preemptive objection about the gnostic
The author anticipates a counter: the gnostic (arif) also has potential hesitation. He clarifies that the 'potential' here means close to actuality under normal circumstances.
This prevents an infinite regress of objections.
5
Third interpretation: scholarly disagreement
Finally, the hesitation may refer to the differing opinions of scholars regarding the muqallid's faith, not any doubt in the muqallid himself.
This ties the passage to later discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واستُشكل بأن العبارة تقتضي أن الجزم يجامعُ التردُّد ! مع أنه متى كان جازماً، لا يكون متردداً أصلا (۱) ، فكيف يقولُ : إيمانه لم يخل من ترديد؟! وأجيب عن ذلك بأن كلامه على حذف مضاف والتقدير : لم يخل عن قبول ترديد، أو المعنى : أنه مصحوب بالترديد بالقوَّةِ، لا بالفعل (۳) ، ولا يرد أن العارف لا يخلو أيضاً عن قبول الترديد أو لم يخلُ عن الترديد بالقوَّة؛ لجواز أن تطمس عين معرفته والعياذ بالله تعالى؛ لأن المراد بالقبول والقوَّة القريبان من الفعل عادةً، ولا يضرُّ غيرهما . ويمكن أن يحمل الترديد على اختلافِ العلماء فيه فما يأتي كالتفسير لهذا المجمل، فهو من ذكر المفصَّل بعد المجمل .
It was objected that the expression implies that certainty (jazm) coexists with hesitation (taraddud), whereas whenever one is certain, he is not hesitant at all (1). So how can he say: 'His faith is not free from hesitation'? And it was answered that his statement involves an omitted genitive construct, and the estimation is: he is not free from acceptation of hesitation, or the meaning is that it is accompanied by hesitation potentially (bi al-quwwa), not actually (bi al-fi'l) (3). And it is not refuted that the gnostic (arif) also is not free from acceptation of hesitation or is not free from hesitation potentially, because it is possible that the eye of his knowledge might be obliterated, God forbid. But what is meant by 'acceptation' and 'potential' are those close to actuality usually, and others do not harm. And it is possible to interpret hesitation as the disagreement of scholars about it, and what follows serves as an explanation of this summary; thus it is from the mentioning of the detailed after the summary.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objection argues that if a person has certainty (jazm), he cannot simultaneously have hesitation (taraddud), yet the author's earlier statement seemed to allow both in the muqallid. The answer provides two interpretations: first, that the muqallid's faith is not free from 'acceptation of hesitation' (i.e., susceptibility to doubt), or second, that the hesitation is potential (bi al-quwwa) rather than actual (bi al-fi'l). The author then preempts a further objection about gnostics also having potential hesitation, clarifying that what matters is potential close to actuality. Finally, a third interpretation is offered: the hesitation refers to the disagreement among scholars regarding the muqallid's faith, and the subsequent discussion will detail that summarized point.

Key Terms
جزم (jazm)
تردد (taraddud)
قبول الترديد (qabul al-taraddud)
بالقوة (bi al-quwwa)
بالفعل (bi al-fi'l)
عارف (arif)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The muqallid's faith contains actual hesitation or doubt.
Correction: The author clarifies that any hesitation is only potential (bi al-quwwa) or refers to scholarly disagreement, not actual doubt in the believer.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the objection that certainty and hesitation cannot coexist in the muqallid?
A) He denies that the muqallid has certainty.
B) He explains that hesitation is only potential or refers to scholarly disagreement.
C) He argues that certainty can coexist with actual hesitation.
D) He says the muqallid is not truly a believer.

Key Takeaway
The muqallid's faith is certain because it is free from actual hesitation, but it may involve potential doubt or scholarly disagreement regarding its status.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you have areas of your faith where you experience theoretical doubts or questions? How does this lesson's distinction between potential and actual doubt change the way you view those uncertainties? Will you now see them as natural susceptibilities rather than signs of weak iman?
?
The muqallid's faith is certain despite potential hesitation. In your own life, are there convictions you hold firmly even though you could imagine counterarguments? What does that tell you about the nature of your certainty?
?
If you were to face a doubt that threatened your faith, would you be able to distinguish between a passing whisper (potential) and a settled uncertainty (actual)? How can you strengthen your faith to ensure that potential doubts do not become actual, using the principles from this lesson?

Six Scholarly Opinions on Taqlid in Faith
The author enumerates six scholarly opinions on whether blind acceptance suffices for valid faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the full spectrum of scholarly opinions on whether blind acceptance (taqlid) suffices for valid faith, including conditions related to the capacity to reason and the type of evidence followed.

Core Idea
Scholarly debate on taqlid ranges from requiring personal reasoning to accepting blind faith under certain conditions.

This passage presents a concise summary of the major scholarly positions on the validity of taqlid in matters of faith. By examining these six opinions, you will see that Islamic theological tradition is not monolithically opposed to taqlid but has nuanced views based on the capacity to reason and the nature of the evidence followed. The author Sanusi catalogs these views to frame the discussion on the necessity of intellectual investigation (nazar) for valid iman.

Guided Path
1
Read the full enumeration
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of all six opinions presented in order.
Notice the author uses 'الأول', 'الثاني', etc. to structure the list.
2

For each opinion, determine: (a) Is taqlid considered valid? (b) Is it sinful? (c) On what conditions does validity depend?
Key conditions include the presence of capacity for reasoning (ahliyyat al-nazar) and the type of evidence followed (definitive vs. non-definitive).
3
Compare the role of reasoning
Notice how the opinions differ on whether having the capacity to reason matters: some make sin conditional on it, others ignore it.
Opinions 2, 3, and 5 directly address capacity.

The diversity shows that the issue was debated vigorously, with no single consensus.
5

You may want to discuss with a teacher or read more on each position.

وحاصل الخلاف فيه أقوال سنة : الأول : عدم الاكتفاء بالتقليد؛ بمعنى عدم صحة التقليد، فيكون المقلّد كافراً، وعليه السنوسي في (الكبرى . الثاني : الاكتفاء بالتقليد مع العصيان مطلقاً ؛ أي: سواء كان فيه أهلية للنظر أم لا . الثالث : الاكتفاء به مع العصيان إن كان فيه أهلية النظر، وإلا فلا عصيان . الرابع : أن من قلَّدَ القرآن والسنة القطعية صح إيمانه؛ لاتباعه القطعي، ومن غير ذلك، لم يصح إيمانُهُ ؛ لعدم أمن الخطأ على غير المعصوم . الخامس : الاكتفاء به من غير عصيان مطلقاً ؛ لأنَّ النظر شرط كمال، فمن كان فيه أهلية النظر ولم ينظر، فقد ترك الأولى. السادس: أن إيمان المقلّد صحيح ويحرم عليه النظر(ه)، وهو محمول على المخلوط بالفلسفة.
The sum of the disagreement concerning it is six opinions: First: Not relying on taqlid (blind acceptance), meaning that taqlid is not valid, so the muqallid is a disbeliever, and al-Sanusi holds this in (al-Kubra). Second: Relying on taqlid with disobedience absolutely, meaning whether he has the capacity for reasoning or not. Third: Relying on it with disobedience if he has the capacity for reasoning, otherwise no disobedience. Fourth: That whoever blindly accepts the Qur'an and the definitively transmitted Sunna, his faith is valid because he follows what is definitive, but otherwise his faith is not valid because of the lack of safety from error regarding the non-infallible. Fifth: Relying on it without disobedience absolutely, because reasoning is a condition of perfection; so if one has the capacity for reasoning and does not reason, he has left the preferable. Sixth: That the faith of the muqallid is valid and it is forbidden for him to engage in reasoning (5), and this is to be understood as referring to someone mixed with philosophy.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of six scholarly opinions on the validity of taqlid (blind acceptance) in matters of faith. The author lists them in order: (1) Taqlid is invalid, making the muqallid a disbeliever—attributed to al-Sanusi. (2) Taqlid is valid but sinful in all cases. (3) Taqlid is valid but sinful only if the person has the capacity to reason; otherwise no sin. (4) Taqlid is valid only when following definitively established texts (Qur'an and mutawatir Sunna), due to certainty; otherwise invalid. (5) Taqlid is valid and not sinful at all, because reasoning is a condition of perfection, not obligation. (6) Taqlid is valid and reasoning is actually forbidden, but this is a position held by those mixed with philosophy. These opinions show the range of scholarly debate on whether intellectual investigation is required for valid faith.

Six Scholarly Opinions on Taqlid in Faith
Enumeration of six scholarly opinions on taqlid in faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the range of scholarly opinions on whether taqlid suffices for valid iman, from those that deem it disbelief to those that affirm it.

Core Idea
Taqlid in faith is a contested issue with six distinct scholarly positions.

This passage presents six opinions on the validity of taqlid in matters of faith. The views range from declaring the muqallid a disbeliever to considering his faith valid and even forbidding inquiry. As you read, notice how each opinion blends considerations of capacity for nazar, the source of authority, and the role of sin.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the six opinions. Don't analyze yet; just absorb the range.
Notice that the opinions are ordered from strictest to most lenient.
2
Identify each opinion's criteria
For each opinion, note the key criteria: whether taqlid is sufficient, whether there is sin, and whether capacity for nazar matters.
Opinion 4 and 5 introduce the nature of the proof followed.
3
Reflect on the spectrum
Consider how the opinions form a spectrum from absolute rejection of taqlid (opinion 1) to full acceptance (opinions 5 and 6).
Opinion 2 and 3 differ on the role of capacity for nazar.
4

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحاصل الخلاف فيه أقوال ستة:
الأول: عدم الاكتفاء بالتقليد؛ بمعنى عدم صحة التقليد، فيكون المقلّد كافراً، وعليه السنوسي في (الكبرى).
الثاني: الاكتفاء بالتقليد مع العصيان مطلقاً؛ أي: سواء كان فيه أهلية للنظر أم لا.
الثالث: الاكتفاء به مع العصيان إن كان فيه أهلية النظر، وإلا فلا عصيان.
الرابع: أن من قلد القرآن والسنة القطعية صح إيمانه؛ لاتباعه القطعي، ومن غير ذلك، لم يصح إيمانه؛ لعدم أمن الخطأ على غير المعصوم.
الخامس: الاكتفاء به من غير عصيان مطلقاً؛ لأن النظر شرط كمال، فمن كان فيه أهلية النظر ولم ينظر، فقد ترك الأولى.
السادس: أن إيمان المقلّد صحيح ويحرم عليه النظر، وهو محمول على المخلوط بالفلسفة.
And the summary of the disagreement concerning it is six opinions:
First: Not sufficing with taqlid (blind following); meaning the invalidity of taqlid, so the muqallid (blind follower) is a disbeliever, and al-Sanusi holds this in (al-Kubra).
Second: Sufficing with taqlid along with sinning absolutely; i.e., whether or not he has the capacity for nazar (rational inquiry).
Third: Sufficing with it along with sinning if he has the capacity for nazar, otherwise no sin.
Fourth: That whoever blindly follows the Qur'an and the categorical Sunnah, his faith is valid due to his following what is categorical, but otherwise his faith is not valid due to the lack of security from error on the part of the non-infallible.
Fifth: Sufficing with it without any sin absolutely, because nazar is a condition of perfection, so whoever has the capacity for nazar and does not engage in it has abandoned what is preferable.
Sixth: That the faith of the muqallid is valid and it is forbidden for him to engage in nazar, and this is interpreted as referring to one mixed with philosophy.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage enumerates six scholarly opinions on the validity of taqlid (blind following) in matters of faith. The author lists them in order of stringency. The first opinion holds that taqlid is insufficient and the muqallid is a disbeliever, citing al-Sanusi. The second opinion allows taqlid but deems it sinful regardless of the person's capacity for nazar (rational inquiry). The third differentiates: if the person has capacity for nazar, taqlid is sinful; otherwise not. The fourth distinguishes between following categorical proofs (Qur'an and mutawatir Sunnah) versus non-categorical ones, deeming only the former valid. The fifth considers taqlid permissible without sin, viewing nazar as a perfection. The sixth affirms the faith of the muqallid but forbids him from nazar, interpreting this as a reference to those influenced by philosophy. The author presents these views neutrally, preparing for later discussion of the correct position.

نظر (nazar)
إيمان (iman)
مقلّد (muqallid)

Correction: The passage shows six nuanced positions depending on capacity, source, and consequences.

Key Takeaway
