(۱) ونزيد في مسألة الآبي بسطاً بنقل ما قاله العلامة الرملي الكبير في فتاويه» (٣٩١/٤)، وهذا نص السؤال والجواب: (سُئل عن شخص بلغته دعوة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ، فآمن بالله بقلبه ولم يتلفظ بالشهادتين مع القدرة على ذلك إلى أن مات فهل هذا الإيمان ينفعُهُ ويكون في الجنة أو لا؟ وإذا قلتم : ينفعه، فما وجب عن قول الإمام النووي في شرح مسلم) حيث قال: واتفق أهل السنة من المحدثين والفقهاء والمتكلمين على أن المؤمن الذي يحكم بأنه من أهل القبلة لا يخلد في النار، لا يكون إلا من اعتقد بقلبه دين الإسلام اعتقاداً جازماً خالياً من الشكوك، ونطق بالشهادتين، فإن اقتصر على أحدهما ، لم يكن من أهل القبلة أصلاً، إلا إذا عجز عن النطق لخلل في لسانه، أو لعدم التمكن منه لمعالجة المنيَّة أو بغير ذلك؟ فأجاب : بأنه مؤمن في أحكام الآخرة دون الدنيا ؛ لأن الإيمان في الشرع تصديق القلب بما عُلم منه ضرورة مجيء الرسول من عند الله به، وقد اختلفوا في أن الإقرار بالشهادتين من المتمكّن م شرط لإجراء أحكام الإيمان عليه أو جزء منه؟ فذهب جمهور المحققين على الأول، وقد قال السعد التفتازاني في شرح المقاصد: أما إذا جعلنا الإيمان اسماً للتصديق فقط، وأن الإقرار شرط لإجراء الأحكام في الدنيا من الصلاة عليه وخلفه والدفن في مقابر المسلمين، والمطالبة بالعشر والزكوات ونحو ذلك، فهو مؤمن في أحكام الآخرة. انتهى. وقال غيره: من عجز عن التلفظ بالشهادتين لخرس أو اخترام منية قبل التمكن، صح إيمانه، قال الإمام الرازي: قطعاً، وقال في الشفاء»: على الصحيح، وإن عرض عليه الإسلام فأبى مع القدرة عليه كأبي طالب، لم يكن مؤمناً بالاتفاق، وكذلك إن لم يعرض عليه عند الجمهور، وقال الغزالي: إنه يكفيه، وقال: كيف يعذَّبُ مَنْ قلبه مملوء بالإيمان؟! وهو المقصود الأصلي، غير أنه لخفاء أنيط الحكم بالإقرار الظاهر، وعلى هذا فهو مؤمن عند الله تعالى، غير مؤمن في أحكام الدنيا عكس المنافق، وهذا ظاهرُ كلام إمام الحرمين في «الإرشاد» أيضاً). بل نقل الإمام الشعراني عن شيخه سيدي علي الخواص في الجواهر والدرر» أن من استفرغ وسعَهُ في النظر ولم يصل للمعرفة، فهو ناج هذا على التسليم بوقوع هذه الصورة، وتقدم أنها مفروضة ولا وجود لها من حيث النقل .

Commentary

It begins by presenting Imam al-Ghazali's preferred view that the refuser is a believer, treating the matter as a fiqhi (jurisprudential) rather than a doctrinal one. The text quotes Ghazali's 'Ihya' where he describes the 'fifth degree': a person who believes in heart, has time to utter the testimony, knows it is obligatory, but does not utter. The Quranic hadith 'there will come out from the Fire he who has the weight of a mustard seed of faith' supports his view. He notes that some scholars consider utterance a pillar (rukn), but Ghazali sees that view as contrary to the linguistic understanding. Then the passage mentions the Murji'a who exaggeratedly claim such a person will not enter the Fire at all, which Ghazali promises to refute. The commentary then clarifies that on Ghazali's view, the conditions attached to the testimony are unnecessary; but there remains the case of a person who believes but refuses to express it—he is a believer in God's sight, not in this world's rulings, unless accompanied by evidence. The text then moves to page 128, discussing a person who believes in heart without utterance: it says if a person believes in heart but does not benefit (even in the afterlife) according to some views. The passage then clarifies the view of the 'people of verification' (ahl al-tahqiq) as transmitted from al-Ramli. It also quotes Shaykh Ahmad Zayni Dahlan that the 'refusal' in the abi case is refusal of Islam, not refusal of utterance, contrary to standard kalam texts. Imam al-Sha'rani is quoted saying that the possessor of tawhid is happy regardless of the aspect of his tawhid. The fatwa also discusses the case of those unable to utter (due to dumbness or death) as valid, and the case of Abu Talib who refused when able is kufr by consensus. Ghazali's view that belief suffices is reiterated. Finally, the passage concludes with a narration from al-Sha'rani that one who exhausts his capacity in speculation but does not reach knowledge is saved, though this scenario is hypothetical and does not occur in transmitted evidence.

Checkpoint
According to al-Sha'rani's narration, who is saved?
A) Whoever believes without effort
B) Whoever exhausts his capacity in reflection but does not attain knowledge
C) Whoever attains knowledge without effort
D) Whoever refuses to reflect

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you content to simply 'exhaust your capacity' in religious matters, or are you truly seeking knowledge? Examine your own efforts: have you ever stopped seeking because you thought you tried enough? What if your 'exhaustion' is really just complacency?
?
This passage says that in reality, sincere seekers will reach knowledge. Is your seeking sincere? What is one area of faith where you have stopped seeking clarity? What would it take to resume that search today?
?
The hypothetical person is saved, but the condition is not actual. Do you rely on God's mercy to excuse your ignorance, or do you actively pursue understanding? What step can you take this week to move from hypothetical safety to actual knowledge?

Faith and Disbelief
intermediate

Faith and Verbal Profession: The Case of the Abider
Al-Ramli's fatwa on faith without verbal profession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction Islamic scholars make between inner faith (taṣdīq al-qalb) and verbal profession (al-iqrār), and how the case of the abider (al-ābī) illustrates that verbal confession is a condition for worldly Islamic rulings but not for salvation in the afterlife.

Core Idea
Inner faith saves in the hereafter; verbal profession governs life in this world.

Sanusi presents a complex theological and legal question: what happens to a person who truly believes in God in their heart but never verbally professes the shahada? Through the fatwa of al-Ramli, we see the nuanced position of Sunni scholars: faith in its essence is inner affirmation, while verbal confession is a requirement for the application of Islamic law in this world. This lesson explores that crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the fatwa question and answer
Start by reading the Arabic passage and translation of al-Ramli's fatwa. Notice how the question is framed and the answer distinguishes between afterlife and worldly rulings.
The fatwa is the core of this lesson.
2
Identify the key distinction
Look for the phrases that separate 'iman in the afterlife' from 'iman in this world'. Understand that the scholars differentiate between the essence of faith (taṣdīq) and the condition for worldly application (iqrār).
This distinction is the main point.
3
Reflect on the scholarly consensus
Consider the references to al-Taftazani, al-Razi, al-Ghazali, and Imam al-Haramayn. Notice that this view is not a fringe opinion but held by the majority of verifiers.
The fatwa aligns with the position that iqrār is a condition (shart) for worldly rulings, not a part (juz') of faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإلهيات في قلبه فلا ينفعُهُ ذلك ولو في الآخرة (۱) ، ومن أقرَّ بلسانه ولم يصدق بقلبه كالمنافق، المسلمون، فيكون ناجياً في الدنيا والآخرة، وعلى هذا القول جرى أهل التحقيق كما سينقل عن الرملي . ونزيد في مسألة الآبي بسطاً بنقل ما قاله العلامة الرملي الكبير في فتاويه» (٣٩١/٤)، وهذا نص السؤال والجواب: (سُئل عن شخص بلغته دعوة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ، فآمن بالله بقلبه ولم يتلفظ بالشهادتين مع القدرة على ذلك إلى أن مات فهل هذا الإيمان ينفعُهُ ويكون في الجنة أو لا؟ وإذا قلتم : ينفعه، فما وجب عن قول الإمام النووي في شرح مسلم) حيث قال: واتفق أهل السنة من المحدثين والفقهاء والمتكلمين على أن المؤمن الذي يحكم بأنه من أهل القبلة لا يخلد في النار، لا يكون إلا من اعتقد بقلبه دين الإسلام اعتقاداً جازماً خالياً من الشكوك، ونطق بالشهادتين، فإن اقتصر على أحدهما ، لم يكن من أهل القبلة أصلاً، إلا إذا عجز عن النطق لخلل في لسانه، أو لعدم التمكن منه لمعالجة المنيَّة أو بغير ذلك؟ فأجاب : بأنه مؤمن في أحكام الآخرة دون الدنيا ؛ لأن الإيمان في الشرع تصديق القلب بما عُلم منه ضرورة مجيء الرسول من عند الله به، وقد اختلفوا في أن الإقرار بالشهادتين من المتمكّن م شرط لإجراء أحكام الإيمان عليه أو جزء منه؟ فذهب جمهور المحققين على الأول، وقد قال السعد التفتازاني في شرح المقاصد: أما إذا جعلنا الإيمان اسماً للتصديق فقط، وأن الإقرار شرط لإجراء الأحكام في الدنيا من الصلاة عليه وخلفه والدفن في مقابر المسلمين، والمطالبة بالعشر والزكوات ونحو ذلك، فهو مؤمن في أحكام الآخرة. انتهى. وقال غيره: من عجز عن التلفظ بالشهادتين لخرس أو اخترام منية قبل التمكن، صح إيمانه، قال الإمام الرازي: قطعاً، وقال في الشفاء»: على الصحيح، وإن عرض عليه الإسلام فأبى مع القدرة عليه كأبي طالب، لم يكن مؤمناً بالاتفاق، وكذلك إن لم يعرض عليه عند الجمهور، وقال الغزالي: إنه يكفيه، وقال: كيف يعذَّبُ مَنْ قلبه مملوء بالإيمان؟! وهو المقصود الأصلي، غير أنه لخفاء أنيط الحكم بالإقرار الظاهر، وعلى هذا فهو مؤمن عند الله تعالى، غير مؤمن في أحكام الدنيا عكس المنافق، وهذا ظاهرُ كلام إمام الحرمين في «الإرشاد» أيضاً).
The divine matters: in his heart, that does not benefit him even in the afterlife (1), and whoever confesses with his tongue but does not believe in his heart is like the hypocrite, the Muslims, so he is saved in this world and the Hereafter. And upon this saying the people of verification proceeded, as will be quoted from al-Ramli. And we add expansion on the issue of the abider by quoting what the great scholar al-Ramli said in his 'Fatawa' (4/391): this is the text of the question and answer: (He was asked about a person whom the call of the Prophet (peace be upon him) reached, so he believed in God in his heart but did not utter the two shahadas despite ability to do so until he died. Is this faith beneficial for him and will he be in Paradise or not? And if you say: it benefits him, then what about the statement of Imam al-Nawawi in 'Sharh Muslim' where he said: The Sunni scholars from the hadith scholars, jurists, and theologians agreed that the believer who is judged to be among the people of the qibla will not abide eternally in Hellfire, [and that] he is only one who believed in his heart in the religion of Islam with a firm belief devoid of doubts, and uttered the two shahadas. If he limits himself to one of them, he is not among the people of the qibla at all, except if he is unable to speak due to a defect in his tongue, or due to inability at the throes of death, or other such reasons? So he answered: He is a believer in the rulings of the afterlife but not in this world, because faith in Islamic law is the affirmation of the heart of what is known necessarily from the messenger's coming from God with it. And they differed: Is the verbal confession by one who is able [to speak] a condition for applying the rulings of faith to him or part of it? The majority of the verifiers inclined to the first. And al-Sa'd al-Taftazani said in 'Sharh al-Maqasid': If we make faith a name only for affirmation, and that confession is a condition for applying the rulings in this world—such as praying over him, following his funeral, burying him in Muslim cemeteries, demanding the tithe and zakat, and so on—then he is a believer in the rulings of the afterlife. End quote. And others said: Whoever is unable to utter the two shahadas due to muteness or sudden death before being able, his faith is valid. Imam al-Razi said: definitely. And he said in 'al-Shifa'': on the correct view. And if Islam was presented to him and he refused despite ability, like Abu Talib, he is not a believer by consensus. And likewise if it was not presented to him, according to the majority. Al-Ghazali said: it is sufficient for him, and he said: How can one be punished whose heart is filled with faith?! And that is the primary objective. However, due to its hiddenness, the ruling is attached to outward confession. On this basis, he is a believer in the sight of God, but not a believer in the rulings of this world, opposite of the hypocrite. And this is apparent from the words of Imam al-Haramayn in 'al-Irshad' as well.)
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is a combined discussion of the ruling on one who believes in heart but does not verbally profess the shahada, known as al-ābī (the abider). The author first states that if belief is only in the heart without utterance, it does not benefit in the afterlife, while the hypocrite who utters but does not believe is judged as a Muslim in worldly affairs. The people of verification follow this view. Then the author adds a lengthy quotation from al-Ramli's fatwa. The fatwa presents a question: if a person believes in God in his heart but never utters the two shahadas while able, is his faith beneficial for the afterlife? The answer distinguishes between divine and worldly rulings: he is a believer in the afterlife but not in worldly rulings, because Islamic faith is affirmation of the heart (taṣdīq al-qalb), and verbal confession (iqrār) is a condition for applying worldly Islamic judgments such as funeral rites, inheritance, and legal treatment. The fatwa cites al-Taftazani, al-Razi, al-Ghazali, and Imam al-Haramayn in support of this distinction. The key point is that faith (īmān) in its essence is inner belief, while verbal confession is a requirement for the application of Islamic law in this world, not for salvation.

Key Terms
الإيمان
التصديق
الإقرار
الآبي
الشهادتان
المنافق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Verbal profession of the shahada is an essential part of faith (juz') without which one cannot be a believer in any sense.
Correction: The majority of Sunni scholars hold that faith is essentially inner affirmation (taṣdīq), and verbal confession is a condition (shart) for applying worldly Islamic rulings, not a part of the essence of faith. Therefore, one who believes in heart but does not verbally profess due to stubbornness (the abider) is still a believer in the afterlife.

Checkpoint
According to the fatwa cited from al-Ramli, what is the status of a person who believes in God in his heart but never verbally professes the shahada, being able to do so?
A) He is a believer in both worldly and afterlife rulings.
B) He is a believer in the afterlife but not in worldly rulings.
C) He is a hypocrite.
D) He is not a believer at all.

Key Takeaway
Inner faith (taṣdīq al-qalb) is sufficient for salvation in the afterlife; verbal profession (al-iqrār) is a condition for the application of Islamic law in this world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Ramli distinguishes between inner faith for the Hereafter and outward confession for this world. Are you satisfied with merely having the outer form of Islam while your heart may be empty of true conviction? What will you do today to ensure your inner faith matches your outer declarations?
?
Imagine you were in the position of the abider: you believe with certainty, yet you never uttered the shahada. Would you be willing to be treated as a non-Muslim in this world for the sake of a hidden faith? What does that tell you about the importance you place on verbal profession?
?
Al-Ghazali asked, 'How can one be punished whose heart is filled with faith?' Examine your own heart: is it truly filled with faith, or just intellectual agreement? What concrete evidence in your life shows that your faith is more than a mental assent?

Sincere Effort in Seeking Knowledge Suffices for Salvation
Al-Sha'rani quotes his teacher on the saved status of the sincere seeker who does not attain knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that in Islamic theology, sincere and exhaustive effort in seeking knowledge of God, even if one does not attain certainty, is sufficient for salvation.

Core Idea
Sincere effort in seeking God's knowledge saves, even without reaching certainty.

This passage from al-Sha'rani addresses a crucial question: what happens to someone who tries their utmost to know God through reflection but never reaches firm knowledge? The answer, transmitted from his teacher, offers reassurance: such a person is saved. However, the author immediately notes that this scenario is merely hypothetical and never actually occurs in transmission.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that it contains two parts: the quote from al-Sha'rani's teacher and the author's own explanatory comment.
Pay attention to the word 'nājī' (saved) – it's the key ruling.
2
Understand the quote
The quote states that one who exhausts his capacity (استفرغ وسعه) in reflection (النظر) but does not attain knowledge (المعرفة) is saved. Reflect on what 'exhausts his capacity' means – it implies maximum sincere effort.
3
Analyze the author's qualification
The author notes that this ruling is based on the admission that such a scenario could occur, but earlier it was stated that this scenario is hypothetical and does not actually happen in transmitted sources. Why do you think the author adds this qualification?
This qualification protects against the idea that one can permanently fail to reach knowledge after sincere effort – in reality, sincere effort always leads to knowledge.
4
Connect to broader theology
This passage reflects a principle: God does not burden a soul beyond its capacity, and sincere seeking is rewarded. It also guards against despair – no one who truly seeks will be left without guidance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بل نقل الإمام الشعراني عن شيخه سيدي علي الخواص في الجواهر والدرر» أن من استفرغ وسعَهُ في النظر ولم يصل للمعرفة، فهو ناج هذا على التسليم بوقوع هذه الصورة، وتقدم أنها مفروضة ولا وجود لها من حيث النقل .
Rather, Imam al-Sha'rani transmitted from his teacher Sayyidi 'Ali al-Khawwas in 'al-Jawahir wa al-Durar' that: 'Whoever exhausts his capacity in reflection and does not arrive at knowledge, he is saved.' This is upon the admission of the occurrence of this scenario, and it has preceded that it is hypothetical and does not exist in terms of transmission.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This short passage is a transmitted quote from Imam al-Sha'rani quoting his teacher Sayyidi 'Ali al-Khawwas. It addresses the case of a person who exerts maximum effort in rational reflection (naẓar) to attain knowledge (ma'rifa) of God but does not succeed in reaching certainty. The ruling is that such a person is saved (nājī). The author notes that this is predicated on the possibility of such a scenario occurring, but earlier it was mentioned that this scenario is hypothetical and has no actual occurrence in transmitted sources (i.e., it is a theoretical possibility but not known to happen in reality).

Key Terms
النظر
المعرفة
الاستفراغ
النجاة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only those who attain certain knowledge of God are saved; sincere but unsuccessful effort is futile.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that one who exhausts his capacity in reflection but does not attain knowledge is saved. Sincere effort itself suffices for salvation.

Checkpoint
According to the quote from al-Sha'rani, what is the ruling on a person who exhausts his capacity in reflection but does not attain knowledge?
A) He is not saved.
B) He is saved.
C) He is sinful.
D) He must repeat his reflection.

Key Takeaway
Sincere and exhaustive effort in seeking knowledge of God, even if one does not reach certainty, is sufficient for salvation, though in reality such effort always leads to knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever feared that your lack of certainty about God might condemn you? This lesson promises that sincere effort itself saves. Do you truly trust that promise, or do you hold yourself to a standard of perfect certainty? What would it look like to rest in the knowledge that God accepts your sincere search, even when you feel uncertain?
?
The passage says the scenario of striving without reaching knowledge is hypothetical—in reality, sincere effort always leads to knowledge. Are you using the excuse of 'I'll never understand' to stop striving? What is one concrete step you can take this week to exhaust your capacity in seeking knowledge of God, leaving no room for half-heartedness?
?
Al-Sha'rani's quote is conditional: it applies only to one who 'exhausts his capacity.' Is your effort in learning your faith truly exhaustive, or do you hold back due to laziness, distraction, or comfort? If you were to exhaust your capacity in one area of Islamic knowledge starting tomorrow, what would that look like? What is the first sacrifice you would need to make?

Verbal Confession: Condition for Worldly Rulings
The author explains that verbal confession is a condition for worldly judgments and discusses the difference between shartiyya and shutriyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that verbal confession is a condition for applying worldly Islamic rulings to a person, not a part of the essence of faith, and that the hypocrite is judged by his outward confession.

Core Idea
Verbal confession is a condition for worldly rulings, not the essence of faith.

This lesson clarifies the role of verbal confession in Islamic law. It addresses the distinction between conditionality (shartiyya) and partiality (shutriyya), and explains why the hypocrite is treated as a Muslim in worldly matters based on his outward confession.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the key ruling
Read the first part where the author states that a person with inner faith but no verbal confession is a believer in worldly rulings unless a sign of unbelief appears. Then read the commentary about the hypocrite.
Pay attention to the phrase 'فهو مؤمن في الأحكام الدنيوية'.
2
Distinguish between conditionality and partiality
The author explains the difference between saying confession is a condition (shart) versus a part (shutr) of faith. Identify which view is preponderant and why.
The author cites Quranic and hadith evidence for conditionality.
3
Reflect on the hypocrite example
Consider why the Prophet treated hypocrites as Muslims. What does this teach about the role of outward confession?
This shows the principle of judging by the apparent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهو مؤمن في الأحكام الدنيوية غير مؤمن عند الله تعالى، ومحل كونه مؤمناً في الأحكام الدنيوية ما لم نطلع على كفره بعلامة كسجود لصنم، وإلا جرتْ عليه أحكام الكفر (1) . (1) وعليه : لو أظهر لنا المنافق أنه مؤمن وتزين بالنطق بالشهادتين، وبالإقرار بكل ما علم من الدين بالضرورة، فهو على ظاهره ونحكم بإسلامه، وما كان المنافقون ليخفّوا على حضرة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ولكنه جرى معهم على الظاهر، والله يتولى السرائر، وزيادة في التفريع : تحل ذبيحة المنافق، وعقد زواجه والإرث وتثبت عصمة دمه وماله ... إلى غير ذلك من فروع الشريعة، ومن غلب على قلبه حكم بنفاق منافق فعليه أن يتحرّى ويستبرئ لدينه ولا يجري على ظواهر الفتاوى دون ورع. وفهم الأقل أن مرادهم أنه شرط في صحة الإيمان، وهذا القول كالقول بالشطرية في الحكم، وإنما الخلاف بينهما في العبارة، والقول الأول هو الراجح، والنصوص بحسب المتبادر منها مقوّيةٌ للقول بالشرطية دون الشطرية؛ كقوله تعالى : أُولَتيك كَتَبَ فِي قُلُوبِهِمُ الإِيمَنَ) [المجادلة: ۲۲]؛ أي: أثبته في قلوبهم، وقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم في دعائه: اللهم ؛ ثبت قلبي على دينك» (۲) قوله: (كَالعَمَلْ ) أي : في مطلق الشرطية، وإن اختلفت جهة الشرطية في المشبَّه والمشبَّه به؛ لأنَّ السابق إما شرط لإجراء الأحكام الدنيوية، أو لصحة الإيمان على ما مر، وهذا شرط كمال على المختار عند أهل السنة (۳) ، فمن أتى بالعمل فقد حصل الكمال، ومن تركَهُ فهو مؤمنٌ ، لكنَّهُ فرَّتَ على نفسه الكمال إذا لم يكن مع استحلال أو عناد للشارع، أو شكٍّ في مشروعيته، وإلا فهو كافر فيما علم من الدين بالضرورة) . (۲) رواه الترمذي (٢١٤٠) ، والنسائي في السنن الكبرى (٧٦٩٠) ، وابن ماجه (٣٨٣٤) من حديث سيدنا أنس والسيدة عائشة رضي الله عنهما مرفوعاً. (۳) يعني : الشرطية في غير النطق للكمال فقط، فمن تركه آبياً مع وجود الإقرار فهو مؤمن عاص؛ كتارك الصلاة عمداً مع الإقرار بوجوبها . (٤) لكنه لو علم وحدانية الله تعالى ورسالة سيدنا محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم وأقرّ بهما ، ثم جهل وراء ذلك معلوماً من الدين بالضرورة، فلا نقول بكفره حتى يُعلَّم ثم ينكر ، وانظر ما تقدم تعليقاً قريباً في قضية الجهل من غير عناد (ص۱۲۸).
So he is a believer in worldly rulings, but not a believer in the sight of God. And the place of his being a believer in worldly rulings is as long as we do not come to know his unbelief by a sign such as prostrating to an idol; otherwise the rulings of unbelief apply to him (1). (1) Based on this: if a hypocrite shows us that he is a believer and adorns himself by uttering the two shahadas and by confessing everything known necessarily of the religion, then he is upon his apparent state and we judge him as a Muslim. And the hypocrites would not have been hidden from the presence of the Prophet (peace be upon him), but he dealt with them according to their apparent state, and God is the one who handles secrets. And additionally regarding branches: the slaughtered animal of a hypocrite is permissible, his marriage contract is valid, inheritance is established, and the inviolability of his blood and wealth is affirmed... and other branches of Islamic law. And whoever's heart overcomes him with the judgment of hypocrisy of a hypocrite, he must investigate and seek clarity for his religion, and not act upon the apparent fatwas without piety. And some understood that their intent is that it is a condition for the validity of faith. This saying is like the saying of it being a part (shutriyya) in the ruling; the difference between them is merely verbal, and the first saying is the preponderant. The texts, according to what is apparent from them, strengthen the saying of conditionality (shartiyya) rather than partiality. For example, God's statement: 'Those – He has written faith in their hearts' [Quran 58:22], meaning He has established it in their hearts. And the Prophet's statement in his supplication: 'O God, make my heart firm upon Your religion' (2). His statement: 'like actions (ka-l-‘amal)' – meaning in absolute conditionality, even though the aspect of conditionality differs in the compared and compared to. Because the former is either a condition for applying worldly rulings, or for the validity of faith as passed, and this is a condition for perfection according to the chosen view of the Ahl al-Sunna (3). So whoever performs actions has attained perfection, and whoever abandons them is a believer, but he has deprived himself of perfection, if it is not accompanied by considering [sin] lawful or stubbornness against the Lawgiver, or doubt in its legislation. Otherwise, he is an unbeliever in what is known necessarily of the religion. (2) Narrated by al-Tirmidhi (2140), al-Nasa'i in al-Sunan al-Kubra (7690), and Ibn Majah (3834) from the hadith of our master Anas and our lady 'Aisha, may God be pleased with them, raised to the Prophet. (3) Meaning: conditionality in other than speech is only for perfection. So whoever abandons it rebelliously while having confession, he is a believer but a sinner, like one who abandons prayer intentionally while acknowledging its obligation. (4) But if he knows the oneness of God and the message of our master Muhammad (peace be upon him) and confesses both, then remains ignorant of something known necessarily of the religion beyond that, we do not say he is an unbeliever until he is informed and then denies [it]. And see what preceded recently as a comment on the issue of ignorance without obstinacy (p. 128).
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal discussion elaborating on the role of verbal confession (iqrār) and actions (ʿamal) in faith. It begins by restating the ruling from the previous discussion: a person who believes in heart but does not utter the shahada is considered a believer in worldly rulings (i.e., treated as a Muslim in society) as long as no sign of unbelief appears, such as prostration to an idol. The footnote (1) explains that the hypocrite who outwardly shows faith is judged as a Muslim in worldly matters, following the precedent of the Prophet's dealings with hypocrites. The passage then clarifies a misunderstanding: some thought that the requirement of verbal confession is a condition for the validity of faith itself, but the author says the difference between that and the view that it is a condition for worldly rulings is merely verbal, with the latter being preponderant. The author cites Quranic and hadith evidence to support conditionality (shartiyya) rather than partiality (shutriyya). Then the passage draws an analogy between actions (ʿamal) and verbal confession: actions are a condition for perfection (kamāl) of faith, not for its validity. Therefore, abandoning actions does not remove faith unless it is accompanied by considering sin lawful or rejecting obligatory knowledge. The footnotes (2-4) provide hadith citation, explanation of the analogy, and an important clarification that ignorance of necessary religious matters does not entail unbelief until after being informed and then rejecting them.

Key Terms
الإقرار
الشرطية
الشطرية
المنافق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Verbal confession is a part of the essence of faith (shutriyya).
Correction: The preponderant view is that it is a condition (shartiyya) for worldly rulings, not a part of faith.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is a hypocrite who outwardly confesses treated as a Muslim?
A) Because his inner faith is genuine.
B) Because we judge by the apparent.
C) Because he is not really a hypocrite.
D) Because the Prophet knew he was a believer.

Key Takeaway
Verbal confession is a condition for worldly Islamic rulings, not a component of faith itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter someone who appears outwardly Muslim but whose beliefs you doubt, how does this lesson challenge your natural inclination to judge their true faith? Are you willing to treat them as a Muslim in worldly dealings despite your suspicions, and what does that require from your heart?
?
The author warns that if your heart overcomes you with judgment of hypocrisy, you must investigate and not act on apparent fatwas without piety. Have you ever acted on your own suspicion of someone's faith? What would it mean for you to 'seek clarity for your religion' in such a situation?
?
The passage distinguishes between conditionality and partiality: verbal confession is not a part of faith's essence but a condition for worldly rulings. How does this affect your understanding of your own faith? Do you rely more on outward acts or inner conviction? Which one do you think God sees?

Actions: Condition for Perfection of Faith
The author states that actions are a condition for perfection of faith, drawing an analogy with verbal confession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that actions are a condition for the perfection (kamāl) of faith, not for its validity, and that abandoning actions without rejection does not remove faith.

Core Idea
Actions perfect faith but are not essential for its validity.

This lesson explains the status of actions in relation to faith. It clarifies that actions are a condition for perfection, not validity, and addresses the case of the one who abandons actions out of laziness versus out of rejection or ignorance.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy with verbal confession
The passage begins by drawing an analogy between actions and verbal confession in terms of conditionality, but clarifies that the aspect differs.
Note the phrase 'في مطلق الشرطية'.
2
Identify the condition for perfection
Find where the author states that actions are a condition for perfection (kamāl) of faith according to Ahl al-Sunna.
The chosen view is that actions are a condition for perfection.
3
Understand the exceptions
Read the conditions under which abandoning actions leads to unbelief (istihlāl, ʿinād, doubt) and the clarification about ignorance.
The passage emphasizes that ignorance does not lead to unbelief until after being informed and then rejecting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهو مؤمن في الأحكام الدنيوية غير مؤمن عند الله تعالى، ومحل كونه مؤمناً في الأحكام الدنيوية ما لم نطلع على كفره بعلامة كسجود لصنم، وإلا جرتْ عليه أحكام الكفر (1) . (1) وعليه : لو أظهر لنا المنافق أنه مؤمن وتزين بالنطق بالشهادتين، وبالإقرار بكل ما علم من الدين بالضرورة، فهو على ظاهره ونحكم بإسلامه، وما كان المنافقون ليخفّوا على حضرة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ولكنه جرى معهم على الظاهر، والله يتولى السرائر، وزيادة في التفريع : تحل ذبيحة المنافق، وعقد زواجه والإرث وتثبت عصمة دمه وماله ... إلى غير ذلك من فروع الشريعة، ومن غلب على قلبه حكم بنفاق منافق فعليه أن يتحرّى ويستبرئ لدينه ولا يجري على ظواهر الفتاوى دون ورع. وفهم الأقل أن مرادهم أنه شرط في صحة الإيمان، وهذا القول كالقول بالشطرية في الحكم، وإنما الخلاف بينهما في العبارة، والقول الأول هو الراجح، والنصوص بحسب المتبادر منها مقوّيةٌ للقول بالشرطية دون الشطرية؛ كقوله تعالى : أُولَتيك كَتَبَ فِي قُلُوبِهِمُ الإِيمَنَ) [المجادلة: ۲۲]؛ أي: أثبته في قلوبهم، وقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم في دعائه: اللهم ؛ ثبت قلبي على دينك» (۲) قوله: (كَالعَمَلْ ) أي : في مطلق الشرطية، وإن اختلفت جهة الشرطية في المشبَّه والمشبَّه به؛ لأنَّ السابق إما شرط لإجراء الأحكام الدنيوية، أو لصحة الإيمان على ما مر، وهذا شرط كمال على المختار عند أهل السنة (۳) ، فمن أتى بالعمل فقد حصل الكمال، ومن تركَهُ فهو مؤمنٌ ، لكنَّهُ فرَّتَ على نفسه الكمال إذا لم يكن مع استحلال أو عناد للشارع، أو شكٍّ في مشروعيته، وإلا فهو كافر فيما علم من الدين بالضرورة) . (۲) رواه الترمذي (٢١٤٠) ، والنسائي في السنن الكبرى (٧٦٩٠) ، وابن ماجه (٣٨٣٤) من حديث سيدنا أنس والسيدة عائشة رضي الله عنهما مرفوعاً. (۳) يعني : الشرطية في غير النطق للكمال فقط، فمن تركه آبياً مع وجود الإقرار فهو مؤمن عاص؛ كتارك الصلاة عمداً مع الإقرار بوجوبها . (٤) لكنه لو علم وحدانية الله تعالى ورسالة سيدنا محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم وأقرّ بهما ، ثم جهل وراء ذلك معلوماً من الدين بالضرورة، فلا نقول بكفره حتى يُعلَّم ثم ينكر ، وانظر ما تقدم تعليقاً قريباً في قضية الجهل من غير عناد (ص۱۲۸).
So he is a believer in worldly rulings, but not a believer in the sight of God. And the place of his being a believer in worldly rulings is as long as we do not come to know his unbelief by a sign such as prostrating to an idol; otherwise the rulings of unbelief apply to him (1). (1) Based on this: if a hypocrite shows us that he is a believer and adorns himself by uttering the two shahadas and by confessing everything known necessarily of the religion, then he is upon his apparent state and we judge him as a Muslim. And the hypocrites would not have been hidden from the presence of the Prophet (peace be upon him), but he dealt with them according to their apparent state, and God is the one who handles secrets. And additionally regarding branches: the slaughtered animal of a hypocrite is permissible, his marriage contract is valid, inheritance is established, and the inviolability of his blood and wealth is affirmed... and other branches of Islamic law. And whoever's heart overcomes him with the judgment of hypocrisy of a hypocrite, he must investigate and seek clarity for his religion, and not act upon the apparent fatwas without piety. And some understood that their intent is that it is a condition for the validity of faith. This saying is like the saying of it being a part (shutriyya) in the ruling; the difference between them is merely verbal, and the first saying is the preponderant. The texts, according to what is apparent from them, strengthen the saying of conditionality (shartiyya) rather than partiality. For example, God's statement: 'Those – He has written faith in their hearts' [Quran 58:22], meaning He has established it in their hearts. And the Prophet's statement in his supplication: 'O God, make my heart firm upon Your religion' (2). His statement: 'like actions (ka-l-‘amal)' – meaning in absolute conditionality, even though the aspect of conditionality differs in the compared and compared to. Because the former is either a condition for applying worldly rulings, or for the validity of faith as passed, and this is a condition for perfection according to the chosen view of the Ahl al-Sunna (3). So whoever performs actions has attained perfection, and whoever abandons them is a believer, but he has deprived himself of perfection, if it is not accompanied by considering [sin] lawful or stubbornness against the Lawgiver, or doubt in its legislation. Otherwise, he is an unbeliever in what is known necessarily of the religion. (2) Narrated by al-Tirmidhi (2140), al-Nasa'i in al-Sunan al-Kubra (7690), and Ibn Majah (3834) from the hadith of our master Anas and our lady 'Aisha, may God be pleased with them, raised to the Prophet. (3) Meaning: conditionality in other than speech is only for perfection. So whoever abandons it rebelliously while having confession, he is a believer but a sinner, like one who abandons prayer intentionally while acknowledging its obligation. (4) But if he knows the oneness of God and the message of our master Muhammad (peace be upon him) and confesses both, then remains ignorant of something known necessarily of the religion beyond that, we do not say he is an unbeliever until he is informed and then denies [it]. And see what preceded recently as a comment on the issue of ignorance without obstinacy (p. 128).
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal discussion elaborating on the role of verbal confession (iqrār) and actions (ʿamal) in faith. It begins by restating the ruling from the previous discussion: a person who believes in heart but does not utter the shahada is considered a believer in worldly rulings (i.e., treated as a Muslim in society) as long as no sign of unbelief appears, such as prostration to an idol. The footnote (1) explains that the hypocrite who outwardly shows faith is judged as a Muslim in worldly matters, following the precedent of the Prophet's dealings with hypocrites. The passage then clarifies a misunderstanding: some thought that the requirement of verbal confession is a condition for the validity of faith itself, but the author says the difference between that and the view that it is a condition for worldly rulings is merely verbal, with the latter being preponderant. The author cites Quranic and hadith evidence to support conditionality (shartiyya) rather than partiality (shutriyya). Then the passage draws an analogy between actions (ʿamal) and verbal confession: actions are a condition for perfection (kamāl) of faith, not for its validity. Therefore, abandoning actions does not remove faith unless it is accompanied by considering sin lawful or rejecting obligatory knowledge. The footnotes (2-4) provide hadith citation, explanation of the analogy, and an important clarification that ignorance of necessary religious matters does not entail unbelief until after being informed and then rejecting them.

Key Terms
العمل
الكمال
أهل السنة
الاستحلال
العناد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Actions are a condition for the validity of faith.
Correction: Actions are a condition for perfection, not validity. A believer who abandons actions is still a believer but lacks perfection.

Checkpoint
What is the status of a person who abandons actions (e.g., prayer) but still has faith and confesses?
A) He is a disbeliever.
B) He is a believer but has deprived himself of perfection.
C) His faith is invalid.
D) He is a hypocrite.

Key Takeaway
Actions are a condition for the perfection of faith, not its validity; a believer who abandons actions is still a believer but lacking perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there actions you have abandoned while acknowledging their obligation? How does this passage challenge your self-perception as a believer? What would it take for you to pursue perfection, knowing that your faith is valid but incomplete?
?
When you sin, do you feel that your faith is nullified or that you have left the fold of Islam? How does the teaching that you remain a believer unless you resort to istihlāl, ʿinād, or shakk guard you against both despair and extreme reckoning?
?
Have you ever judged someone as a disbeliever based on their actions or ignorance? How does the footnote about ignorance and the need for informing before takfīr correct your inclination to be hasty in judgment? How can you apply this in your interactions with those who are neglectful?

Faith and Disbelief

The Hypocrite's Faith and Action as Perfection
Defines the hypocrite's status and argues action is a condition for perfection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the hypocrite's outward faith for worldly rulings and his inward disbelief, and why action is considered a condition for perfection, not a part of the essence of faith.

Core Idea
Action perfects faith but is not its essence; the hypocrite's faith is valid in this world but not with Allah.

This passage from al-Sanusi's commentary clarifies a critical point in Islamic theology: the status of those who outwardly profess faith but inwardly disbelieve. It goes on to resolve a key debate about whether actions are part of faith or a condition for its perfection. Let's follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Notice how the author distinguishes between worldly and real faith.
The passage has two main sections: the hypocrite's ruling and the action condition.
2
Identify the two rulings for the hypocrite
The passage says the hypocrite is a believer in worldly rulings but a disbeliever with Allah. Find the condition for applying worldly rulings and the signs that change that.
The condition is that we do not see a clear sign of disbelief like prostration to an idol.
3
Understand the shart vs shatr debate
The passage contrasts two views: action as a condition for validity of faith (shart) and action as a part of faith (shatr). See how the author argues for the condition view using Quran and hadith.
The preponderant view is that action is a condition for perfection, not essence.
4
Reflect on the perfection condition
The passage concludes that action is a condition for perfection. Note what happens if someone leaves action defiantly or out of ignorance.
Leaving action without obstinacy does not negate faith itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهو مؤمن في الأحكام الدنيوية غير مؤمن عند الله تعالى، ومحل كونه مؤمناً في الأحكام الدنيوية ما لم نطلع على كفره بعلامة كسجود لصنم، وإلا جرتْ عليه أحكام الكفر (1).
وعليه : لو أظهر لنا المنافق أنه مؤمن وتزين بالنطق بالشهادتين، وبالإقرار بكل ما علم من الدين بالضرورة، فهو على ظاهره ونحكم بإسلامه، وما كان المنافقون ليخفّوا على حضرة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ولكنه جرى معهم على الظاهر، والله يتولى السرائر، وزيادة في التفريع : تحل ذبيحة المنافق، وعقد زواجه والإرث وتثبت عصمة دمه وماله ... إلى غير ذلك من فروع الشريعة، ومن غلب على قلبه حكم بنفاق منافق فعليه أن يتحرّى ويستبرئ لدينه ولا يجري على ظواهر الفتاوى دون ورع.
وفهم الأقل أن مرادهم أنه شرط في صحة الإيمان، وهذا القول كالقول بالشطرية في الحكم، وإنما الخلاف بينهما في العبارة، والقول الأول هو الراجح، والنصوص بحسب المتبادر منها مقوّيةٌ للقول بالشرطية دون الشطرية؛ كقوله تعالى : أُولَتيك كَتَبَ فِي قُلُوبِهِمُ الإِيمَنَ) [المجادلة: ۲۲]؛ أي: أثبته في قلوبهم، وقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم في دعائه: اللهم ؛ ثبت قلبي على دينك» (۲) قوله: (كَالعَمَلْ ) أي : في مطلق الشرطية، وإن اختلفت جهة الشرطية في المشبَّه والمشبَّه به؛ لأنَّ السابق إما شرط لإجراء الأحكام الدنيوية، أو لصحة الإيمان على ما مر، وهذا شرط كمال على المختار عند أهل السنة (۳) ، فمن أتى بالعمل فقد حصل الكمال، ومن تركَهُ فهو مؤمنٌ ، لكنَّهُ فرَّتَ على نفسه الكمال إذا لم يكن مع استحلال أو عناد للشارع، أو شكٍّ في مشروعيته، وإلا فهو كافر فيما علم من الدين بالضرورة.
(۲) رواه الترمذي (٢١٤٠) ، والنسائي في السنن الكبرى (٧٦٩٠) ، وابن ماجه (٣٨٣٤) من حديث سيدنا أنس والسيدة عائشة رضي الله عنهما مرفوعاً. (۳) يعني : الشرطية في غير النطق للكمال فقط، فمن تركه آبياً مع وجود الإقرار فهو مؤمن عاص؛ كتارك الصلاة عمداً مع الإقرار بوجوبها .
(٤) لكنه لو علم وحدانية الله تعالى ورسالة سيدنا محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم وأقرّ بهما ، ثم جهل وراء ذلك معلوماً من الدين بالضرورة، فلا نقول بكفره حتى يُعلَّم ثم ينكر ، وانظر ما تقدم تعليقاً قريباً في قضية الجهل من غير عناد (ص۱۲۸).
So he is a believer in worldly rulings, not a believer with Allah. The condition for being a believer in worldly rulings is that we do not discover his disbelief by a sign such as prostration to an idol; otherwise the rulings of disbelief apply to him (1). And based on that: if a hypocrite shows us that he is a believer and adorns himself with uttering the two testimonies and affirming everything known necessarily from the religion, then he is on his apparent state and we judge him as a Muslim. The hypocrites would not have been hidden from the Prophet ﷺ, but he dealt with them according to the apparent, and Allah oversees the secrets. Additionally, as a subsidiary point: the slaughtered animal of a hypocrite is permissible, his marriage contract and inheritance are valid, and the inviolability of his blood and property is established... and other branches of the Sharia. And he who is convinced in his heart that a particular person is a hypocrite must investigate and be cautious for his religion, and not act merely according to the surface of fatwas without piety. And the lesser understood [from the statement] is that they intended that it is a condition for the validity of faith. This statement is like the statement of part-whole in the ruling, and the difference between them is only in expression. The first opinion is the preponderant, and the primary apparent meanings of the texts support the condition view over the part-whole view, as His statement: "Those are those in whose hearts He has inscribed faith" [58:22] i.e., He established it in their hearts, and his statement ﷺ in his supplication: "O Allah, establish my heart upon Your religion" (2). His statement: "like action" i.e., in absolute conditionality, even though the aspect of conditionality differs in the comparison; because the preceding [action] is either a condition for applying worldly rulings or for the validity of faith as has passed, and this is a condition for perfection according to the chosen position of Ahl al-Sunna (3). So whoever performs the action has attained perfection, and whoever leaves it is a believer, but he has deprived himself of perfection if it is not accompanied by deeming it lawful or obstinacy towards the Lawgiver or doubt in its legality; otherwise he is a disbeliever in what is known necessarily from the religion. (2) Reported by al-Tirmidhi (2140), al-Nasa'i in al-Sunan al-Kubra (7690), and Ibn Majah (3834) from the hadith of our master Anas and Lady Aisha, may Allah be pleased with them, raised as marfu'. (3) Meaning: conditionality in other than utterance is only for perfection, so whoever leaves it defiantly while having affirmation is a disobedient believer; like the one who deliberately leaves prayer while affirming its obligation. (4) But if he knows the oneness of Allah and the message of our master Muhammad ﷺ and affirms them, then becomes ignorant beyond that of something known necessarily from the religion, we do not say he is a disbeliever until he is taught and then denies. And refer to what was recently commented on the issue of ignorance without obstinacy (p.128).
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage defines the intermediate status of a hypocrite: outwardly a believer for worldly rulings but inwardly a disbeliever. It explains that as long as no sign of disbelief appears, we treat the hypocrite as a Muslim. Then it contrasts two views: action as a condition for the validity of faith (shartiyya) versus action as a part (shutriyya). The author argues for the condition view, citing Quran and hadith, and clarifies that action is a condition for perfection, not for the essence of faith. Footnotes provide supporting hadith and clarify rulings on ignorance.

Key Terms
مؤمن
منافق
شرط
شطر
شرط كمال
أهل السنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that action is a part (shatr) of the essence of faith, meaning that without action, faith is invalid or incomplete in essence.
Correction: The passage clarifies that action is a condition for perfection, not a part of the essence. One can have faith without action, though it is deficient. The preponderant view is that action is a condition (shart) for perfection, not a component of the reality of faith.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, if a person performs the outward acts of faith but is inwardly a hypocrite, what is his ruling in worldly matters?
A) He is treated as a disbeliever.
B) He is treated as a believer.
C) He is treated as a Muslim only if he performs all actions perfectly.
D) He is considered a believer only if he has no inner doubt.

Key Takeaway
The hypocrite is treated as a believer in this world, but only actions done with sincere faith bring perfection; deeds are a condition for perfection, not the core of faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe, but are there actions you consistently neglect—like prayer, charity, or honesty—that you know are obligations? If action is a condition for perfection, are you content with an imperfect faith? What will you commit to change starting today?
?
The Prophet ﷺ judged by the apparent and left secrets to Allah. Do you secretly judge others based on what you see, forgetting that only Allah knows the heart? How can you cultivate humility by leaving judgment to Allah while still holding yourself to a higher standard?
?
You have learned that leaving an action without deeming it lawful does not remove your faith, but it does deprive you of perfection. Are you comfortable with that deprivation? Imagine standing before Allah with a faith that is technically valid but incomplete—what actions are you willing to sacrifice for that flawed state? Ask yourself: what one deed, if you did it consistently, would bring you closer to perfection?

Verbal Confession as a Part of Faith and the Mute Objection
Presents the position that iqrar is a part of faith and raises the objection about the mute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the alternative definition of faith that includes verbal confession as an essential part, and the objection raised concerning the mute person.

Core Idea
Faith includes both inner affirmation and verbal confession, but what about those who cannot speak?

This lesson examines a significant theological debate about the definition of faith. Some scholars, including Imam Abu Hanifa, argued that verbal confession is an essential part of faith, not merely a condition. But this raises a puzzle: how can a mute person, who cannot speak, be a believer? We'll explore this objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice the key terms: shart (condition) and shatr (part).
The passage ends abruptly; the objection is incomplete.
2
Identify the two views on iqrar
The passage contrasts two positions: iqrar as a condition (shart) vs. iqrar as a part (shatr). Locate the claim that for Abu Hanifa and some Ash'aris, iqrar is a part.
3
Understand the mute objection
The objection states that a mute person (who cannot speak) still has faith. How does this challenge the view that iqrar is a part of faith?
The argument would be: if iqrar is indispensable, the mute cannot be a believer, but by consensus he is.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider why this objection is powerful. It suggests that verbal confession is not an essential part of faith but rather a condition or outward expression.
This connects to the broader debate on the definition of faith between various schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَقِيلَ : بَلْ شَطْرُ ) أي : وقال قوم محققون ؛ كالإمام أبي حنيفة وجماعة من الأشاعرة : ليس الإقرار بالشهادتين شرطاً ، بل هو شطر، فيكون الإيمان عند هؤلاء اسماً لعملي القلب واللسان جميعاً، وهما التصديق والإقرار . واعترض على هذا القول بأن الإيمان يوجد في المعذور كالأخرس، والشيء
His statement: 'And it is said: rather it is a part (shatr)', i.e., some verifying scholars, like Imam Abu Hanifa and a group of Ash'aris, said: Confession (iqrar) with the two testimonies is not a condition (shart), but rather it is a part (shatr). So faith according to these people is a name for both the actions of the heart and the tongue, namely affirmation (tasdiq) and confession (iqrar). This statement was objected to by [the argument] that faith exists in the excused one, like the mute, and the thing...
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage states an alternative view on the definition of faith: it includes not only inner affirmation but also verbal confession as an essential part. This is attributed to Abu Hanifa and some Ash'aris. The author then reports an objection: if confession is a part of faith, then how can a mute person, who is excused (ma'dhur), be considered a believer? The mute cannot verbally confess, yet he is still a believer by consensus. The objection cuts off at 'and the thing', but the argument would be that a person who is incapable of speech still has faith without utterance, proving utterance is not an essential part.

Key Terms
إقرار
شطر
أبو حنيفة
أخرس
معذور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Verbal confession is indispensable for faith.
Correction: The mute example shows that a person who cannot speak still has faith, proving confession is not an indispensable part.

Checkpoint
What objection is raised against the view that verbal confession is a part of faith?
A) That confession is not mentioned in the Quran
B) That a mute person has faith without being able to speak
C) That Abu Hanifa himself retracted this view
D) That the Khawarij also reject this view

Key Takeaway
The view that verbal confession is a part of faith faces the objection that a mute person, who cannot speak, is still considered a believer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were struck mute tomorrow, would your inner conviction be strong enough to sustain your faith? What does your answer reveal about the depth of your heart's affirmation right now?
?
The mute is excused because of inability—but you have the ability to speak. Are you using your tongue to confess your faith openly, or are you silent when you should speak? What justifies your silence?
?
Do you treat the words of shahada as a mere utterance, or do you mean them with your whole being? What needs to change so that your tongue and heart are perfectly aligned?

Definitions of Iman and Islam and Legal Concomitance
Defining iman and islam and their legal relationship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and technical definitions of iman and islam and the concept of legal concomitance between them when considering the same aspect.

Core Idea
Iman and islam are legally inseparable, but conceptually distinct.

The passage begins by establishing the foundational definition of religion as necessary knowledge and submission. It then defines iman and islam as both meaning and individuals, and explains how they are legally concomitant when the aspect is unified. This lesson will guide you through these definitions and the important condition of 'considered aspect'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the literal sense.
Pay attention to the terms 'concept' (mafhūm) and 'individuals' (afrād).
2
Identify the definitions and condition
Note how iman and islam are defined linguistically and as concepts, and the condition for their legal concomitance: unification of the considered aspect (ittihād al-jihah al-muʿtabarah).
The aspect refers to whether we consider God's judgment or human judgment.
3
Reflect on the example
Consider the example of a person who believes but dies before outward submission. In God's sight he is both believer and Muslim, but in our worldly judgment he is not. This illustrates the condition.

الإلهيات
الدين بالضرورة(۱) ؛ وأما معناه لغةٌ : فهو مطلقُ الامتثالِ والانقياد، وعلى هذا فالإيمان والإسلام مفهوماً :أي معنى وماصدقاً ؛ أي: أفراداً، وإن تلازما شرعاً باعتبار المحل بعد اتحاد الجهة المعتبرة (۲) ، فلا يُوجد مؤمنٌ ليس بمسلم، ولا مسلم ليس بمؤمن . ولا يرد من صدَّقَ واخترمته المنيَّة مثلاً ؛ لأنه عند الله مؤمن ومسلم(۳)، وعندنا ليس بمؤمن ولا مسلم، فالتلازم بعد اتحاد الجهة المعتبرة كما علمت.
(٤)
والكلام في الإيمان المنجي والإسلام كذلك (4) ، وإلا فلا تلازم، بل بينهما العموم والخصوص الوجهي، يجتمعان فيمن صدَّقَ بقلبه وانقاد بظاهره، وينفرد الإيمانُ فيمن صدَّقَ بقلبه فقط، والإسلام فيمن انقاد بظاهره فقط، وهذا ما ذهب إليه جمهورُ
الأشاعرة.
وذهب جمهورُ الماتريدية والمحققون من الأشاعرة إلى اتحاد مفهوميهما. وظاهره أن الخلاف حقيقي، والتزمَهُ بعضهم قائلاً بأن معنى الإسلام عندهم الإذعان الباطني ؛ بدليل : أَفَمَن شَرَحَ اللهُ صَدْرَهُ لِلْإِسْلَامِ ﴾ [الزمر: ٢٢]، والأولون يجيبون بأن المعنى: أفمن شرح الله صدره لقبول الإسلام وإن كان ادعاء الحذف خلاف الأصل، وعلى هذا فالنطقُ دليل عليهما، والعمل كمالٌ لهما .
وبعضُهُمْ جعل الخلاف لفظيًّا باعتبار المآل فحمل القول باتحاد مفهوميهما
(۱) قال ابن الناظم في حاشيته (ص) (٦٠) : (والمراد : الإذعان لتلك الأحكام وعدم ردّها ؛ سواء عملها أو لم يعملها، والإذعان الظاهري يحصل بالنطق بالشهادتين، والإقرار بالمعلوم من الدين
بالضرورة. (۲) قوله : ( بعد اتحاد الجهة المعتبرة) المراد بالجهة المعتبرة التقييد بما عند الله أو بما عندنا . انتهى
أجهوري.
(۳) قوله : (ومسلم) بمعنى أن الله يُعامله معاملة المسلمين، وليس المراد أنه مسلم حقيقة؛ لأن الفرض أنه لم يقع منه إسلام. انتهى أجهوري. (٤) فلا يرد عليه قوله سبحانه: ﴿قَالَتِ الْأَعْرَابُ وَامَنَّا قُل لَّمْ تُؤْمِنُوا وَلَكِن قُولُوا أَسْلَمْنَا وَلَمَّا يَدْخُلِ الْإِيمَانُ فِي قُلُوبِكُمْ ﴾ [الحجرات : ١٤]؛ إذ المراد من الآية كما عند العلامة الخازن في تفسيره» (أي: استسلمنا وانقدنا مخافة القتل والسبي)، فهو معنى لغوي؛ إذ التحقيق اتحاد مفهومي الإيمان والإسلام.
تحفة المريد على جوهرة التوحيد
على معنى أن كل من اتَّصف بأحدهما فهو متصف بالآخر شرعاً وإن تغايرا معنى وحمل القول بتغاير مفهوميهما على أنهما متغايران معنى وإن اتحدا محلا . فآلَ الأمرُ إلى أنهما متغايران معنى وأفراداً باتفاق فمعنى الإيمان: التصديق الباطني، وأفراده: تصديقات كتصديق زيد وتصديق عمرو وتصديق بكر وهكذا، ومعنى الإسلام الانقياد وأفراده: انقيادات؛ كانقياد زيد وانقياد عمرو وانقياد بكر وهكذا، وأما محلُّهما فهو واحدٌ ؛ فكلُّ محلّ لأحدِهِما محلّ للآخر، وبالعكس.
The Divine Affairs. Religion is by necessity (1); as for its linguistic meaning, it is absolute compliance and submission. On this basis, iman and islam are concepts: meaning and extension; i.e., individuals, and they are legally concomitant considering the locus after the unification of the considered aspect (2), so there is no believer who is not a Muslim, and no Muslim who is not a believer. The case of one who believed and was cut off by death, for example, is not an objection; because he is a believer and Muslim in the sight of God (3), while in our sight he is neither a believer nor a Muslim. Thus the concomitance is after the unification of the considered aspect, as you have learned. (4) The discussion concerns saving iman and islam similarly (4); otherwise there is no concomitance, but rather between them is general and particular in one respect; they combine in one who believes in his heart and submits outwardly, and iman is unique to one who believes in his heart only, and islam is unique to one who submits outwardly only. This is the view of the majority of Ash'aris. The majority of Maturidis and the investigators among the Ash'aris held that their two concepts are one. The apparent meaning is that the disagreement is real, and some of them adhered to it, saying that the meaning of islam according to them is inner submission, based on the proof: 'Is he whose breast God has expanded to Islam...' [Zumar: 22]. The former group answers that the meaning is: Is he whose breast God has expanded to accept Islam, even if claiming ellipsis is against the principle. On this basis, speech is evidence for both, and action is perfection for both. Some of them made the disagreement verbal, considering the outcome, and interpreted the statement that their two concepts are one (1) Ibn al-Nazim said in his gloss (p. 60): 'What is meant is submission to those rulings and not rejecting them, whether he acts upon them or not; and outward submission occurs by uttering the two testimonies and acknowledging what is known of religion by necessity.' (2) He said: 'After the unification of the considered aspect' means by the considered aspect is the restriction to what is in God's sight or in our sight. End of Ajhuri. (3) He said: 'And Muslim' means that God treats him with the treatment of Muslims, and it is not meant that he is a Muslim in reality because the assumption is that no islam has occurred from him. End of Ajhuri. (4) Thus it does not refute His saying: 'The Bedouins say: We believe. Say: You do not believe, but say: We have submitted, for faith has not yet entered your hearts' [Hujurat: 14]; for what is meant by the verse, as according to al-'Allama al-Khazin in his Tafsir, is: 'We have surrendered and submitted out of fear of killing and captivity,' so it is a linguistic meaning; for the verified view is the unity of the concepts of iman and islam. Tuhfat al-Murid 'ala Jawharat al-Tawhid, meaning that whoever is described by one of them is described by the other legally, even if they differ in meaning, and the statement that their two concepts are different is interpreted as meaning they are different in meaning even if their locus is one. So the matter results in that they are different in meaning and individuals by agreement. The meaning of iman is inner belief, and its individuals are beliefs, such as Zayd's belief, 'Amr's belief, and Bakr's belief, and so on. The meaning of islam is submission, and its individuals are submissions, such as Zayd's submission, 'Amr's submission, and Bakr's submission, and so on. As for their locus, it is one; every locus of one of them is a locus for the other, and vice versa.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal discussion defining the terms 'iman' (faith) and 'islam' (submission) and examining their relationship according to different schools of Sunni theology. The commentary begins by stating that 'religion' (din) is known by necessity, and its linguistic meaning is absolute compliance and submission. Building on this, the text explains that iman and islam are considered as concepts (meaning) and extensions (individuals). They are legally concomitant when considering the same locus and aspect (e.g., in God's sight or in human judgment). This means that in God's sight, a person who dies after believing but before performing outward acts is still a believer and a Muslim, whereas in our worldly judgment, we cannot call him such because we only judge by outward appearance. The discussion then moves to the specific relationship between 'saving iman' and 'islam' itself. The majority of Ash'aris hold that they are related as general and particular in one respect: they combine in one who believes inwardly and submits outwardly, but iman can exist alone (true inner belief without outward acts), and islam can exist alone (outward submission without true inner belief). The Maturidis and some advanced Ash'aris hold that the concepts are identical. The apparent disagreement is real, with some arguing that islam means inner submission, citing the Quranic verse about 'expanding the breast to Islam.' The first group (Ash'ari majority) responds by interpreting the verse ellipitically as 'acceptance of Islam,' preferring literal interpretation. They see the utterance of the testimony as evidence of both, and works as perfection. Some later scholars considered the dispute merely verbal, since it ultimately means that both terms apply legally regardless of conceptual difference. The passage concludes by summarizing the agreed-upon result: iman and islam differ in meaning and individuals (iman is inner belief, its individuals are beliefs; islam is submission, its individuals are submissions), but their locus is one—the same person can be described by both.

Key Terms
الإيمان
الإسلام
التصديق الباطني
الانقياد
التلازم
الجهة المعتبرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that iman and islam are always identical in meaning and extension, or that they are completely independent.
Correction: They are legally concomitant under a unified aspect, but conceptually distinct. The example of the believer who dies before outward submission shows that in God's sight they coincide, but in human judgment they do not.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under what condition are iman and islam legally concomitant?
A) Always, without exception
B) Only after unification of the considered aspect
C) Only in this world
D) Only for believers

Key Takeaway
Iman and islam are legally inseparable under a unified aspect (e.g., God's judgment), but conceptually distinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at others, which aspect do you judge by—God's merciful sight or people's outward acts? And when you examine yourself, do you prioritize inner faith over outward performance, or do you rely on deeds while neglecting the heart?
?
If death were to cut off your life right now, would God accept you based on your iman alone, or would your islam be incomplete? What does that reveal about the state of your submission?
?
The condition of unified aspect means that our human judgment of faith and submission is limited. Are you willing to accept that your assessment of others may be wrong? How can you live with the humility that only God truly knows who is a believer and Muslim?

Faith and Disbelief
advanced
8 min
Scholarly Views on the Relationship Between Iman and Islam
Exploring scholarly disagreements on iman and islam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disagreement between the Ash'ari majority and the Maturidi/investigators on the relationship between iman and islam, and the resolution of the dispute.

Core Idea
The relationship between iman and islam is a matter of scholarly debate with real implications.

After establishing the basic definitions, the passage delves into the different scholarly opinions on whether iman and islam are conceptually distinct or identical. This lesson will guide you through the Ash'ari position of general-particular, the Maturidi position of unity, the use of Quranic evidence, and the eventual reconciliation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the translation. Notice the different groups mentioned.
Look for 'majority of Ash'aris', 'majority of Maturidis', 'investigators among the Ash'aris'.
2
Identify the two main views
The first view (majority Ash'ari) holds that iman and islam are related as general and particular in one respect (al-ʿumūm wa al-khuṣūṣ al-wajhi). The second view (Maturidi/investigators) holds that their concepts are identical.
3
Examine the Quranic evidence and response
The Maturidi view cites Quran 39:22: 'Is he whose breast God has expanded to Islam...' as evidence that islam means inner submission. The Ash'ari view responds by interpreting it as 'acceptance of Islam' (ellipsis).
Note the methodological difference: Maturidis allow ellipsis? Actually, the Ash'ari majority objects to claiming an ellipsis (ḥadhf) as it is against the principle.
4
Understand the final resolution
Some scholars considered the disagreement merely verbal (lafẓī). The passage concludes that by agreement, iman and islam differ in meaning and individuals, but their locus (maḥall) is one: the same person can be described by both.

The Divine Affairs. Religion is by necessity (1); as for its linguistic meaning, it is absolute compliance and submission. On this basis, iman and islam are concepts: meaning and extension; i.e., individuals, and they are legally concomitant considering the locus after the unification of the considered aspect (2), so there is no believer who is not a Muslim, and no Muslim who is not a believer. The case of one who believed and was cut off by death, for example, is not an objection; because he is a believer and Muslim in the sight of God (3), while in our sight he is neither a believer nor a Muslim. Thus the concomitance is after the unification of the considered aspect, as you have learned. This is the view of the majority of Ash'aris. The majority of Maturidis and the investigators among the Ash'aris held that their two concepts are one. The apparent meaning is that the disagreement is real, and some of them adhered to it, saying that the meaning of islam according to them is inner submission, based on the proof: 'Is he whose breast God has expanded to Islam...' [Zumar: 22]. The former group answers that the meaning is: Is he whose breast God has expanded to accept Islam, even if claiming ellipsis is against the principle. On this basis, speech is evidence for both, and action is perfection for both. Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal discussion defining the terms 'iman' (faith) and 'islam' (submission) and examining their relationship according to different schools of Sunni theology. The commentary begins by stating that 'religion' (din) is known by necessity, and its linguistic meaning is absolute compliance and submission. Building on this, the text explains that iman and islam are considered as concepts (meaning) and extensions (individuals). They are legally concomitant when considering the same locus and aspect (e.g., in God's sight or in human judgment). This means that in God's sight, a person who dies after believing but before performing outward acts is still a believer and a Muslim, whereas in our worldly judgment, we cannot call him such because we only judge by outward appearance. The discussion then moves to the specific relationship between 'saving iman' and 'islam' itself. The majority of Ash'aris hold that they are related as general and particular in one respect: they combine in one who believes inwardly and submits outwardly, but iman can exist alone (true inner belief without outward acts), and islam can exist alone (outward submission without true inner belief). The Maturidis and some advanced Ash'aris hold that the concepts are identical. The apparent disagreement is real, with some arguing that islam means inner submission, citing the Quranic verse about 'expanding the breast to Islam.' The first group (Ash'ari majority) responds by interpreting the verse ellipitically as 'acceptance of Islam,' preferring literal interpretation. They see the utterance of the testimony as evidence of both, and works as perfection. Some later scholars considered the dispute merely verbal, since it ultimately means that both terms apply legally regardless of conceptual difference. The passage concludes by summarizing the agreed-upon result: iman and islam differ in meaning and individuals (iman is inner belief, its individuals are beliefs; islam is submission, its individuals are submissions), but their locus is one—the same person can be described by both.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the disagreement between Ash'aris and Maturidis on iman and islam is purely semantic with no practical implications.
Correction: The passage shows that the disagreement has implications: the Ash'ari majority allows for the possibility of iman without islam (inner belief without outward acts), while the Maturidi view requires inner submission as part of islam, making the two identical. Both sides still agree that the same person can be described by both.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between iman and islam according to the majority of Ash'aris?
A) They are identical in meaning.
B) They are related as general and particular in one respect (al-ʿumūm wa al-khuṣūṣ al-wajhi).
C) They are completely separate.
D) Islam is a subset of iman.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari majority sees iman and islam as intersecting but distinct, while Maturidis see them as identical; the disagreement is resolved by noting they differ in meaning and individuals but share the same locus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Ash'ari majority holds that iman (inner belief) and islam (outward submission) are distinct but overlapping. Reflect on your own life: is there an area where your outward compliance (Islam) exceeds your inner conviction (Iman), or where your inner belief is not matched by outward action? What is one specific step you can take today to close that gap?
?
The Maturidi view identifies iman and islam as one reality—true submission of the heart. Have you ever treated your religious duties as mere rituals rather than expressions of inner surrender? What would change if you saw every act of worship as a direct reflection of your iman?
?
The lesson concludes that despite scholarly disagreement, iman and islam share the same person as their locus. How does this unity of location challenge any tendency you might have to compartmentalize your faith—separating 'spiritual' beliefs from 'daily' actions? What is one practical way you can ensure your entire being—inner and outer—submits to Allah today?

The Testimony of Faith as the Foundation of Islam
Example of pillars emphasizing testimony as foundational.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the testimony of faith is the foundational pillar of Islam and how the other pillars serve as examples of outward submission.

Core Idea
The testimony of faith is the bedrock; all other pillars follow from it.

In this passage, the commentator explains why the poet omitted the testimony of faith from the list of five pillars in the example. The key insight is that the testimony is not just one pillar among equals—it is the fundamental act of submission that underlies all others. The example illustrates the principle that the testimony alone suffices as the basis for outward compliance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the main point about the testimony and the example.
Pay attention to the phrase 'كلمة الشهادة تكفي' (the word of testimony suffices).
2
Identify the role of the testimony
Notice that the author omitted the testimony from the list, and the reason given is that its explanation preceded. Reflect on why the testimony is treated separately from the other pillars.
The testimony is the verbal acknowledgment that validates all other actions.
3
Understand 'submission' (الإذعان)
The passage states that the basis of Islam is submission to the mentioned obligations. For the testimony, submission means uttering it; for others, it is evident in the act itself.
Outward submission is the key concept—it is verbal acknowledgment of obligation.
4
Connect the example to the rule
The example of hajj, prayer, etc., is a particular instance of the universal rule that the testimony suffices. Recognize that the example does not specify the ruling; it merely illustrates it.
The footnote: 'the example does not specify' is important.
5
Reflect on the centrality of the testimony
Consider why the testimony alone is highlighted as sufficient. How does this shape our understanding of the pillars?
This connects to the learning objective.

Commentary
This passage provides an example from the poem and commentary explaining the ruling on one who leaves prayer and the other pillars. It begins by presenting a line of poetry: 'The example of this: hajj, prayer, likewise fasting, so understand, and zakat.' The commentary explains that this is an instance of applying particulars (the specific pillars) to the universal rule. The demonstrative pronoun in 'this example' refers back to 'action' (i.e., the general acts of worship). The author (of the poem, al-Laqqani) omitted the utterance of the two testimonies from the list of the five pillars. The commentator justifies this omission because the testimony has already been discussed earlier in terms of its role in iman; here the focus is on the outward actions. However, some might object that it was discussed in the context of iman, not islam, so it is not redundant. The passage then states a key principle: the foundation of islam is submission (outward acknowledgment) to the mentioned obligations (the pillars). For most pillars, this submission is evident in the act itself, but for the testimony, it must be obtained explicitly. The utterance of the testimony implies submission to the message of the Prophet, and by extension, to all other obligations. Therefore, the testimony alone suffices as the foundational acknowledgment. The footnotes clarify that the example does not restrict the rule, that the testimony was previously discussed, and that the verification of obligation is based on outward verbal acknowledgment.

The Increase and Decrease of Faith in the Ash'ari View
The commentator discusses the issue of increase and decrease of faith and the Ash'ari view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position on the increase and decrease of faith and the reasoning behind it, including the linguistic analysis of the key phrase.

Core Idea
Faith increases with obedience and decreases with disobedience.

The question of whether faith can increase or decrease touches on the very nature of faith itself. In this passage, the commentator presents the Ash'ari view that faith does indeed vary with one's obedience, and he explains why this view is given preponderance. We'll read the original Arabic and unpack its meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic matn lines and the following commentary carefully, then read the translation. Let the author's words speak first.
Notice the structure: two lines of matn followed by a prose commentary.
2
Identify the two positions mentioned
The matn mentions a preponderant view that faith increases with obedience, and also mentions alternative views ('and it was said: no, and it was said: no disagreement'). Identify these.
The commentator clarifies that the preponderant view is held by the majority of Ash'aris.
3
Understand the reasoning for preponderance
The commentator explains that giving preponderance to the increase of faith only makes sense if faith can actually increase. The increase is due to an increase in obedience.
Obedience is defined as doing what is commanded and refraining from what is forbidden.
4
Analyze the linguistic structure
Examine the phrase 'bima tazidu ta'atu al-insan'. Break it down: the ba is causative, ma is masdariyya, making the phrase mean 'due to the increase of man's obedience'.
This linguistic analysis reinforces the causal link between obedience and faith increase.
