The Definition of Repentance and Its Essential Pillars
The commentator defines repentance linguistically and legally, and states its three essential pillars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and legal definition of repentance (tawbah) and its three essential pillars, as well as the additional condition for sins involving the rights of others.

Core Idea
True repentance requires three things: abandoning the sin, regret, and resolve never to return.

In this passage, the commentator explains the meaning of 'mutāb' and provides a precise definition of tawbah. He then lays out the three pillars that constitute valid repentance, and adds a crucial fourth condition when the sin involves another person's rights. This lesson will help you distinguish between the essential elements and the additional requirements of sincere repentance.

Guided Path
1
Understand the linguistic and legal meaning of tawbah
Read the first line where the commentator defines 'mutāb' as tawbah, and explains its linguistic meaning (absolute return) and legal meaning (three pillars).
Notice that the legal definition is more specific than the linguistic one.
2
Identify the three pillars of repentance
The passage lists three pillars: abandoning the sin, regret for Allah's sake, and resolve not to return. Pay attention to the examples given for each.
Each pillar is illustrated with a counterexample to show what invalidates repentance.
3
Examine the fourth condition for sins against others
Read the part about sins involving others' rights. Note the difference between Shafi'i and Maliki views on restitution, and what to do if unable to repay.
This condition is not always mentioned in simpler definitions of tawbah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمراد بالمتاب: التوبة، فهو مصدر ميمي بمعنى التوبة؛ وهي لغة مطلق الرجوع وشرعاً ما استجمع ثلاثة أركان : الإقلاع من الذنب، فلا تصح توبة المكاس مثلاً إلا إذا أقلع عن المَكْس . والندم على فعلها لوجه الله تعالى فلا تصح توبة مَنْ لم يندم، أو ندم لغير وجه الله تعالى؛ كأن ندم لأجل مصيبة حصلت له . والعزم على ألا يعود إلى مثلها أبداً ، فلا تصح توبة مَنْ لم يعزم على عدم العود . وهذا إن لم تتعلَّقِ المعصية بالآدمي ؛ فإن تعلقت به فلها شرط رابع : وهو ردُّ الظلامة إلى صاحبها أو تحصيل البراءة منه تفصيلاً عندنا معاشر الشافعية، وأما عند المالكية فيكفي تحصيل البراءة إجمالاً ، وفيه فُسحةٌ، فإن لم يقدر على ذلك بأن كان مُستغرق الذمم فالمطلوب منه الإخلاص، وكثرة التضرع إلى الله لعله يُرضي عنه خصماءه يوم القيامة.
قوله : (العزم على ألا يعود . إلخ) رخص ابن العربي في هذا الركن فقال : يكفي الندم ولا يشترط العزم على ألا يعود، بل التفويض أولى، كما وقع لآدم عليه السلام. ذكره الأمير في الحاشية». انتهى أجهوري.
And what is meant by al-mutāb is tawbah (repentance); it is a verbal noun (maṣdar mīmī) with the meaning of tawbah. It is linguistically absolute return, and legally what comprises three pillars: (1) abandoning the sin, so the repentance of a tax collector, for example, is not valid unless he abandons the tax; (2) regret over having done it for the sake of Allah, so the repentance of one who does not regret is not valid, or who regrets for other than Allah, such as regretting because a calamity befell him; (3) resolve not to return to the like of it ever, so the repentance of one who does not resolve not to return is not valid. And this [applies] if the sin does not involve another human; if it does involve another human, then it has a fourth condition: restoring the injustice to its owner or obtaining release from him in detail according to us, the Shafi'is; as for the Malikis, obtaining release in general is sufficient, and there is some leeway in it. If he is unable to do that, such as when he has overwhelmed many debtors, then what is required of him is sincerity and abundant supplication to Allah, that perhaps He will satisfy his adversaries against him on the Day of Resurrection.
His statement: (resolve not to return etc.) Ibn al-Arabī granted a concession regarding this pillar, saying: regret is sufficient, and resolve not to return is not a condition; rather, delegation (al-tafwīḍ) is better, as happened with Adam (peace be upon him). Mentioned by al-Amīr in the margin. End of al-Ajūrī.
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage defines repentance (tawbah) by first clarifying the word 'mutāb' as a verbal noun meaning tawbah. Linguistically, it means absolute return (al-rujūʿ). Legally, it consists of three pillars (arkān): First, abandoning the sin entirely (al-iqlāʿ), illustrated by a tax collector who must stop taking illegal taxes. Second, regret (al-nadam) for having committed the sin, solely for Allah's sake; regret due to worldly loss is insufficient. Third, firm resolve (al-ʿazm) never to return to that sin again. The commentator then adds a fourth condition for sins involving the rights of others: the sinner must restore what was taken or seek forgiveness from the victim. The Shafi'is require detailed restitution, while the Malikis accept a general release. If the sinner cannot do that (e.g., too many debts), he must be sincere and pray for Allah to reconcile him with his adversaries. A footnote mentions that Ibn al-Arabi considered regret sufficient without the resolve not to return, citing Adam's example, but this is a concession.

Key Terms
متاب
توبة
إقلاع
ندم
عزم
رد الظلامة
تحصيل البراءة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the third pillar of repentance?
A) Abandoning the sin
B) Regret
C) Resolve not to return to the sin
D) Restoring rights

Key Takeaway
Repentance is not just feeling sorry; it requires concrete actions of abandonment, regret, and firm resolve, plus restitution when others' rights are violated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a sin you have committed against another person. Have you sincerely sought to make amends with them, or have you only repented to Allah? What is stopping you from taking that step today?
?
When you feel regret for a sin, is it because you fear Allah's displeasure, or because you suffered some worldly loss? Examine your heart: would you repent if there were no negative consequences for you?
?
The passage mentions 'resolve not to return.' Is there a sin you have resolved to abandon but then fell into again? What does your pattern reveal about the sincerity of your resolve? Pledge now to renew that resolve with a concrete plan.

Repentance Remains Valid Despite Relapse
Commentator asserts that repentance is not invalidated by relapse, refuting Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that a sincere repentance is not undone if the person later commits the same sin, and what this means for the status of the sin.

Core Idea
Repentance once valid stays valid even if you sin again.

One of the common doubts about repentance is whether falling back into sin nullifies it. The Mu'tazila argued that it does, but the Sunni position is that repentance once accepted remains intact. This short passage from the commentary clarifies that the original sin does not return; the new act is a fresh sin requiring renewed repentance.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Start by reading the Arabic text and its literal translation. Notice the key phrase 'la intiqad' (no invalidation).
The Arabic is compact; the commentary unpacks it.
2
Understand the core assertion
The passage states that returning to sin does not invalidate the earlier repentance. The sin that was forgiven does not come back.
This is a categorical denial of the Mu'tazilite view.
3
Contrast with the Mu'tazila position
The Mu'tazila held that repentance is invalidated by relapse, meaning the original sin returns. Appreciate why this difference matters for theological anthropology.
Their view creates a more fragile concept of repentance.
4
Apply the lesson
Reflect on how this teaching offers hope and encourages perpetual repentance without despairing over relapses.
It distinguishes between a valid repentance and a new sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وَلَا انْتِقَاضَ إِنْ يَعُدْ لِلحَالِ أي ولا انتقاض لتوبة التائب الشرعية إن يعد للحال التي كان عليها من التلبس بالذنب، فلا يعود ذنبه الذي تاب منه بعوده له، خلافاً للمعتزلة في قولهم بانتقاض التوبة بعوده للذنب، فيعود ذنبه الذي تاب منه بعوده.
His statement: (And no invalidation if he returns to the state) means: there is no invalidation of the repentant person's legitimate repentance if he returns to the state he was in of being engaged in sin, so his sin from which he repented does not return to him by his return to it, contrary to the Muʿtazila in their saying that repentance is invalidated by returning to the sin, so his sin from which he repented returns to him by his return.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage comments on the second part of the poem line: 'And no invalidation if he returns to the state.' It means that if a person who had repented subsequently commits the same sin again, the original repentance is not rendered invalid. The sin he had repented from does not come back; rather, the new act is a new sin. This contradicts the Muʿtazila, who held that returning to the sin invalidates the previous repentance, effectively bringing back the original sin. The commentator affirms that repentance, when properly made, remains valid.

Misconception: If you commit the same sin again after repenting, your previous repentance was invalid or the original sin comes back.
Correction: This passage teaches that the earlier repentance remains valid; only the new act is a new sin. The original sin does not return.

B) It remains valid

Key Takeaway
A sincere repentance remains valid even if one falls back into the same sin; the new sin does not undo the forgiveness already granted.

Ruling on Denying a Necessarily Known Matter of Religion
States that denying a necessary matter of religion is apostasy punishable by death, not a hadd penalty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ruling for one who denies a matter necessarily known from Islam and why it is considered apostasy rather than a fixed punishment.

Core Idea
Denying a necessary matter of religion is apostasy, not a crime with a fixed penalty.

This lesson presents a critical ruling in Islamic law: what happens when someone rejects a core, universally known teaching of Islam? The poet and commentator clarify that such denial is an act of disbelief (kufr), not a mere legal infraction. This distinction has profound implications for how the community views and treats such a person.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and translation
Read the Arabic verse once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the ruling sink in before looking at the grammar.
2
Identify key terms
Note the terms 'al-ma'lum bil-darura' (necessarily known), 'kufr' (disbelief), and 'hadd' (fixed punishment). Understanding these is essential to grasp the ruling.
3
Grasp the implication
Contrast the ruling with hadd punishments. Why is this not a hadd? Because the denial itself is an act of apostasy, which has its own ruling.
4
Reflect on the grammatical nuance
Notice the extra 'lam' in 'lima'lum'. The commentator explains it strengthens the verb. This is a stylistic point, but the key is the ruling itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حكمُ مَنْ أنكر معلوماً من الدين بالضرورة] ۱۲۸ - وَمَنْ لِمَعْلُومٍ ضَرُورَةٌ جَحَدْ مِنْ دِيْنِنَا يُقْتَلُ كُفْراً لَيْسَ حَدٌ قوله : (وَمَنْ لِمَعْلُومٍ ضَرُورَةٌ جَحَدٌ مِنْ دِيننا يُقْتَلُ كُفْراً لَيْسَ حَدٌ) (من) مبتدأ، و (المعلوم) معمول مقدَّم لـ (جحد) ، واللام زائدةٌ لتقوية العامل فإنه ضعف بالتأخير،
Ruling of one who denies a matter known necessarily from the religion: 128 - And whoever denies something necessarily known from our religion is killed as disbelief (kufr), not as a prescribed punishment (hadd). His saying: 'and whoever denies something necessarily known from our religion is killed as disbelief, not as a prescribed punishment' — (man) is a subject, and (al-ma`lum) is an object presented before (jahada), and the lam is extra to strengthen the agent, for it is weakened by delay.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling for one who denies a matter that is necessarily known from the religion, such as the obligation of prayer or the prohibition of adultery. The verse (line 128 of the poem) states that such a person is to be killed as an act of disbelief (kufr), not as a fixed legal punishment (hadd). The commentary then provides a grammatical analysis: the word 'man' is the subject, 'al-ma`lum' is the object that has been moved before the verb 'jahada' for metrical or emphatic reasons, and the 'lam' in 'lima`lum' is an extra letter to strengthen the verbal force because the verb is delayed in the sentence.

Key Terms
المعلوم بالضرورة
الكفر
الحد
الردة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that denying a necessary matter of religion incurs a hadd punishment (fixed penalty) like other crimes.
Correction: The verse explicitly clarifies that it is killed as kufr (disbelief), not as a hadd. It is an act of apostasy, which has its own ruling.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Denying a necessarily known matter of Islam is apostasy (kufr) punishable by death, not a fixed hadd penalty.

The Prohibition of Backbiting and Six Duties of the Listener
Author defines backbiting and lists six duties for the listener

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of backbiting (al-namimah) according to major scholars and the six obligations incumbent upon anyone who hears a backbiting report.

Core Idea
Backbiting is more than speech; it includes any disclosure of what one dislikes, and the listener bears six duties.

This lesson explores the Islamic ruling on backbiting (al-namimah), a major sin that severs community bonds. The author presents a nuanced definition from al-Ghazali and al-Nawawi, then provides a practical list of six duties for anyone who encounters backbiting. Understanding these duties helps protect oneself from falling into the same sin and upholds the Quranic injunctions against suspicion and spying.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then its translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before interpreting.
Notice the shift from definition to enumeration of duties.
2
Understand the definition of backbiting
Identify the two definitions: the common one (transmitting speech to cause discord) and al-Ghazali's broader one (revealing what one dislikes). Note al-Nawawi's similar definition.
This broader definition includes writing, gestures, etc., even if not a defect.
3
Memorize the six duties
List the six duties in order: not believing, forbidding, hating, not thinking ill, not spying, not repeating. Note each is supported by Quran or hadith.
The duties protect both the listener and the person spoken about.
4
Reflect on the consensus and warning
The passage states backbiting is a major sin by consensus, citing the hadith that a backbiter will not enter Paradise (unless forgiven). Consider the severity.
This reinforces why the six duties are so important.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اجتناب النميمة] قوله: (وَاجْتَنِبْ نَمِيمَهُ) أي: انفرّ منها وتباعد عنها، والأمر في ذلك للوجوب العيني. والنميمة : نقل كلام الناس بعضهم إلى بعض على وجه الإفساد بينهم؛ كقوله : فلان يقول فيك كذا، لكن قال أبو حامد الغزالي وليست النميمة مختصة بذلك؛ بل حدها كشف ما يكره كشفه ؛ سواء كان الكشف بالقول أو بالكتابة أو الرمز أو نحوها، وسواء كان المنقول من الأعمال أو من الأحوال، وسواء كان عيباً أو غيره. قال النووي : فحقيقة النميمة : إفشاء السرّ، وهتك الستر عما يكره كشفه . قال : وكل من حُمِلتُ إليه نميمةٌ لزمه ستة أمور : الأول: ألا يصدِّقَه ؛ لأن النمام فاسقٌ، والفاسق مردود الخبر. الثاني : أن ينهاه عن ذلك، وينصحه · الثالثُ : أن يُبغضه فإنه بغيض عند الله ، ويجب بغض من أبغضه الله تعالى. الرابع : ألا يظنَّ بالمنقول عنه السوء؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿اجْتَنِبُوا كَثِيرًا مِّنَ الظَّنِّ إِنَّ بَعْضَ الظَّنِّ إِثْمٌ﴾ [الحجرات: ١٢]. الخامس : ألا يحمله ما حكى له على التجسس والبحث عن تحقيق ذلك، قال تعالى: ﴿وَلَا تَجَسَّسُوا﴾ [الحجرات: ١٢]. السادس : ألا يحكي نميمة عنه فيقول : فلان حكى لي كذا، فيصير بذلك نماماً . والنميمة محرمة بالإجماع، والمذاهب متفقة على أنها كبيرة، لحديث «الصحيحين»: لا يدخل الجنةَ نمام»، وفي رواية مسلم: «فَتَّات» بتاءين أولاهما مشددة؛ أي : نمام، من (قَتَّ الحديثَ) نمَّه والمراد : لا يدخلها مع السابقين، إلا إن غُفر له.
Avoidance of backbiting: His saying: 'And avoid backbiting' (wa-ijtanib namimahu) means: separate yourself from it and keep away from it, and the command here is for individual obligation. Backbiting (al-namimah) is the transmission of people's speech among themselves with the aim of causing corruption between them; like saying: 'So-and-so says such-and-such about you.' But Abu Hamid al-Ghazali said: Backbiting is not limited to that; rather its definition is revealing what one dislikes to have revealed, whether the revelation is by speech, writing, gesture, or the like, and whether what is transmitted is actions or states, and whether it is a defect or not. Al-Nawawi said: The reality of backbiting is disclosing secrets and tearing the veil of what one dislikes to have revealed. He said: Whoever is brought a backbiting report—six things are incumbent upon him: First: not to believe him, because the backbiter is a sinner, and a sinner's report is rejected. Second: to forbid him from that and advise him. Third: to hate him, for he is hateful before Allah, and it is obligatory to hate those whom Allah hates. Fourth: not to think ill of the person reported about, due to the saying of the Exalted: 'Avoid much suspicion; indeed some suspicion is sin' (Quran 49:12). Fifth: not to let what was told to him lead him to spy and investigate the truth of that, as the Exalted said: 'And do not spy' (Quran 49:12). Sixth: not to relate the backbiting from him by saying, 'So-and-so told me such-and-such,' for thereby he becomes a backbiter himself. Backbiting is forbidden by consensus, and the schools agree that it is a major sin, due to the hadith in the Two Sahihs: 'A backbiter will not enter Paradise.' And in a narration of Muslim: 'qattat'—with two tā's, the first shaddah—meaning backbiter, from 'qatta al-hadith' i.e., spread it. The intended meaning is: he will not enter it with the foremost, unless he is forgiven.
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage explains the prohibition of backbiting (al-namimah) and the obligations of the listener. The author begins by glossing the poem's line 'And avoid backbiting', stating the command is for individual obligation (wujub 'ayni). He then defines backbiting in two ways: the common understanding—transmitting speech to cause discord—and al-Ghazali's broader definition: revealing anything the person dislikes to be revealed, regardless of method or type. Al-Nawawi's definition is similar: disclosing secrets and tearing veils. The author then lists six duties upon one who hears backbiting: (1) Do not believe the backbiter, as he is sinful and his report is rejected (based on Quran 49:6). (2) Forbid him and advise him. (3) Hate him for Allah's sake, as he is hateful to Allah. (4) Do not think ill of the person spoken about, avoiding suspicion (Quran 49:12). (5) Do not spy or investigate to verify the report (Quran 49:12). (6) Do not repeat the backbiting to others, lest you become a backbiter yourself. Finally, the author affirms that backbiting is a major sin by consensus, citing the hadith that a backbiter will not enter Paradise, meaning not with the first ones unless forgiven.

Key Terms
النميمة
الغيبة
الفسق
الكبائر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Backbiting only refers to speaking about someone's defects.
Correction: Al-Ghazali defines backbiting as revealing anything the person dislikes to be revealed, whether by speech, writing, gesture, etc., and whether it is a defect or not.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ghazali's definition, does backbiting include revealing something about a person's actions that is not a defect, if the person dislikes it being revealed?
A) Yes, because the definition includes any disclosure of what one dislikes.
B) No, only defects are considered backbiting.
C) Yes, but only if it causes corruption.
D) No, only speech is backbiting.

Key Takeaway
Backbiting is a major sin, and upon hearing it, a Muslim must not believe, must forbid, must hate the act, must not suspect the victim, must not spy, and must not repeat it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
Reflect on a time you suspected someone of something based on a report. Did you investigate or spy to confirm it? How does the command to avoid suspicion and spying (Quran 49:12) challenge your natural curiosity, and what step will you take to obey it?

The Three Cures for Pride
The author enumerates three cures for pride and describes the humble person.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational, religious, and customary cures for pride and what true humility entails.

Core Idea
Pride is cured by knowing our lowliness, fearing God's warning, and reflecting on our origins.

This passage from Tuhfat al-Murid presents three practical cures for the deadly sin of pride. It combines intellectual insight, spiritual fear, and self-reflection to uproot arrogance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author structures three distinct cures.
The rational cure comes first, then religious, then customary.
2
Identify each cure
For each cure, note the key reasoning: rational (divine power and inherent lowliness), religious (warning because it is God's attribute), customary (lowly origin and wealth).
The customary cure includes reflecting on one's birth and bodily functions.
3
Understand the description of the humble person
After the three cures, the author describes the humble person as one who knows truth, sees all as God's favor, and does not disdain anything.
4
Ask yourself: how do these cures work together?
Reflect on how each cure targets a different aspect of pride: mind, heart, and ego.
This integrates the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وله دواء عقلي وشرعي وعادي :
أما العقليُّ فأَنْ يعلم بأن التأثير الله، وأنه لا يملك لنفسه ولا لغيره نفعاً ولا ضراً؛ فلا ينبغي لعاقل أن يتكبر، فإنه قد استوى القوي والضعيف والرفيع والوضيع في الذل الذاتي؛ وقد قيل لسيد الكائنات : لَيْسَ لَكَ مِنَ الْأَمْرِ شَيْء [آل عمران: ۱۲۸]. وأما الشرعى فهو الوعيد الوارد فيه لكونه صفة الربِّ، من نازعه فيها أهلكه الكائنات لخروجه على سيدها، فيستثقل ظاهراً وباطناً، كما وغارت عليه جميع هو مشاهد.
وأما العادي فأنْ ينظر لأصله وماله وتقلباته، فإن أصله نطفة قذرة أصلها من دم، وأقام مُدَّةً وسط القاذورات من دم حيض وغيره، ومُدَّةٌ يبول على نفسه ويتغوط، ثم هو الآن جسمه ، محشو بقاذورات لا تحصى ويباشر العَذِرة بيده كذا كذا مرَّةً، يغسلها عن وماله جيفةٌ منتنة . فمن تأمل صفات نفسه، عرف مقداره.
والمتواضع من عرف الحق، ورأى جميع ما معه من فضل الله، ولا يحقر شيئاً في مملكة سيده ويسأله دوام ما تفضل به عليه
ومحل كون الكبر حراماً إذا كان على عباد الله الصالحين وأئمة المسلمين وهو حينئذ من الكبائر ومن أعظم الذنوب القلبية. وأما إذا كان على أعداء الله فهو مطلوب شرعاً حسن عقلاً. والمراد بالكبر عليهم احتقارهم لأجل كفرهم ومعصيتهم، لا احتقار ذاتهم.
And it has a rational, religious, and customary cure.
As for the rational: that he should know that effective power belongs to God, and that he does not own for himself or for others any benefit or harm. So it is not fitting for an intelligent person to be proud, for the strong and the weak, the high and the low have all become equal in inherent lowliness (al-dhull al-dhātī). Indeed, it was said to the master of all beings (the Prophet): 'You have no authority in the matter' (Qur'an 3:128). As for the religious: it is the warning (al-waʿīd) that has come concerning it, because it is an attribute of the Lord; whoever disputes with Him over it, the creatures destroy him because of his rebellion against their Master, so he becomes heavy outwardly and inwardly, as everything resents him and he is witnessed.
As for the customary: that he should look to his origin, his wealth, and his vicissitudes. For his origin is a filthy drop whose origin is from blood, and he stayed for a period amid filths of menstrual blood and other things, and a period during which he urinates and defecates upon himself, and now his body is filled with countless filths, and he handles excrement with his hand so many times, washing it from his body, and his wealth is a stinking carcass. Whoever contemplates his own attributes knows his measure.
And the humble person is one who knows the truth, and sees all that he has from God's favor, does not disdain anything in the kingdom of his Master, and asks Him for the continuance of what He has bestowed upon him.
And the ruling on pride: it is forbidden (ḥarām) if it is directed against the righteous servants of God and the leaders of the Muslims, and in that case it is among the major sins (kabāʾir) and the greatest of heart sins. But if it is directed against the enemies of God, it is required by law (sharʿan) and good rationally. What is meant by pride against them is holding them in contempt on account of their disbelief and disobedience, not holding their very selves in contempt.
Source pages p. 414

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of pride (kibr) by presenting three cures — rational, religious, and customary — and then delineates when pride is forbidden versus praiseworthy. The rational cure involves recognizing that only God has true power and that all creatures are equal in their inherent lowliness; even the Prophet was told he has no authority in the matter. The religious cure involves reflecting on the severe warning (waʿīd) against pride because it is an attribute of God, and anyone who disputes with God over it will be destroyed by all of creation. The customary cure involves contemplating one's own lowly origin (from a drop of fluid, dwelling in filth, handling excrement) and the fact that one's wealth is like a stinking carcass. True humility comes from knowing the truth, recognizing all blessings as from God, and not disdaining anything in His kingdom. The author then clarifies that pride is only forbidden when directed against the righteous believers and Muslim leaders; but pride against God's enemies (i.e., despising them for their disbelief) is required by the law and rational. This nuance guards against the misunderstanding that all forms of pride are sinful.

Key Terms
دواء عقلي (rational cure)
دواء شرعي (religious cure)
دواء عادي (customary cure)
ذل ذاتي (inherent lowliness)
وعيد (threat/warning)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that intellectual reflection alone is enough to cure pride.
Correction: The author includes religious and customary dimensions, showing that a comprehensive approach is needed.

Checkpoint
According to the rational cure for pride (الدواء العقلي), why should one not be proud?
A) Because pride leads to Hellfire
B) Because all creatures are equal in their inherent lowliness and God alone has power
C) Because the Prophet was humble
D) Because wealth is temporary

Key Takeaway
Humility comes from knowing God's power, fearing His warning, and reflecting on one's own lowliness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent moment when you felt proud of an achievement or status. Which of the three cures—rational, religious, or customary—would have most effectively humbled you in that moment? What specific thought from this lesson would you need to hold onto to prevent that pride from taking root?
?
The source says that the humble person 'does not disdain anything in the kingdom of his Master.' Is there any person, animal, or thing that you secretly look down on? If so, how does seeing it as part of God's kingdom change your attitude? What concrete step will you take this week to honor that which you have disdained?
?
Re-read the part about the customary cure: 'his origin is a filthy drop... his wealth is a stinking carcass.' When you consider your own body and your possessions, does this reflection make you feel disgust or gratitude? How can you use this genuine self-awareness to deepen your humility without falling into self-hatred?

intermediate

When Pride is Blameworthy or Praiseworthy
The author distinguishes between forbidden pride against believers and required pride against disbelievers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that pride is only forbidden when directed against the righteous, but required when directed against the enemies of God.

Core Idea
Pride against Allah's enemies is a duty, not a sin.

After presenting the cures for pride, the author clarifies a crucial distinction: pride is not always blameworthy. It is a major sin when directed at the believers, but it becomes an obligation when directed at God's enemies out of contempt for their disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the ruling passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the final part of the source. Pay attention to the two conditions.
The passage starts with 'ومحل كون الكبر حراماً'.
2
Identify the two categories
Find where the author describes forbidden pride (against God's righteous servants and leaders) and required pride (against God's enemies).
3
Understand the basis for contempt
Note that contempt for enemies is due to their disbelief and disobedience, not their inherent selves.
This prevents misunderstanding that all contempt is sinful.
4
Reflect on the wisdom
Consider why the law requires pride against disbelievers: to uphold the honor of faith and discourage imitation of evildoers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وله دواء عقلي وشرعي وعادي :
أما العقليُّ فأَنْ يعلم بأن التأثير الله، وأنه لا يملك لنفسه ولا لغيره نفعاً ولا ضراً؛ فلا ينبغي لعاقل أن يتكبر، فإنه قد استوى القوي والضعيف والرفيع والوضيع في الذل الذاتي؛ وقد قيل لسيد الكائنات : لَيْسَ لَكَ مِنَ الْأَمْرِ شَيْء [آل عمران: ۱۲۸]. وأما الشرعى فهو الوعيد الوارد فيه لكونه صفة الربِّ، من نازعه فيها أهلكه الكائنات لخروجه على سيدها، فيستثقل ظاهراً وباطناً، كما وغارت عليه جميع هو مشاهد.
وأما العادي فأنْ ينظر لأصله وماله وتقلباته، فإن أصله نطفة قذرة أصلها من دم، وأقام مُدَّةً وسط القاذورات من دم حيض وغيره، ومُدَّةٌ يبول على نفسه ويتغوط، ثم هو الآن جسمه ، محشو بقاذورات لا تحصى ويباشر العَذِرة بيده كذا كذا مرَّةً، يغسلها عن وماله جيفةٌ منتنة . فمن تأمل صفات نفسه، عرف مقداره.
والمتواضع من عرف الحق، ورأى جميع ما معه من فضل الله، ولا يحقر شيئاً في مملكة سيده ويسأله دوام ما تفضل به عليه
ومحل كون الكبر حراماً إذا كان على عباد الله الصالحين وأئمة المسلمين وهو حينئذ من الكبائر ومن أعظم الذنوب القلبية. وأما إذا كان على أعداء الله فهو مطلوب شرعاً حسن عقلاً. والمراد بالكبر عليهم احتقارهم لأجل كفرهم ومعصيتهم، لا احتقار ذاتهم.
And it has a rational, religious, and customary cure.
As for the rational: that he should know that effective power belongs to God, and that he does not own for himself or for others any benefit or harm. So it is not fitting for an intelligent person to be proud, for the strong and the weak, the high and the low have all become equal in inherent lowliness (al-dhull al-dhātī). Indeed, it was said to the master of all beings (the Prophet): 'You have no authority in the matter' (Qur'an 3:128). As for the religious: it is the warning (al-waʿīd) that has come concerning it, because it is an attribute of the Lord; whoever disputes with Him over it, the creatures destroy him because of his rebellion against their Master, so he becomes heavy outwardly and inwardly, as everything resents him and he is witnessed.
As for the customary: that he should look to his origin, his wealth, and his vicissitudes. For his origin is a filthy drop whose origin is from blood, and he stayed for a period amid filths of menstrual blood and other things, and a period during which he urinates and defecates upon himself, and now his body is filled with countless filths, and he handles excrement with his hand so many times, washing it from his body, and his wealth is a stinking carcass. Whoever contemplates his own attributes knows his measure.
And the humble person is one who knows the truth, and sees all that he has from God's favor, does not disdain anything in the kingdom of his Master, and asks Him for the continuance of what He has bestowed upon him.
And the ruling on pride: it is forbidden (ḥarām) if it is directed against the righteous servants of God and the leaders of the Muslims, and in that case it is among the major sins (kabāʾir) and the greatest of heart sins. But if it is directed against the enemies of God, it is required by law (sharʿan) and good rationally. What is meant by pride against them is holding them in contempt on account of their disbelief and disobedience, not holding their very selves in contempt.
Source pages p. 414

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of pride (kibr) by presenting three cures — rational, religious, and customary — and then delineates when pride is forbidden versus praiseworthy. The rational cure involves recognizing that only God has true power and that all creatures are equal in their inherent lowliness; even the Prophet was told he has no authority in the matter. The religious cure involves reflecting on the severe warning (waʿīd) against pride because it is an attribute of God, and anyone who disputes with God over it will be destroyed by all of creation. The customary cure involves contemplating one's own lowly origin (from a drop of fluid, dwelling in filth, handling excrement) and the fact that one's wealth is like a stinking carcass. True humility comes from knowing the truth, recognizing all blessings as from God, and not disdaining anything in His kingdom. The author then clarifies that pride is only forbidden when directed against the righteous believers and Muslim leaders; but pride against God's enemies (i.e., despising them for their disbelief) is required by the law and rational. This nuance guards against the misunderstanding that all forms of pride are sinful.

Key Terms
كبائر (major sins)
ذنوب قلبية (heart sins)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think all pride is sinful.
Correction: The author shows that pride against God's enemies is required and praiseworthy.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is pride considered a major sin (من الكبائر)?
A) When it is against the righteous servants and leaders of the Muslims
B) When it is against the enemies of God
C) When it is due to wealth
D) When it is based on lineage

Key Takeaway
Pride is forbidden against believers but required against disbelievers because of their disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt a sense of superiority over a fellow believer? Does this passage convict you that such pride is a greatest heart sin? What concrete step will you take today to repent and see that person as Allah does?
?
When you encounter a disbeliever, do you examine your heart? Is your contempt for their disbelief or for them as a person? Ask Allah to purify your intentions and align them with the precise ruling here.
?
This lesson demands a heart check: Is there any believer you look down on? Will you actively ask Allah to remove that pride and replace it with love and humility?

The Sin of Envy: Definition, Prohibition, and Cure
Defines envy, distinguishes it, proves its prohibition, and gives its cure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of envy (hasad), how it differs from ghibtah, its prohibition based on the Qur'an, Sunnah, and consensus, its harmful effects, and its cure.

Core Idea
Envy is the desire for another's blessing to be removed — it is a spiritual disease that consumes good deeds.

Envy is not merely disliking someone else's good fortune; it is an active wish for their blessing to vanish. The author clarifies this definition, draws a clear line between blameworthy envy and praiseworthy competition, and then establishes the severe prohibition of envy from divine sources. Finally, he prescribes a profound cure: recognizing envy as bad manners with Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own words set the stage.
Note the poetic line at the end, which encapsulates the cure.
2
Identify the definition and distinction
Find the definition of hasad and how it differs from ghibtah. Underline what makes hasad the 'vilest of the vile'.
Hasad is wishing for removal; ghibtah is wishing for a similar blessing.
3
Examine the prohibition and harms
List the three proofs of prohibition (Qur'an, Sunnah, ijma). Then distinguish between non-acquired harm (evil eye) and acquired harm (active attempts to harm).
The hadith compares envy to fire consuming wood.
4
Understand the cure
Reflect on how contemplating the warning (wa'id) and realizing that envy is bad manners with Allah serves as the cure. Consider the poetry line as a reminder.
The cure is spiritual: realign one's attitude toward Allah's decree.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَدَاءِ الحَسَدِ) أي : داء هو الحسد ; فالإضافة للبيان، هذا إن أريد الداء المعنوي، فإن أُريد الداء الحسي كان من إضافة المشبه به للمشبه; أي: الحسد الشبيه بالداء، وهو تمني زوال نعمة الغير ; سواء تمناها لنفسه أو لا، بأن تمنى انتقالها عن غيره لغيره، وهذا أخس الأخساء; لأنه باع آخرته بدنيا غيره ; بخلاف ما إذا تمنى مثل نعمة الغير، فإنه غِبْطَةٌ محمودة في الخير، كما ورد : لا حسد إلا في اثنتين ... (۱) الحديث .
و دليل تحريمه الكتاب والسنة والإجماعُ، قال تعالى: ﴿وَمِن شَرِّ حَاسِدٍ إِذَا حَسَدَ [الفلق ، وشره كثير، فمنه ما هو غير مكتسب، وهو إصابة العين ومنه ما هو مكتسب; كسعيه في تعطيل الخير عنه، وتنقيصه عند الناس، وربما دعا عليه أو بطش به، إلى غير ذلك. وقال : إياكم والحسد; فإنَّ الحسد يأكل الحسنات، كما تأكل النار الحطب أو العُشْبَ» (۳).
ودواء الحسد النظر للوعيد مع أنه إساءة أدب مع الله تعالى; كأنه لا يُسلَّمُ له حكمه، ولذلك قال بعضهم : [من المتقارب] أَلا قُلْ لِمَنْ باتَ لي حاسداً : أَتَدْرِي عَلَى مَنْ أَسَأْتَ الأَدَبْ؟
His statement: 'and the disease of envy (ḥasad)' means: a disease which is envy; the genitive construction is for specification. This is if the spiritual disease is intended. However, if the physical disease is intended, then it is a metaphorical genitive: i.e., envy which resembles a disease. It is the desire for the removal of a blessing from another, whether one desires it for oneself or not, such that one wishes it to transfer from him to someone else. This is the vilest of the vile, because he sells his afterlife for the worldly life of another. This is unlike the case where one desires a similar blessing as another — that is ghibṭah (admirable competition) which is praiseworthy in good things, as reported: 'There is no envy except in two [cases]' — the ḥadīth.

The proof of its prohibition is the Book, the Sunna, and consensus. Allah said: 'And from the evil of an envier when he envies' (Qur'an 113:5). Its evil is great. Some of it is non-acquired (ghayr muktasab), which is the evil eye (iṣābat al-ʿayn); and some of it is acquired (muktasab), such as his striving to nullify good from him, belittling him in people's eyes, and perhaps supplicating against him or harming him, and so on. And he (the Prophet) said: 'Beware of envy, for envy consumes good deeds just as fire consumes wood or grass.'

The cure for envy is to contemplate the warning (waʿīd), and that it is bad manners (sūʾ al-adab) with Allah Almighty, as if one does not submit to His decree. Therefore some said (in poetry): 'Say to the one who spent the night envying me: Do you know against Whom you have shown bad manners?'
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage defines envy (ḥasad) and distinguishes it from permissible competition (ghibṭah). The author explains that envy is the wish for the removal of a blessing from someone else, whether or not one wants that blessing for oneself. This is the lowest of low traits because it trades one's afterlife for another person's worldly gain. In contrast, desiring a similar blessing for oneself (ghibṭah) is praiseworthy, as indicated by the hadith that there is no (blameworthy) envy except in two cases (of good deeds). The prohibition of envy is established by the Qur'an, Sunna, and consensus. The harms of envy include both non-acquired harm (like the evil eye) and acquired harm (like actively trying to deprive others). The cure is to reflect on the severe threat and to realize that envy is bad manners with Allah, as it implies discontent with His distribution of blessings. The poetic line reinforces this by asking the envier: against Whom are you showing poor etiquette?

Key Terms
حسد (envy)
غبطة (admirable competition)
إصابة العين (evil eye)
مكتسب (acquired harm)
غير مكتسب (non-acquired harm)
وعيد (warning)
إساءة أدب (bad manners)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Envy is the same as wanting what someone else has (jealousy).
Correction: Envy (hasad) is specifically the desire for the removal of the blessing from the other person, not merely wanting it for oneself. Wanting the same blessing for oneself is ghibtah and is praiseworthy.

Checkpoint
What is the definition of hasad (envy) according to the passage?
A) Wishing for the same blessing as another
B) Wishing for the removal of a blessing from another, regardless of wanting it for oneself
C) Disliking that another has a blessing
D) Being pleased when a blessing is taken away

Key Takeaway
Envy is a spiritual disease that is strictly prohibited; its cure lies in recognizing it as bad manners with Allah and reflecting on the divine warning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see someone blessed with something you desire, does your heart immediately wish for their loss before you even think of asking Allah for the same? Reflect on the state of your heart—are you content with Allah's distribution?
?
You know that envy is bad manners with Allah. But when you envy someone, are you not, in that moment, implying that Allah has made a mistake in giving that blessing? How will you train yourself to submit to His decree when envy stirs?
?
The Prophet said envy consumes good deeds like fire consumes wood. Think of a recent moment of envy. Can you identify the good deeds it has cost you? Write down one specific action you will take today to uproot that envy from your heart.

The Meaning of 'This' and Rhetorical Transition (Takhallus)
Commentator explains 'this' and introduces takhallus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator explains the word 'this' and introduces the rhetorical device of takhallus to transition between topics.

Core Idea
Rhetorical transitions connect related themes while maintaining coherence.

The commentator begins by clarifying the referent of the word 'this' in the poem. He then highlights a subtle rhetorical move called takhallus, which smoothly shifts from the previous subject (following the Salaf) to the new one (hope and sincerity). Understanding this transition helps us appreciate the literary structure of the poem.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the commentator first explains 'this' as referring to the entire poem being the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunnah.
2
Identify the rhetorical transition
Look for the term 'تخلص' (takhallus). The commentator defines it as moving from one purpose to another. See how the two purposes are related: avoiding innovation and hoping for sincerity.
3
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why does the commentator bother to explain this transition? It shows that the poem is not a random collection but a carefully structured argument.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (هذا) مفعول المحذوف؛ أي: افهم هذا، أو مبتدأ والخبر محذوف، والتقدير هذا الذي ذكرته في هذه المنظومة مذهب أهل السنة، أو نحو ذلك. وهذا من باب التخلص ؛ وهو الانتقال من غَرَض - وهو هنا الأمر بمتابعة السلف الصالح، ومجانبة البدعة ممن خلف - إلى غرض آخر - وهو هنا رجاء الإخلاص، وما ذكر بعده - وبين الغرضين تناسب قوله : (هَذَا وَأَرْجُو الله الرجاء بالمد : هو تعلُّق القلب بمرغوب فيه مع الأخذ في الأسباب، وإلا فهو طمع مذموم. قال ابن الجوزي: مثل الراجي مع الإصرار على المعصية، كمثل من رجا حصاداً وما زرع أو ولداً وما نكح ، وقال عبد الله بن المبارك: [من البسيط] مَا بَالُ دِينِكَ تَرْضَى أَنْ تُدَنْسَهُ وثَوْبُكَ الدَّهْرَ مَنْسُولٌ مِنَ الدَّنَسِ؟ تَرْجُو النَّجَاةَ وَلَمْ تَسْلُكْ طَرِيْقَتَها إِنَّ السَّفِينَةَ لَا تَجْرِي عَلَى البَبَسِ. وفي الحديث القدسي : مَا أَقلَّ حياءَ مَنْ يَطْمَعُ في جَنَّتي بغير عَمَلٍ، كيف أجودُ برحمتي على مَنْ بخل بِطَاعَتِي؟». قوله : (في الإِخْلاصِ أي في اتصافي به ؛ وهو قصد الله بالعبادة وحده، وهو سبب للخلاص من أهوال يوم القيامة، وهو واجب عيني على كل مُكلَّف في جميع الطاعات. قال تعالى: ﴿وَمَا أُمِرُوا إِلَّا لِيَعْبُدُوا اللهَ مُخْلِصِينَ لَهُ الذِينَ) (البيئة: دا [؟]، وقال : إِنَّ اللهَ لا يقبلُ مِنَ العمل إلَّا ما كان خالصاً، وما ابتغي به وجهه (۱) ، وفي حديث أنس رضي الله تعالى عنه : قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «مَنْ فارق الدنيا على الإخلاص لله وحده لا شريك له، وإقام الصلاة، وإيتاء الزكاة.. فارقها والله عنه راض (۲) وعن ثوبان قال : سمعت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يقول : طُوبَى للمخلصين، أولئك مصابيح الهدى تنجلي عنهم كلُّ فتنة ظلماء (۳)، وفي رواية: (قتماء)، وهي بمعنى ظلماء. وممَّا يعين على الإخلاص استحضار أن ما سوى الله لا شيء بيده، وأن كل شيء بيد الله تعالى، والصادق في إخلاصه لا يحبُّ اطلاع الناس على حُسْنِ عمله، ولا يكره أن يَطَّلِعَ الناس على سيئ عمله ولا يبالي بخروج قَدْره من قلوب الخلق، ورؤي بعضهم في المنام بعد الموت يقول : (الجنَّة أرضُها الإيمان، وشجرها الأعمال، وثمرُها الإخلاص). قوله : (مِنَ الرِّيَاءِ) بالمد؛ أي: بدله فـ (من) للبدل على حد قوله تعالى : أَرَضِيتُم بِالْحَيَوةِ الدُّنْيَا مِنَ الْآخِرَةُ التّوبَة : ۱۳۸؛ أي: بَدَلَها، وليست للتعدية؛ لأنه لم يُعبر بالخلاص أو الخلوص، بل عبر بالإخلاص. والرياء أن يعمل القُرية ليراه الناس . وأما التَّسْمِيعُ : فهو أن يعمل العمل وحده، ثم يُخبر به الناس لأجل تعظيمهم له،
His statement: 'This' is the object of an omitted verb; i.e., understand this, or it is a subject with a missing predicate, and the estimation is: 'This which I have mentioned in this poem is the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunnah,' or something similar. And this is from the category of takhallus (transition); it is the transition from one purpose – and it is here the command to follow the righteous Salaf and avoid innovation from those who came after – to another purpose – and it is here hope for sincerity, and what is mentioned after it – and between the two purposes there is appropriateness. His statement: 'and from my side I hope in Allah for sincerity.' Al-raja' (hope) with prolongation: it is the attachment of the heart to something desired while taking the means; otherwise it is blameworthy greed. Ibn al-Jawzi said: The example of the one who hopes while persisting in disobedience is like one who hopes for a harvest without planting, or a child without marrying. And 'Abdullah ibn al-Mubarak said [in al-basit meter]: 'Why is it that you are content for your religion to be stained, while your garment is constantly washed from dirt? You hope for salvation but do not follow its path; the ship does not sail on dry land.' And in the hadith qudsi: 'How little shame has the one who hopes for my Garden without work! How can I bestow my mercy on one who is stingy with obedience to me?' His statement: 'in sincerity' means in my being characterized by it; and it is intending Allah alone in worship, and it is the cause of deliverance from the terrors of the Day of Resurrection, and it is an individual obligation on every legally responsible person in all acts of obedience. He said, exalted: 'And they were not commanded except to worship Allah, sincere to Him in religion' (al-Bayyinah: 5). And he said: 'Indeed Allah does not accept from action except what is pure and what is sought by His Face.' And in the hadith of Anas, may Allah be pleased with him: The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: 'Whoever leaves the world upon sincerity to Allah alone, with no partner, establishing prayer, and giving zakat – he leaves it while Allah is pleased with him.' And from Thawban: I heard the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, say: 'Good news to the sincere ones; they are lamps of guidance; every dark fitnah will be dispelled from them.' And in another narration: (قتماء) which means dark. Among the things that help sincerity is realizing that other than Allah has nothing in its hand, and that everything is in the hand of Allah, the Exalted. The truthful in his sincerity does not love that people see his good deed, nor does he dislike that people see his bad deed, nor does he care about the loss of his status from the hearts of creation. And one was seen in a dream after death saying: 'Paradise – its earth is faith, its trees are deeds, and its fruit is sincerity.' His statement: 'from ar-riya'' with prolongation; i.e., instead of it, so 'min' is for substitution, like His statement, exalted: 'Are you satisfied with the life of this world instead of the Hereafter?' (al-Tawbah: 38); i.e., instead of it, and it is not for transitivity, because he did not use 'khalaas' (deliverance) or 'khuluus' (purity), but rather 'ikhlaas' (sincerity). And ar-riya' (show-off) is that one does the good deed so that people see him. And at-tasmee' (hearing-seeking) is that he does the deed alone and then informs people of it for the sake of their glorification of him.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the line of poetry: 'هذا وأرجو الله في الإخلاص من الرياء'. The commentator first explains the word 'هذا' (this) as referring to the entire poetic composition being the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunnah. He notes that this is a rhetorical transition (takhallus) from the previous subject (following the Salaf and avoiding innovation) to the new subject (hope for sincerity and avoiding riya'), and there is an appropriate connection between them. Then he defines 'الرجاء' (hope) as the heart's attachment to something desired while taking the means; if one hopes without effort, it is blameworthy greed. He quotes Ibn al-Jawzi and 'Abdullah ibn al-Mubarak to illustrate that hope without action is futile. A hadith qudsi reinforces that hoping for Paradise without obedience is shameless. Next, he explains 'الإخلاص' (sincerity) as intending Allah alone in worship, which is an individual obligation in all acts of obedience. He supports this with Quranic verses and hadith, including a hadith that those who die with sincerity are pleasing to Allah, and a hadith that the sincere are lamps of guidance in darkness. He lists aids to sincerity: meditating that all things are in Allah's hand, not caring about people's opinions, and not seeking recognition. Finally, he explains 'مِنَ الرِّيَاءِ' (from riya') by parsing 'min' as substitutional, meaning 'instead of riya''. He defines riya' as doing good deeds to be seen by people, and tashmi' (hearing-seeking) as doing a deed alone then telling people about it for their glorification. The commentator distinguishes sincerity from mere deliverance or purity, emphasizing that it is a specific state of devotion.

Key Terms
تخلص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what does the word 'this' (هذا) refer to?
A) The previous line of poetry
B) The entire poem as the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunnah
C) The concept of sincerity
D) The act of avoiding innovation

Key Takeaway
The commentator begins by grounding the poem's claim as the creed of Ahl al-Sunnah and highlights a rhetorical transition to the next topic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The commentator states that the poem's message is that following the Salaf and avoiding innovation is the way. Do you truly consider the Salaf as your guide in every aspect of your life, or have you adopted innovations without realizing? Be honest: what is one area where you have compromised?
?
The takhallus in this poem moves from the command to avoid innovation to the hope for sincerity. Is your own life a smooth transition from avoiding sin to actively seeking sincerity, or do you stop at avoidance without moving to positive action? What step can you take today to move from avoidance to sincere worship?
?
The appropriateness between the two purposes shows that sincerity is part of the Salaf's way. Are you seeking sincerity in your actions, or are you content with outward conformity? What specific act of worship do you need to purify your intention in right now?

Hope (Raja'): Definition and Conditions
Commentator defines hope (raja') and warns against hope without action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will distinguish between proper hope (with effort) and blameworthy wishful thinking, based on the commentator's explanation and supporting proofs.

Core Idea
True hope requires action; hope without effort is mere greed.

After clarifying the rhetorical transition, the commentator defines hope (raja') as the heart's attachment to something desired while taking the necessary means. He warns that hope without action is blameworthy, citing scholars and a hadith qudsi to emphasize that expecting Paradise without obedience is shameless.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of hope
Read the Arabic and translation for the definition of 'الرجاء'. Note the key condition: 'مع الأخذ في الأسباب' (while taking the means).
2
Study the supporting quotes
Read Ibn al-Jawzi's analogy, Ibn al-Mubarak's verse, and the hadith qudsi. These all reinforce the same point: hope must be accompanied by action.
3
Reflect on the contrast
Contrast proper hope with 'طمع مذموم' (blameworthy greed). Understand why hope without effort is criticized.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (هذا) مفعول المحذوف؛ أي: افهم هذا، أو مبتدأ والخبر محذوف، والتقدير هذا الذي ذكرته في هذه المنظومة مذهب أهل السنة، أو نحو ذلك. وهذا من باب التخلص ؛ وهو الانتقال من غَرَض - وهو هنا الأمر بمتابعة السلف الصالح، ومجانبة البدعة ممن خلف - إلى غرض آخر - وهو هنا رجاء الإخلاص، وما ذكر بعده - وبين الغرضين تناسب قوله : (هَذَا وَأَرْجُو الله الرجاء بالمد : هو تعلُّق القلب بمرغوب فيه مع الأخذ في الأسباب، وإلا فهو طمع مذموم. قال ابن الجوزي: مثل الراجي مع الإصرار على المعصية، كمثل من رجا حصاداً وما زرع أو ولداً وما نكح ، وقال عبد الله بن المبارك: [من البسيط] مَا بَالُ دِينِكَ تَرْضَى أَنْ تُدَنْسَهُ وثَوْبُكَ الدَّهْرَ مَنْسُولٌ مِنَ الدَّنَسِ؟ تَرْجُو النَّجَاةَ وَلَمْ تَسْلُكْ طَرِيْقَتَها إِنَّ السَّفِينَةَ لَا تَجْرِي عَلَى البَبَسِ. وفي الحديث القدسي : مَا أَقلَّ حياءَ مَنْ يَطْمَعُ في جَنَّتي بغير عَمَلٍ، كيف أجودُ برحمتي على مَنْ بخل بِطَاعَتِي؟». قوله : (في الإِخْلاصِ أي في اتصافي به ؛ وهو قصد الله بالعبادة وحده، وهو سبب للخلاص من أهوال يوم القيامة، وهو واجب عيني على كل مُكلَّف في جميع الطاعات. قال تعالى: ﴿وَمَا أُمِرُوا إِلَّا لِيَعْبُدُوا اللهَ مُخْلِصِينَ لَهُ الذِينَ) (البيئة: دا [؟]، وقال : إِنَّ اللهَ لا يقبلُ مِنَ العمل إلَّا ما كان خالصاً، وما ابتغي به وجهه (۱) ، وفي حديث أنس رضي الله تعالى عنه : قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «مَنْ فارق الدنيا على الإخلاص لله وحده لا شريك له، وإقام الصلاة، وإيتاء الزكاة.. فارقها والله عنه راض (۲) وعن ثوبان قال : سمعت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يقول : طُوبَى للمخلصين، أولئك مصابيح الهدى تنجلي عنهم كلُّ فتنة ظلماء (۳)، وفي رواية: (قتماء)، وهي بمعنى ظلماء. وممَّا يعين على الإخلاص استحضار أن ما سوى الله لا شيء بيده، وأن كل شيء بيد الله تعالى، والصادق في إخلاصه لا يحبُّ اطلاع الناس على حُسْنِ عمله، ولا يكره أن يَطَّلِعَ الناس على سيئ عمله ولا يبالي بخروج قَدْره من قلوب الخلق، ورؤي بعضهم في المنام بعد الموت يقول : (الجنَّة أرضُها الإيمان، وشجرها الأعمال، وثمرُها الإخلاص). قوله : (مِنَ الرِّيَاءِ) بالمد؛ أي: بدله فـ (من) للبدل على حد قوله تعالى : أَرَضِيتُم بِالْحَيَوةِ الدُّنْيَا مِنَ الْآخِرَةُ التّوبَة : ۱۳۸؛ أي: بَدَلَها، وليست للتعدية؛ لأنه لم يُعبر بالخلاص أو الخلوص، بل عبر بالإخلاص. والرياء أن يعمل القُرية ليراه الناس . وأما التَّسْمِيعُ : فهو أن يعمل العمل وحده، ثم يُخبر به الناس لأجل تعظيمهم له،
His statement: 'This' is the object of an omitted verb; i.e., understand this, or it is a subject with a missing predicate, and the estimation is: 'This which I have mentioned in this poem is the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunnah,' or something similar. And this is from the category of takhallus (transition); it is the transition from one purpose – and it is here the command to follow the righteous Salaf and avoid innovation from those who came after – to another purpose – and it is here hope for sincerity, and what is mentioned after it – and between the two purposes there is appropriateness. His statement: 'and from my side I hope in Allah for sincerity.' Al-raja' (hope) with prolongation: it is the attachment of the heart to something desired while taking the means; otherwise it is blameworthy greed. Ibn al-Jawzi said: The example of the one who hopes while persisting in disobedience is like one who hopes for a harvest without planting, or a child without marrying. And 'Abdullah ibn al-Mubarak said [in al-basit meter]: 'Why is it that you are content for your religion to be stained, while your garment is constantly washed from dirt? You hope for salvation but do not follow its path; the ship does not sail on dry land.' And in the hadith qudsi: 'How little shame has the one who hopes for my Garden without work! How can I bestow my mercy on one who is stingy with obedience to me?' His statement: 'in sincerity' means in my being characterized by it; and it is intending Allah alone in worship, and it is the cause of deliverance from the terrors of the Day of Resurrection, and it is an individual obligation on every legally responsible person in all acts of obedience. He said, exalted: 'And they were not commanded except to worship Allah, sincere to Him in religion' (al-Bayyinah: 5). And he said: 'Indeed Allah does not accept from action except what is pure and what is sought by His Face.' And in the hadith of Anas, may Allah be pleased with him: The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: 'Whoever leaves the world upon sincerity to Allah alone, with no partner, establishing prayer, and giving zakat – he leaves it while Allah is pleased with him.' And from Thawban: I heard the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, say: 'Good news to the sincere ones; they are lamps of guidance; every dark fitnah will be dispelled from them.' And in another narration: (قتماء) which means dark. Among the things that help sincerity is realizing that other than Allah has nothing in its hand, and that everything is in the hand of Allah, the Exalted. The truthful in his sincerity does not love that people see his good deed, nor does he dislike that people see his bad deed, nor does he care about the loss of his status from the hearts of creation. And one was seen in a dream after death saying: 'Paradise – its earth is faith, its trees are deeds, and its fruit is sincerity.' His statement: 'from ar-riya'' with prolongation; i.e., instead of it, so 'min' is for substitution, like His statement, exalted: 'Are you satisfied with the life of this world instead of the Hereafter?' (al-Tawbah: 38); i.e., instead of it, and it is not for transitivity, because he did not use 'khalaas' (deliverance) or 'khuluus' (purity), but rather 'ikhlaas' (sincerity). And ar-riya' (show-off) is that one does the good deed so that people see him. And at-tasmee' (hearing-seeking) is that he does the deed alone and then informs people of it for the sake of their glorification of him.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the line of poetry: 'هذا وأرجو الله في الإخلاص من الرياء'. The commentator first explains the word 'هذا' (this) as referring to the entire poetic composition being the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunnah. He notes that this is a rhetorical transition (takhallus) from the previous subject (following the Salaf and avoiding innovation) to the new subject (hope for sincerity and avoiding riya'), and there is an appropriate connection between them. Then he defines 'الرجاء' (hope) as the heart's attachment to something desired while taking the means; if one hopes without effort, it is blameworthy greed. He quotes Ibn al-Jawzi and 'Abdullah ibn al-Mubarak to illustrate that hope without action is futile. A hadith qudsi reinforces that hoping for Paradise without obedience is shameless. Next, he explains 'الإخلاص' (sincerity) as intending Allah alone in worship, which is an individual obligation in all acts of obedience. He supports this with Quranic verses and hadith, including a hadith that those who die with sincerity are pleasing to Allah, and a hadith that the sincere are lamps of guidance in darkness. He lists aids to sincerity: meditating that all things are in Allah's hand, not caring about people's opinions, and not seeking recognition. Finally, he explains 'مِنَ الرِّيَاءِ' (from riya') by parsing 'min' as substitutional, meaning 'instead of riya''. He defines riya' as doing good deeds to be seen by people, and tashmi' (hearing-seeking) as doing a deed alone then telling people about it for their glorification. The commentator distinguishes sincerity from mere deliverance or purity, emphasizing that it is a specific state of devotion.

Key Terms
رجاء
طمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that hope alone suffices for salvation without any effort or obedience.
Correction: The passage defines hope as requiring 'taking the means'; otherwise it is blameworthy greed. The examples and hadith confirm that genuine hope must be coupled with action.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of 'raja'' (hope)?
A) Desiring something without any effort
B) Attachment of the heart to something desired while taking the means
C) Simply expecting Allah's mercy
D) Giving up on Allah's mercy

Key Takeaway
Hope without action is blameworthy greed; true hope requires effort and obedience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the hadith qudsī 'How few are the shy of those who desire My Paradise without deeds,' does your hope for Paradise ever lack the effort that pleases Allah? What specific obedience have you been neglecting while still hoping for His mercy? This week, commit to one action that proves your hope is real.
?
Ibn al-Jawzī compares the hopeful sinner to someone expecting a harvest without planting. What harvest are you hoping for from Allah that you haven't 'planted' for through good deeds or repentance? Identify one area where your hope might actually be greed.
?
The poem asks why you accept your religion being defiled while your clothes are kept clean. What spiritual dirt have you become comfortable with? What would it mean for you to truly 'take the path' of salvation as the poem demands?

Sincerity (Ikhlas): Definition, Evidences, and Aids
Commentator defines sincerity and supports it with evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of sincerity (ikhlas), its obligations, and the practical aids to achieving it, as explained by the commentator.

Core Idea
Sincerity is to intend Allah alone in worship; it is an obligation and the key to deliverance.

The commentator now turns to sincerity (ikhlas), defining it as intending Allah alone in worship. He presents Quranic verses and hadith to show its importance and obligation, then lists practical means to cultivate it, such as realizing that all things are in Allah's hand and not caring about people's opinions.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of ikhlas
Read the Arabic and translation. Sincerity is defined as 'قصد الله بالعبادة وحده' – intending Allah alone in worship. Note that it is an individual obligation.
2
Study the proofs
Read the Quranic verses and hadith quoted. They establish that sincerity is a command and that Allah only accepts deeds that are sincere.
3
Identify the aids to sincerity
The commentator lists things that help sincerity: realizing Allah's control, not seeking people's approval, etc. Reflect on how these can be applied.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The passage teaches that the sincere do not care if their status falls in people's hearts. Is there a person whose good opinion of you that makes you afraid to lose? Will you ask Allah tonight to free you from that fear?
?
Imagine standing before Allah on the Day of Resurrection, and He asks you: 'Did you truly worship Me alone, or did you seek the praise of others?' What will you answer? What one concrete change can you make today to ensure your answer is 'only You'?

Riya' and Tasmee': Show-Off and Hearing-Seeking
Commentator defines riya' and tasmee' as opposites of sincerity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of riya' (show-off) and tasmee' (seeking to be heard) as distinct forms of insincerity, and how they contrast with genuine sincerity.

Core Idea
Riya' is doing good deeds to be seen; tasmee' is telling others about them for praise.

The commentator concludes by explaining the phrase 'from riya'' (مِنَ الرِّيَاءِ). He clarifies that the preposition 'min' here indicates substitution, meaning 'instead of riya''. Then he defines riya' as doing a good deed to be seen by people, and tasmee' as doing a deed alone and then informing others to gain their glorification. These are both threats to sincerity.

Guided Path
1
Read the grammatical explanation
Read the passage where the commentator explains that 'min' is for substitution (بدل). Understand the difference between 'ikhlas' and 'khulas' or 'khalaas'.
2
Define riya' and tasmee'
Find the definitions of riya' and tasmee' in the text. Note the subtle difference: riya' is for being seen, tasmee' is for being heard about.
3
Relate back to sincerity
Reflect on how both riya' and tasmee' are opposite to ikhlas. True sincerity is for Allah alone, not for any human attention.

قوله : (هذا) مفعول المحذوف؛ أي: افهم هذا، أو مبتدأ والخبر محذوف، والتقدير هذا الذي ذكرته في هذه المنظومة مذهب أهل السنة، أو نحو ذلك. وهذا من باب التخلص ؛ وهو الانتقال من غَرَض - وهو هنا الأمر بمتابعة السلف الصالح، ومجانبة البدعة ممن خلف - إلى غرض آخر - وهو هنا رجاء الإخلاص، وما ذكر بعده - وبين الغرضين تناسب قوله : (هَذَا وَأَرْجُو الله الرجاء بالمد : هو تعلُّق القلب بمرغوب فيه مع الأخذ في الأسباب، وإلا فهو طمع مذموم. قال ابن الجوزي: مثل الراجي مع الإصرار على المعصية، كمثل من رجا حصاداً وما زرع أو ولداً وما نكح ، وقال عبد الله بن المبارك: [من البسيط] مَا بَالُ دِينِكَ تَرْضَى أَنْ تُدَنْسَهُ وثَوْبُكَ الدَّهْرَ مَنْسُولٌ مِنَ الدَّنَسِ؟ تَرْجُو النَّجَاةَ وَلَمْ تَسْلُكْ طَرِيْقَتَها إِنَّ السَّفِينَةَ لَا تَجْرِي عَلَى البَبَسِ. وفي الحديث القدسي : مَا أَقلَّ حياءَ مَنْ يَطْمَعُ في جَنَّتي بغير عَمَلٍ، كيف أجودُ برحمتي على مَنْ بخل بِطَاعَتِي؟». قوله : (في الإِخْلاصِ أي في اتصافي به ؛ وهو قصد الله بالعبادة وحده، وهو سبب للخلاص من أهوال يوم القيامة، وهو واجب عيني على كل مُكلَّف في جميع الطاعات. قال تعالى: ﴿وَمَا أُمِرُوا إِلَّا لِيَعْبُدُوا اللهَ مُخْلِصِينَ لَهُ الذِينَ) (البيئة: دا [؟]، وقال : إِنَّ اللهَ لا يقبلُ مِنَ العمل إلَّا ما كان خالصاً، وما ابتغي به وجهه (۱) ، وفي حديث أنس رضي الله تعالى عنه : قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «مَنْ فارق الدنيا على الإخلاص لله وحده لا شريك له، وإقام الصلاة، وإيتاء الزكاة.. فارقها والله عنه راض (۲) وعن ثوبان قال : سمعت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يقول : طُوبَى للمخلصين، أولئك مصابيح الهدى تنجلي عنهم كلُّ فتنة ظلماء (۳)، وفي رواية: (قتماء)، وهي بمعنى ظلماء. وممَّا يعين على الإخلاص استحضار أن ما سوى الله لا شيء بيده، وأن كل شيء بيد الله تعالى، والصادق في إخلاصه لا يحبُّ اطلاع الناس على حُسْنِ عمله، ولا يكره أن يَطَّلِعَ الناس على سيئ عمله ولا يبالي بخروج قَدْره من قلوب الخلق، ورؤي بعضهم في المنام بعد الموت يقول : (الجنَّة أرضُها الإيمان، وشجرها الأعمال، وثمرُها الإخلاص). قوله : (مِنَ الرِّيَاءِ) بالمد؛ أي: بدله فـ (من) للبدل على حد قوله تعالى : أَرَضِيتُم بِالْحَيَوةِ الدُّنْيَا مِنَ الْآخِرَةُ التّوبَة : ۱۳۸؛ أي: بَدَلَها، وليست للتعدية؛ لأنه لم يُعبر بالخلاص أو الخلوص، بل عبر بالإخلاص. والرياء أن يعمل القُرية ليراه الناس . وأما التَّسْمِيعُ : فهو أن يعمل العمل وحده، ثم يُخبر به الناس لأجل تعظيمهم له،
His statement: 'This' is the object of an omitted verb; i.e., understand this, or it is a subject with a missing predicate, and the estimation is: 'This which I have mentioned in this poem is the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunnah,' or something similar. And this is from the category of takhallus (transition); it is the transition from one purpose – and it is here the command to follow the righteous Salaf and avoid innovation from those who came after – to another purpose – and it is here hope for sincerity, and what is mentioned after it – and between the two purposes there is appropriateness. His statement: 'and from my side I hope in Allah for sincerity.' Al-raja' (hope) with prolongation: it is the attachment of the heart to something desired while taking the means; otherwise it is blameworthy greed. Ibn al-Jawzi said: The example of the one who hopes while persisting in disobedience is like one who hopes for a harvest without planting, or a child without marrying. And 'Abdullah ibn al-Mubarak said [in al-basit meter]: 'Why is it that you are content for your religion to be stained, while your garment is constantly washed from dirt? You hope for salvation but do not follow its path; the ship does not sail on dry land.' And in the hadith qudsi: 'How little shame has the one who hopes for my Garden without work! How can I bestow my mercy on one who is stingy with obedience to me?' His statement: 'in sincerity' means in my being characterized by it; and it is intending Allah alone in worship, and it is the cause of deliverance from the terrors of the Day of Resurrection, and it is an individual obligation on every legally responsible person in all acts of obedience. He said, exalted: 'And they were not commanded except to worship Allah, sincere to Him in religion' (al-Bayyinah: 5). And he said: 'Indeed Allah does not accept from action except what is pure and what is sought by His Face.' And in the hadith of Anas, may Allah be pleased with him: The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: 'Whoever leaves the world upon sincerity to Allah alone, with no partner, establishing prayer, and giving zakat – he leaves it while Allah is pleased with him.' And from Thawban: I heard the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, say: 'Good news to the sincere ones; they are lamps of guidance; every dark fitnah will be dispelled from them.' And in another narration: (قتماء) which means dark. Among the things that help sincerity is realizing that other than Allah has nothing in its hand, and that everything is in the hand of Allah, the Exalted. The truthful in his sincerity does not love that people see his good deed, nor does he dislike that people see his bad deed, nor does he care about the loss of his status from the hearts of creation. And one was seen in a dream after death saying: 'Paradise – its earth is faith, its trees are deeds, and its fruit is sincerity.' His statement: 'from ar-riya'' with prolongation; i.e., instead of it, so 'min' is for substitution, like His statement, exalted: 'Are you satisfied with the life of this world instead of the Hereafter?' (al-Tawbah: 38); i.e., instead of it, and it is not for transitivity, because he did not use 'khalaas' (deliverance) or 'khuluus' (purity), but rather 'ikhlaas' (sincerity). And ar-riya' (show-off) is that one does the good deed so that people see him. And at-tasmee' (hearing-seeking) is that he does the deed alone and then informs people of it for the sake of their glorification of him.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the line of poetry: 'هذا وأرجو الله في الإخلاص من الرياء'. The commentator first explains the word 'هذا' (this) as referring to the entire poetic composition being the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunnah. He notes that this is a rhetorical transition (takhallus) from the previous subject (following the Salaf and avoiding innovation) to the new subject (hope for sincerity and avoiding riya'), and there is an appropriate connection between them. Then he defines 'الرجاء' (hope) as the heart's attachment to something desired while taking the means; if one hopes without effort, it is blameworthy greed. He quotes Ibn al-Jawzi and 'Abdullah ibn al-Mubarak to illustrate that hope without action is futile. A hadith qudsi reinforces that hoping for Paradise without obedience is shameless. Next, he explains 'الإخلاص' (sincerity) as intending Allah alone in worship, which is an individual obligation in all acts of obedience. He supports this with Quranic verses and hadith, including a hadith that those who die with sincerity are pleasing to Allah, and a hadith that the sincere are lamps of guidance in darkness. He lists aids to sincerity: meditating that all things are in Allah's hand, not caring about people's opinions, and not seeking recognition. Finally, he explains 'مِنَ الرِّيَاءِ' (from riya') by parsing 'min' as substitutional, meaning 'instead of riya''. He defines riya' as doing good deeds to be seen by people, and tashmi' (hearing-seeking) as doing a deed alone then telling people about it for their glorification. The commentator distinguishes sincerity from mere deliverance or purity, emphasizing that it is a specific state of devotion.

Key Terms
رياء
تسميع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that only riya' (showing off) is a problem, while tasmee' (telling others) is fine.
Correction: The passage explicitly defines tasmee' as a separate blameworthy act, distinct from riya', where one seeks praise through reporting good deeds.

Checkpoint

A) Performing a good deed loudly so people can hear
B) Doing a good deed alone and then telling people about it for their glorification
C) Asking others to pray for you
D) Giving charity in secret

Key Takeaway
Riya' and tasmee' are two forms of insincerity that undermine worship; sincerity requires doing deeds solely for Allah, not for human observation or praise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The commentator emphasizes that the poet chose 'ikhlas' over 'khalaas' or 'khuluus'. Does your understanding of sincerity go beyond just 'getting rid of' showing off? Are you willing to examine every deed you do and ask: 'Am I doing this for Allah alone?' If not, what will you change starting now?

Ikhlas: Sincerity in Worship and Its Fruits
The commentator defines ikhlas with proofs and aids.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of ikhlas (sincerity) in worship, its status as an individual obligation, and how it is a cause of salvation.

Core Idea
Ikhlas is the single intention of God in every act of worship, and it is the fruit of all good deeds.

This lesson begins by explaining the foundational concept of ikhlas. The commentator defines it as intending God alone in worship, supporting it with sacred texts and wise sayings. We will see why ikhlas is essential for every Muslim and how it leads to salvation.

Guided Path
1
Read the sacred hadith and definition
Begin by reading the sacred hadith that highlights the importance of action, then the commentator's definition of ikhlas.
Notice the sequence: hadith leads into definition.
2
Note the proofs: Quranic verse and hadiths
Identify the Quranic verse (al-Bayyinah 5) and the two hadiths from Anas and Thawban that establish ikhlas as obligatory and rewarding.
3
Understand the aids to sincerity
Read the aids to sincerity: realizing everything is in God's hand and not caring about people's opinions.
The truly sincere person does not seek praise.
4
Reflect on the dream analogy
The dream likens faith to earth, deeds to trees, and ikhlas to fruit. Consider how this illustrates the central role of sincerity.
Ikhlas is the final, most essential element.

قَدْره من قلوب الخلق، ورؤي بعضهم في المنام بعد الموت يقول : (الجنَّة أرضُها الإيمان، وشجرها الأعمال، وثمرُها الإخلاص). قوله : (مِنَ الرِّيَاءِ) بالمد؛ أي: بدله فـ (من) للبدل على حد قوله تعالى : أَرَضِيتُم بِالْحَيَوةِ الدُّنْيَا مِنَ الْآخِرَةُ [التوبة : ۱۳۸]؛ أي: بَدَلَها، وليست للتعدية؛ لأنه لم يُعبر بالخلاص أو الخلوص، بل عبر بالإخلاص. والرياء أن يعمل القُرية ليراه الناس . وأما اللهِ، وإلا فهو رياء. وفي الحديث القدسي: أنا أغنى الشركاء عن الشركِ ؛ فمَنْ عمل عملاً أشرك فيه ،غيري، تركْتُهُ لشريكي (١) ، وقال تعالى: ﴿فَوَيْلٌ لِلْمُصَلِّينَ الَّذِينَ هُمْ عَن صَلَاتِهِمْ سَاهُونَ His saying: '(concerning sincerity) means concerning my being described with it; and it is intending God alone in worship, and it is a cause of salvation from the terrors of the Day of Resurrection, and it is an individual obligation on every morally responsible person in all acts of obedience. He said, the Most High: {And they were not commanded except to worship God, sincere to Him in religion} [al-Bayyinah: 5], and He said: 'Indeed God does not accept from action except that which is pure and intended for His Face.' In the hadith of Anas, may God be pleased with him: The Messenger of God, peace be upon him, said: 'Whoever leaves this world upon sincerity to God alone, with no partner, and establishing prayer, and giving zakat... leaves it while God is pleased with him.'
